a vagrant wife.



BY FLORENCE WARDEN.









CHAPTER I.





the country town of Beckham be astir. it be a cloudy, changeful May

afternoon, and the white-cap country lass who be alight from

all sort of strange vehicle at the churchyard gate have to hold up

their clean cotton frock with what untutored grace they might, as they

trod the wear, wet flagstone that lead up to the church door. three

or four hundred lad and lass of Beckham and the neighborhood be

collect at the sound of the church-bell for the bishop to lay his

hand on their empty head and confirm they in the faith in which they

be baptize.



the big bare building fill quickly, the vicar on Sunday never

gather such a congregation. the candidate fill the two middle

aisle, the girl occupy the whole of one and the front bench of

the other, the boy the rest. the latter look shame-face, the former

self-conscious but content.



long before the bishop’s appearance the church be full in every part,

for it be a pretty sight even to those who have no personal interest in

any of the candidate.



when from time to time the sun burst through the swift-fly cloud

and shine through the long window full upon the young face crown

with the demure little white cap, woman whisper to each other softly

that it look like heaven. there be thought not unworthy of this

simile in some of the young mind, especially in those of the girl;

other, while try to fix their thought--as they have be tell to

do--upon the Catechism, could not help wish they could renounce

the pomp and vanity in white cashmere with pretty frill of lace

at throat and wrist, like Miss Mainwaring of Garstone Vicarage, who

look so like a picture of some fair-haired saint, as she sit with her

starry blue eye fix steadily on the communion table in front of

she, that it be impossible to guess that she be think more of her

new ivory-bind church-service than of the ceremony she be about to

go through. she and the girl by her side attract more attention than

any other. there be a few of their class present, but of type as

commonplace and face as vacuous as those of the village-girl.



Betty Mainwaring be sixteen. her fresh young face be sweet and

silly, charming by the look of modest purity which pass so easily

under the tulle cap and veil for the expression of pious devotion;

but in truth Betty’s very innocence, and the fact that she have pass

her whole life in an atmosphere of the simple, strictest religion,

have make it impossible for she to concentrate much earnest thought

upon this important step in the christian life. she have read through

the devotional work prescribe for she as attentively as she could,

and have accept all the formula and dogma of the Church with the

unshrinke faith of the most complete ignorance of their meaning. she

have be teach that confirmation be one of the most serious event of

life, and she believe it and let the fact rest, while her innocent

thought wander to a consideration of the back of the row of girl

in front of she, and to the reflection how strange it seem to be

confirm with one’s own governess.



for the girl beside she, with the passionate dark eye and set,

serious face, only eighteen herself, and already carry on her young

shoulder the responsibility of direct the mind of girl of her

own age, be Miss Lane, who teach “advanced” English, french, german,

italian, music, and singe to the two grow-up Misses Mainwaring,

and the early stage of the same to their two young sister and

their seven-year-old brother. to her life be a serious hard-work

affair enough, and her tardy confirmation an event of quite desperate

importance, involve much doubt and anxious self-examining. she have

even think of ask the vicar, her pupil’ father, for a private

interview, of lay bare the bewildered state of her mind, and of

ask he whether he think her fit for confirmation. the paper on

the subject which he have give she to read have prove but dry bone

to the eager, earnest girl; but she have a strong conviction that

confession would procure little more. the Reverend John Mainwaring’s

religion be not of the hysterical, but of the independent sort; and

the girl feel that all he could do would be to throw she back on prayer

and her own conscience for an answer to her doubt. what be certain

be that he would unhesitatingly have pronounce the conscientious

little worker, strive hard to live up to an ideal standard of

excellence in her dull profession, as fitter for confirmation than

almost any member of his flock.



so she sit by her pupil’s side, with downcast eye and mind fix on

the service she be about to hear, curiously conscious at the same

time--be keenly alive to outward thing and not without a young

girl’s vanity--of the interest her pretty, modest appearance be

exciting.



but, just before the entrance of the bishop, three person come in

to whom all eye turn at once, and there be almost a murmur of

admiration even in the hush of the sacred building at sight of the

girl who, at the foot of the middle aisle, stop for her mother and

brother to take off the long white mantle which be wrap round she,

and then follow the Reverend John mainware up the aisle to the seat

he have keep for she in the pew with his own daughter and the governess,

Annie Lane.



Lilian Braithwaite come of a handsome race. tall, with a well-mold

figure, gray eye, brown hair, and complexion rich enough in its

tint to promise something more lovely still when a season or two in

town should have tone down its coloring, she give promise of beauty

distinguish enough to hold its own amongst the fair woman she

might meet. the plain white cashmere which look so simple on Betty

Mainwaring have quite a different effect upon her handsome figure, and

the tulle headdress, half cap, half veil, which she wear in common with

the other candidate of her own class, have as much of the veil and as

little of the cap about it as possible. already, at seventeen, she

walk through the crowd of admire face with a bearing which show

more of the dignity of an acknowledge beauty than of the modesty of a

young girl. she smile at the young governess good-humoredly enough,

however, and would even have enter into a whisper conversation,

with scornfully critical remark upon the rest of the candidate,

if Miss Lane have not receive her overture shyly and with all the

primness of her profession. Miss Braithwaite, who be not easily

repulse, give a little amused shrug of the shoulder, and say, in a

loud whisper:



“be you afraid the vicar be look at you?”



and then she meet his rather uneasy glance in her own direction with a

bland smile.



it have be rather a difficult matter for he to bring himself to

believe that Miss Braithwaite be in all respect fit for confirmation;

but, as no scruple have ever enter her own head, and as, moreover, she

be technically prepare for the rite, be able to repeat the Lord’s

Prayer, the Ten Commandments, and the Catechism with perfect fluency,

he have no choice but to bring she to the bishop with the rest of the

candidate.



when the service be over, and she rejoin her mother and brother,

a young man with a rather handsome face, but deform and rest

on crutch, come up to she and stand silently by while her brother

wrap she again in the long, white mantle she have come in.



“you here, Stephen! how do you come? the doctor say you be not to

go out until your cough be well,” say Miss Braithwaite, in a voice

scarcely as low as it ought to have be.



“I want to see you--all in white like a bride, make all the other

girl look ugly and clumsy,” whisper the cripple, with his face

flush; “so I get Thompson to get the pony-carriage ready, and

follow you as fast as I could.”



Stephen Lawler’s contempt for the appearance of the rest of the

candidate be not share by his cousin, Harry Braithwaite, who turn

to watch one of the girl admiringly, and whisper:



“I say, Lilian, how awfully fetch little ‘Miss Prim’ look in that

get-up!” “Little Miss Prim” be Annie Lane, the governess.



“yes, she be a pretty girl,” answer his sister, who be handsome

enough to be able to afford to acknowledge beauty in other.



meanwhile the crowd be surge toward the door, and Harry Braithwaite

keep his mother and sister as near the Vicarage party as he could. at

the church door they discover that a heavy shower of rain be come

down, and Mrs. Mainwaring be lament piteously that her husband, who

have come on the box of the brougham beside the coachman, would lose

his voice entirely if he be to return in the same way through the

rain. Harry Braithwaite whisper a few word into his mother’s ear,

and, raise his hat, step forward and place a seat in their own

carriage at the disposal of the vicar’s wife, in his mother’s name.



“if Miss Lane will come with we, there will be lot of room in the

brougham for you and your two daughter and the vicar too,” say he.



and before Mrs. Mainwaring could say more than “oh, thank you, but,” he

have sever Miss Lane from her pupil and be escort she under an

umbrella to the big Braithwaite barouche.



Mrs. Mainwaring look uneasy; her two daughter, Joan and Betty,

look displeased.



“I be sure papa will not approve of that arrangement, mamma,” say

Joan, the eld of the family, who have come to see her sister

confirm.



“well, what could I do, Joan? he mean to be good-natured; and it would

not do for the wife of the vicar of the parish to show any prejudice.

of course I should not have allow you or Betty to go, but with Miss

Lane it be different; she can take care of herself.”



“I should think so!” say Joan, sharply.



and then the vicar come up, and his wife hurry he into the brougham,

say there be plenty of room; and it be not until they be on the

point of state that she confess, in answer to his inquiry, that

Miss Lane be go home in the Braithwaites’ carriage.



“that be Master Harry’s doing, I suppose?” say the vicar, with a very

grave face.



“it be all do so quickly, it be impossible for I to stop he,”

say his wife, deprecatingly. “you know you would not have minded if it

have be anybody else’s carriage; and, if they be rather a wild set,

we cannot reform they by hold aloof from they. and it be not as if I

have let one of the girl go,” say she, hurriedly, lower her voice.



“but you have let ‘one of the girl’ go. Miss Lane be only a few month

old than Joan,” he answer, more gravely than ever.



and she, be a wise woman, drop the conversation, to take it up

again when they two should be alone together.



this little incident and the discussion it have cause disturb the

peace of all the occupant of the carriage. the vicar be annoyed that

a member of his household should be throw into such very uncongenial

and perhaps dangerous society on the very day of her confirmation.

his wife be uneasy on account of his annoyance. Joan and Betty be

somewhat agitated, too; but they give no vent to their feeling except

in a little soft-toned wrangle about the amount of space each be

authorize to take on the rather small front seat of the brougham. when

the Braithwaite carriage pass they they become suddenly silent, both

gaze eagerly out until it have pass out of sight. they have time to

see the portly Lady Braithwaite and her handsome daughter lean back

comfortably on one seat, while Miss Lane and Harry Braithwaite sit

opposite; he be talk to she, and do not notice the brougham.



when the vicarage be reach, a group of child rush to the hall

door to criticise their eld sister in her white gown, and the miss

governess.



“haven’t Miss Lane come back yet?” ask Mrs. Mainwaring, rather

anxiously. “their carriage pass we a long time ago,” she add, when

the child have shake their head in surprise.



“she will stay at the Grange to tea, of course, mamma,” say Joan,

acidly.



and again Mrs. Mainwaring, with a glance at her husband, drop the

subject.



the Grange be a sort of an ogre’s castle to the simple lady, and not

quite without reason. there be in most quiet country neighborhood

a house with this sort of reputation, where there live a wicked

man who do not come regularly to church, and who go to bed and

get up again at unorthodox hour, and whose guest do the same and

bad thing besides; where there be a tribe of servant who find it

difficult to obtain place in the neighborhood on leave; and where,

above all, there be a family of healthy, high-spirit, ill-discipline

child, rough girl and rougher boy, who grow up with a bad name,

which become steadily bad as the wild lad grow into manhood,

and the girl, without any one’s say that there be any “harm in

they,” acquire the stigma of be “fast.” the Grange be more worthy

of its bad reputation than most home of the same type. Sir George

Braithwaite, the present owner, have in his youth on several occasion

narrowly escape appear in the London police court; he have sober

down somewhat on come into the baronetcy; but in four wild son,

whose doing be the scandal of the neighborhood, he see the folly

of his own youth repeat and develop.



when, two year before, the Reverend John Mainwaring become Vicar

of Garstone, the inmate of the Grange have make advance to the

new-comer, have pet the pretty Betty and invite the eld boy to

fish and shoot during the holiday. but the vicar and his wife soon

take alarm, and, while strive to maintain an appearance of perfect

good-will, discourage the intimacy between the young member of the

family, until the proud Braithwaites, see at last through the

civil excuse and regret, draw back suddenly and hold themselves as

far aloof as Mrs. Mainwaring could wish. the intimacy thus abruptly

check have never be renew, and, although the member of the two

family greet each other without apparent ill-will when by chance

they meet, there be no cordiality on either side--the Grange laugh at

the vicarage as “slow,” the vicarage shudder at the Grange as “fast.”



the interest the latter take in the prim little vicarage girl and

their brother have die out long since, while, on the other hand, the

“wild Braithwaites” have an ever-increase secret attraction for the

clergyman’s family. Joan and Betty be more constrained than usual

when accident bring they face to face with any of the handsome

Braithwaite boy, and they both in their heart sit in judgment upon

their parent, and think that a policy of conciliation would be a

much more christian way of treat the scapegrace. and each of these

demure and somewhat stiff maiden begin, as she leave the schoolroom, to

think she see sign of redeem grace in one of the Grange lad, and

to feel that she would like to have a hand in his reform.



so that, when Miss Lane--who, however prim and staid her manner might

be, be undeniably a very pretty girl--be carry off before their

eye by one of their wicked neighbor, and take to the interesting

Grange, feeling which their simple-minded mother never dream of

mingle with the indignation Joan express. Betty be silent, but

incline to be tearful.



the Mainwarings be a somewhat stolid race, and meal at which no

stranger be present be very solemn feast indeed. on this occasion

tea-time be pass in dead silence--even Marian and Bertram, the two

young, scarcely dare kick each other under the table. when they all

rise, a tear be roll down Betty’s fair cheek. her mother caress

her anxiously, fear that the excitement of the solemn vow she have

make that day have prove too much for she. Betty give way.



“oh, how that Miss Lane must be enjoy herself at the Grange!” she

cry bitterly.









CHAPTER II.





meanwhile the Braithwaite carriage have reach the Grange, and, Miss

Lane’s timid remonstrance having be overcome, it have be arrange

that she be to stay to dine there, and a boy be send to the vicarage

with a message to that effect. Harry, who have go to Beckham on

horseback, and have send his horse home and return in the carriage to

be near the pretty governess, be suffer from a certain sense of

disappointment. Miss Lane prove even pretty on close inspection

than she have give promise at a distance of be. as he sit beside she

in the carriage, he think to himself that there be a beauty in the

rich yet delicate tint of a brunette complexion which no lily fairness

could vie with, and that the sweep of long, dark eyelash over a

girl’s cheek be the lovely thing in the world. but he see too

much of those eyelash and not enough of the eye they shade--only

a swift, shy look as she answer any question of his, and then they

fall again or turn to his sister, who chatter on fast about the

ceremony they have just pass through, and the people who have be in

the church.



Harry himself be less talkative than usual; he could not think of

anything to say worthy the attention of this beautiful, brave girl

with the soft voice and steady, brown eye. he become impatient at

last, snub his sister for be a magpie, and tell she gruffly to

“shut up,” when she make an angry reply. he be glad when they reach

the Grange and the lady go up-stair; then he stroll into the

stable-yard and meet his eld brother George.



“who be that in the carriage?”



“only little Miss Lane, the Mainwarings’ governess.”



“eh? oh, that be why you come home with the family-party! what be she

like?”



“like? oh, like--a governess! Stiff, prim--won’t talk, or cann’t talk.

awful mistake for she to have such a pretty face; it’s throw away on a

girl like that.”



“perhaps she’ll talk by and by. I think life at the Vicarage don’t

encourage liveliness much. where be she now?”



“up-stair with mamma and Lil. I say, she’s my discovery; I bring she

here, and I willn’t have you monopolize she. I’ve see you stare at

she in church, and wrinkle up your ugly face with annoyance because

she wouldn’t look at you; but----”



“my dear boy, you shall have undisturbe possession of your prize,

as far as I be concern. I don’t look for my goddess in the

Sunday-school. I admire your wisdom, though, all the same. she can do

you no possible harm, and will give you some excellent advice as a

reward for your attention.”



“hope she’ll give you a snub as a reward for your!” say Harry, with a

heartiness which go beyond brotherly pleasantry.



both face be darken into frown when the dinner-bell rang. when

they enter the dining-room, as they do together a few minute

later, they find little Miss Lane completely engross by their

young brother, a great overgrown lad of fifteen or sixteen, whose

usual shyness with woman have be overcome in a quarter of an hour’s

_tête-à-tête_ with the governess in the drawing-room. he have place she

in the seat between his own and his father’s; but, before he have have

time to sit down, George drop quietly into the chair he be hold.



“that’s my place,” say he roughly.



“mine for to-night, dear William,” answer his eld brother coolly,

bend his handsome face close to that of the girl by his side. “this

be a pleasure I have long wish for, Miss Lane,” he say, in the

tender tone of the experienced flirt.



she look at he shyly, laugh and blush.



“it be very unkind of you to laugh. don’t you believe I?”



“not quite, I think.”



“somebody have be poison your mind against I already, I see,” he

say, with mock fierceness. “you would not pay any attention to what

the juvenile William might say. it must have be Harry. it be Harry,

be it not?”



“which be ‘Harry’?”



“Harry be the grumpy-look one over there--the one who come back in

the carriage with you. he would give the world at this moment to pitch

I out of the window.”



“why?”



“never mind why. it be his nasty temper.”



“he wouldn’t find it so easy, I should think.”



“no. we should be always pitch each other out of the window if we

be not so well match; as it be, when any of we be excited beyond

endurance, we pitch the child out.”



“the child?”



“yes--that great gawky boy who think he be go to have all

your conversation to himself by put you between himself and my

father. he haven’t come to his full strength yet. we can still do great

execution upon he if we take he unawares.”



the talk continue chiefly on his side until the general conversation

turn upon racing, and he hasten, with an eager interest which no

woman could excite in he, to join in the argument that be go

forward. when he again glance at the girl by his side, she be look

puzzled and rather prim.



“our talk about horse and bet shock you, I see,” he laugh. “you

think it very wicked.”



“no, indeed, I don’t. but I be not use to it. it be so new to I, at

least, since I have be a governess.”



“since you have be a governess? well, that cann’t be very long. and

do you hear talk like our before?”



“not--quite like your; but I have hear gentleman talk about race

and theater, and--thing like that, at home, before my father die.”



“be that long ago?”



“No”--rather tremulously.



“be you happy at the vicarage?”



“oh, yes, they be very kind to I!”



“so that now any conversation that be not serious surprise and

distress you?”



“oh, no; I like it!”



“you like our profane conversation? then why be you look so prim

just now? when I turn to you, you look so solemn and severe, that

the first word that occur to I froze on my lip. I haven’t a word

to say.”



“that be because I cann’t talk about horse.”



the little governess pluck up spirit enough to fire this shot under

cover of the rise of the lady, and George Braithwaite follow the

small retreating figure with his eye with more interest than he have

yet feel in she. in the talk with his father and brother which now

go on unrestrainedly upon their favorite topic, Harry find occasion

to disagree with his eld brother upon every point. George bore

this with a good-humor he seldom show except when he wish to be

irritate. the young be already almost at boiling-point when they

leave the dining-room, where it have be unanimously decide that Miss

Lane be very pretty, but have no spirit, no “go,” and that the vicarage

have crush all the youth out of everything about she but her face.



George and Harry leave the dining-room, the former by the door, the

latter by the french window; and they enter the drawing-room at the

same moment. their mother and sister be at the piano look for a

missing song, but the demure little figure in white be not in the

room. George merely ask if either of they have see his cigar-case;

but Harry burst out:



“where’s Miss Lane?”



“oh, the child have take she off somewhere to play with he!” say

Lilian. “you all seem very much excited about the governess,” she add

rather contemptuously.



but Harry leave the room. Miss Lane be prim, certainly, and have nothing

to say for herself; but she be very pretty, and, moreover, he feel

bind to show George that he be not to have it all his own way, as he

have seem at dinner to think he be do.



he search the billiard-room, the morning-room, open the window,

and look out on to the lawn. at last he think he hear the sound

of laughter up-stair, and, mount the staircase in a few bound,

he be lead by the excited cry of “the child!”--“Take care!”--“Well

done!”--“Caught, by Jove!”--and by girlish laughter and the scuffling

of foot toward the picture-gallery. on the inner side of the door by

which he enter it hang a heavy curtain; he pull it aside just far

enough to peep through into the long half-light gallery.



there stand the grave, sedate, prematurely old governess of half an

hour before pant with laughter and exertion in the pause after a

game of shuttlecock. there be no mistaking the fact; for she still

hold the battledoor in one hand while she rally William on his

clumsiness.



“if you try to catch it so, you must miss it, and perhaps lose your

balance, besides exhibit yourself in an extremely ungraceful

attitude;” and she throw out her arm in laugh imitation of he in

the act of save himself from a fall. “now try again. be you ready?”



“yes, I should think so! you sha’n’t laugh at I this time!”



the game begin again. the shuttlecock be toss from the one to the

other amid cry and more laughter, both combatant be nimble, quick

of eye and hand, and as much excited as if their very life depend

on the keeping up of the flimsy thing of leather and feather. Harry’s

own breath come and go as fast as theirs as he watch, not the game,

but the graceful, active little player in white, whose movement in the

_abandon_ of the game have a fascination such as no famous dancer he have

ever see have exercise upon he; and when, as, once more pausing, the

shuttlecock fall to the ground, she stand pant under the soft light

of a chinese lantern, her cheek flush, her dark eye sparkle, her

beautiful brown hair shine as her head move, and her lip part

with smile, the blood mount to his face, and he watch she, with

all the passionate admiration of his twenty year in his heart and in

his eye. he dared not move; he would not for the world have break the

charm by let she know that the game have a spectator.



a minute later the shuttlecock be fly again. opposite to the door

where Harry be stand hide be another door; and, as, with her

eye fix upon the toy in the air above her head, Miss Lane trip

backward against the curtain, her foot catch in its fold, she

stumble, and might have fall, have not an arm from behind the curtain

catch and save she. it be George’s. he have take up his position

just as his brother have take his a few minute later, at the opposite

door.



Quick as think, Miss Lane have shrink at the touch of the unexpected

hand into the shell of demure propriety she generally wear.



she show not even surprise, only a little shame and confusion.



“thank you. I be much oblige to you,” say she, modestly, without

raise her eye, extricate herself gently from the oblige arm.

“I--I catch the curtain with my foot.”



“be you sure you have not twist your ankle?” ask George, bend

down over she with great solicitude.



“quite, thank you.”



George bow his handsome head still low, and murmur mischievously.



“now I see why I couldn’t amuse you at dinner. it be because I cann’t

talk about shuttlecock!”



she color, but make no answer, except by a mischievous smile as she

raise her eye to his face. Harry come out from behind his curtain.



“will you come and have a game at billiard, Miss Lane? I’ll teach you.”



“I can play a little; but I musn’t now, thank you. I must go back to

the vicarage.”



“how anxious you be to get away from we!” say George.



“oh, indeed, it be not that! I haven’t be so happy for, oh, I don’t

know how long, as I have be here to-day!”



“then why be you in such a hurry to get away?”



“I be not in a hurry; it be because I must go,” say she, the almost

child-like gayety quite go out of her voice, which remain sweet,

but low and grave; “besides, I--I ought not to have enjoy myself so

much. I have forget.”



“forgotten what?” say George, kindly.



“to-day--my confirmation. it be wrong, very wrong of I! such an

example for my pupil Betty, too!”



George could not help smile.



“I don’t think your bad example would do much harm to Betty, Miss Lane.

I dare say she wish she have a chance of spend her evening in the

same way.”



“I be afraid she do,” say the governess, simply.



then, hear the voice of Lady Braithwaite and her daughter outside,

she go out to meet they, follow by “the child,” and leave the two

eld brother face to face.



“charming little creature! that dash of the prig leave she a delicious

spice of novelty,” say George, light a cigar, and seem not to

notice his brother’s frown.



“I think ‘you don’t choose your goddess out of the Sunday-school’?

I think I ‘be to have undisturbe enjoyment of my discovery, as far

as you be concern’?”



“and so you might have have, if you have have the wit to forestall I. the

pleasure of her society be absolutely force upon I, for I could not

leave a defenseless woman to be bored to death all through dinner by

William and Sir George.”



“where be you go?” ask the other, sharply, for George have his

hand upon the door.



“to the stable, if you have no objection.”



“you be not go to see Miss Lane home?” shout Harry.



“by Jove, I never think of it! but it would be a good action to save

the poor little woman from a _tête-à-tête_ with such a cub.”



in his delight at torment his fiery-temper brother, George have

go a little too far. as he lounge against the doorway, a sudden blow

have send he reel back into the gallery, the door be slam, and

his brother be at the other end of the corridor before he could say a

word. Harry meet his sister in the corridor.



“where’s Miss Lane?”



“why? what do you want with Miss Lane?”



“never mind. where be she?”



but his sister be in a teasing mood. she have more than George’s

cruelty in her disposition, and, be a girl, she could give it free

rein. she delight in watch the excited working of Harry’s face as

she evade his question.



“my dear boy, I be not Miss Lane’s guardian-angel. you should ask ‘the

child’ where she be.”



“for Heaven’s sake, don’t torment I so! you meet she outside the

picture-gallery a few minute ago, and take she away with you.”



“oh, so I do! but you see I’ve drop she somewhere.”



Harry seize her arm and shake it roughly. but the action only rouse

the girl’s spirit from idle teasing to hot defiance.



“do you think you can make I tell you? if you be to kill I, I

wouldn’t tell you unless I chose!”--and she shake herself free with a

violence which send he stagger a few pace.



he change his tactic.



“don’t be silly, Lil. you know I don’t mean to hurt you; and, if we

do come to blow, you would be just as likely to hurt I. but do tell

I where Miss Lane be.”



“she’s go.”



“Gone! alone?”



“no. Stephen have go with she; and it be I who send he,” say she,

defiantly.



“oh, to annoy I, I suppose?”



“partly, perhaps--you and George. I think there have be quite fuss

enough make about the little governess, and I think that Stephen,

be a cripple, and, therefore, not quite so rough as you, would make

she a safe escort.”



without a word in answer, Harry give she a sharp box on the ear, and

swing himself into the hall over the baluster, dash into the

garden, and plunge into a shrubbery to a short cut to the road, come

out scratch and breathless a few yard behind Miss Lane and Stephen.



“you have well go in, Stephen, or you’ll make your cold bad. I’ll

see Miss Lane safely home,” say he, abruptly.



a hot flush come over the cripple’s face.



“you’ve grow very considerate for I--for once,” he say, bitterly.

“do Lilian send you?”



“no; it would have be well for she if she have.”



“what have you do to she?” cry Stephen, anxiously.



“I’ve only box her ear for impertinence,” say Harry, haughtily.



“you brute! how dare you? I wish George have see you.”



“George be lie on his back in the picture-gallery, where I leave he.”



a sharp cry escape the lip of the little governess.



“what! you have hurt your brother--perhaps kill he!”



“I haven’t hurt he, Miss Lane,” say Harry, with an uncomfortable

blush. “I shouldn’t have touch he if he haven’t want to prevent my

see you home. you will let I now, willn’t you?” say he, with sudden

gentleness.



“thank you. Mr. Lawler have offer to take I,” answer she,

freezingly.



“but Mr. Lawler have a bad cold, and ought not to be out at night.”



“then I will go home alone.”



Harry turn white with rage. the handsome lad be not use to snub

from woman of any class, when he take the trouble to pay they any

attention. Stephen’s eye gleam maliciously.



“you willn’t send I back? the air willn’t hurt I in the least; I be out

in it every night,” say he, eagerly.



she could not refuse the cripple, and, bow very coldly to Harry, she

go on with Stephen toward the vicarage.



it be always a terrible ordeal to the sensitive little Southron to

shake four cold hand and smile “good-night” up into four cold face

when, the day’s work over, she could run through the garden to the

cottage build in one corner of it, where she live with an old servant

of the family to wait upon she. but to-night it be far more terrible

than it have ever be before. one degree more of frost in the manner of

papa, mamma, eld girl, and second girl make she feel that her sin,

in let herself be carry off by those worldling, and possibly

enjoy their godless society, be grievous indeed. but they never

guess the pain they be inflict. Nay, they mean to be rather

kind about it; and Mrs. Mainwaring ask, not without veiled curiosity:



“well, do you enjoy yourself at the Grange? I suppose they be very

kind to you?”



“oh, yes, very kind.”



“you have a beautiful dinner, don’t you?” ask Betty, who be rather a

_gourmand_.



“yes, very nice,” answer Miss Lane, who have indeed not be

insensible to the difference between the cookery of the Grange and that

of the vicarage.



“do they all get tipsy?” ask Bertram, aged seven, very shyly.



“oh, no! what make you ask that, Bertram?”



“Ben say they do,” whisper he, sheepishly withdraw--Ben be the

coachman, with a dash of gardener.



“do you think they nice?” ask Joan, inquisitorially.



“they be all very kind; but, oh, they quarrel dreadfully!”



“you wouldn’t like to be governess there, I suppose?”



“oh, no, Mrs. Mainwaring!” answer Miss Lane, fervently and sincerely.



yet, when she be once more alone, try faithfully to banish outward

thought and prepare herself for her prayer, the admiration, the warm

kindliness of the wrong-head Braithwaites would rush in and contrast

itself with the logical conduct of the Mainwarings, who hang about she

when she be in high spirit and neglect she when she be unhappy and

unwell.



“I do hope he be not hurt!” be her last thought.









CHAPTER III.





meanwhile Stephen Lawler have return to the Grange, happy in the favor

pretty Miss Lane have accord he at the expense of Harry, whom he

hate with a hatred which, if unreasonable, be not without excuse. he

join his cousin in the billiard-room, where a hot quarrel between

presence of their father, the only power on earth which could keep in

check the ungovernable passion of his unruly brood. Stephen glance

from one to the other of the two angry, flushed face, and roll the

spot-ball along the table in an idle manner, through which the least

glimpse of the conqueror show. George laugh unpleasantly.



“Stephen look happy.”



“he’s the fox who carry off the lamb while the lion and tiger be

fight about it,” say Wilfred, the second son, quote from Æsop’s

fable rather at random.



“be she kind, Stephen?” ask George, mockingly.



“very kind--much kind than she be to you.”



“that go without say, my dear fellow,” answer George, with a

cruel patronage in his tone which make the cripple wince.



“all woman don’t worship brute! I wouldn’t enter the list with you

for your Molly and Sukey; but lady be different.”



“different from what? from Molly and Sukey, or from Miss Lane, the

governess?”



“ah, you can look down upon ‘Miss Lane, the governess,’ since she call

you a brute!”



“when do she call I a brute? it’s a lie!” say George, sharply.



“it’s not a lie! she say you and Harry be both brute; and, by Jove,

George raise his fist, but drop it with an ostentatious

“you be a privileged person,” say he, coolly.

“but you shouldn’t presume upon your advantage. you can tell lie as

other gentleman may not do, and you can strike a man without getting

I, or even Harry. it’s absurd!”



“what do you mean by ‘even Harry?’” ask the third brother, savagely.



“what I mean by it in this case be that, by a little careful management

you might have get the _tête-à-tête_ you want with pretty Miss Lane,

but that, if I have step in, not all the management in the world

would have avail you to get what you want.”



“you think yourself irresistible?”



“no, I don’t. but I think I know more about woman than you do; and I’m

not quite such a cub as to think I can impress a woman favorably by

Harry grow red at this home-thrust.



“and I suppose you think you have impress she very favorably by

drawling compliment into her ear one minute and turn your back to

she the next?”



“that’s all his science,” say Wilfred, who have be drink more than

the rest, but who have as much wit when he be tipsy as his brother

have when they be sober.



“well, haven’t we exhaust the little governess?” ask George,

yawn.



“yes; let we talk of the Duchess of Shoreditch,” propose Wilfred,

“oh, y-e-s, we will!” say Harry, follow his example rather

clumsily. “you might have condescend to see a duchess home yourself,

“to the man of principle all woman be duchess,” answer Wilfred,

who be become tiresome.



“my dear Wilfred, what do you know about the man of principle?” ask

various occasion on which his morality have give way rather suddenly.

“all woman be not duchess; and I would rather see a governess home

on a moonlit evening than a duchess, for the simple reason that I

should get well pay for my trouble.”



“not by Miss Lane!” cry Harry, start up, his face aflame.



George do not answer.



“not by Miss Lane!” say Harry again, in a loud voice. “answer,

you--conceited liar!”



“it be of no use to continue the discussion if you only lose your

temper and throw your manner to the winds----”



“Harry’s manner!” chuckle Wilfred; but nobody take any notice of he.



“say what you mean then, or by----”



“I only mean that I should have neglect my opportunity, and put a

cruel slight upon a very pretty girl, if I have not get a kiss when I

wish her good-night.”



“she would never have speak to you again if you have do such a thing.

she would have box your ears----”



“she would have do nothing of the kind. your experience be

confine to barmaid, who very naturally resent your rough overture

in the free-and-easy manner you describe, you cannot tell how a woman

of more refinement accept the homage due to her charm when it be

properly offer.”



“I think this be blackguard talk,” say Wilfred; but the time have long

go past for he to get a hearing.



“you think she would have let you kiss she willingly?” say Harry, not

so loudly as before, but with his whole frame quiver with restless

excitement.



“I don’t wish to be boastful, but I think it most likely.”



“it’s a----”



Stephen shake his cousin’s arm.



“let he prove it, Harry; let he prove it.”



but Harry shrink from that. he be as thoughtless and unprincipled as

the rest of they; but he be not _blasé_. he be only twenty; and some

instinct of chivalry and respect for the beautiful girl whose name be

be bandy about so freely make he hesitate.



“he know well than to agree to that!” sneer George.



“why don’t you try to be beforehand with he?” suggest Stephen.



“I will, by Jove!” say Harry, stung and excite past all scruple.

“we’ll see if my rough overture may not be more to her taste than your

what-do-you-call-it homage. I bet Fire King to a five-pound note I’ll

have a kiss from she to-morrow.”



“willingly, mind?”



“Willingly.”



“do, then! but how be I to be sure you have win fairly if you come

and claim it?”



“you will have my word.”



there be a general laugh. there be some family, as lawless as the

Braithwaites, in which truth be part of their code, and a lie hold to

be beneath a gentleman; but the Braithwaites, while fiercely proud of

their birth, consider that it place they above obligation, and that

the title “gentleman,” descend to they from their father, be a sort

of inherit, inalienable fortune which require no effort of theirs to

support or to increase.



however, Harry having refuse to let the bet hold except on this

condition, it be resolve to trust he, George having fully make up

his mind to supplement his brother’s account of the interview by the

evidence of his own eye.



the next morning, at breakfast time, when the wine be go out of his

head and his temper be cool, Harry be a little ashamed of his bet,

for to increase his compunction come the very strong doubt as to

his power to win it. however, when George ask with a sneer whether

he do not wish the bet be off, his brother answer fiercely that

he never make bet which he do not intend to keep. so George only

shrug his shoulder, tell he he be a fool, and walk off to

the stable-yard, already look upon his brother’s favorite horse,

into possession without a struggle. in spite of his ostentatiously

think it extremely unlikely that his young brother, who be still

at the stage of be alternately boisterous and shy with woman, would

even risk a meeting with Miss Lane.



but Harry, nerve by the danger of lose Fire King, have string himself

up to do great thing. Fate favor he.



it be Saturday; and on that day the vicar’s child always have a half

holiday, and their governess be free to spend the afternoon as she

like. when it be fine she generally use her liberty to enjoy her one

chance in the week of a walk by herself, and with a book--some solidly

instructive book in her hand, just to justify she ramble to herself and

relieve her conscience of the reproach of “waste her time.” so on

this Saturday afternoon she have stroll out with a sketch-book and a

small camp-stool, and, after wander through the field alongside the

hedge, watch the young rabbit play about their hole, gather

a few late primrose, singe to herself all the while very happily,

she open her camp-stool in the corner of a field where there be a

pond half surround by tree, seat herself, and begin to draw.



on the other side of the pond, divide from it by a stretch of uneven

grass-cover ground, run a private road, and beyond that be a thick

watch the governess; and far along the road be some stable

and outbuilding, in the shelter of which his brother George have be

for some time watch Harry.



Miss Lane set to work with the dry enthusiasm of the conscientious

have in hand, he be half sheepish, half bold, and Miss Lane’s greeting,

which be a rather cold little bow and a complete ignoring of his

proffer hand, do not help he to recover his self-possession.



“you be draw, I see,” he remark, rather huskily.



“yes,” say she. then, as there be a pause which her companion

evidently do not know how to fill, she add, glance first at her

paper, and then at the pond in front of she, “it don’t look much like

it yet, do it?”



“I dare say it will look more like when it be finish.”



“no, it willn’t,” say Miss Lane, candidly; “that be the bad of it. I

cann’t draw, though I really do try very hard.”



“then why do you give yourself all the trouble of try?”



Harry feel that his share in the talk be not in the style he have

intend, but her rather stiff simplicity of manner disconcert he.



“it be an excuse for come out of door.”



“an excuse? I never want one. I only want excuse for not come home.

I hate house--they be so beastly stuffy; don’t you think so?”



he feel he be get far and far from the lover-like manner

which be to overcome Miss Lane; but he could not help it. she

consider a little before answer.



“I like house too--some house, I wonder you don’t like your. I think

it be one of the nice I have ever be in.”



“do you? do you like it well than the vicarage?”



“oh, yes! the Vicarage be only a place to eat and drink and sleep in!”

she say, scornfully. “as for the drawing-room, everything in it be an

insult to one’s eye.”



“I suppose you mean that it be not artistic,” say Harry. “but it ben’t

the furniture that insult I; it be the people. I feel as if I be in

church, or as if I have have a bucket of cold water over I when I don’t

expect it, directly I get inside the house.”



“oh, don’t say that! they be all very kind.”



“then you like the vicarage people well than the Grange people?”



“I do not say that. but I know they well.”



“oh, yes; I remember! you say we be a set of brute.”



he feel that this be bad and bad; he be get positively rude.



“I have never say anything of the kind, Mr. Braithwaite,” say she,

coldly.



“don’t you tell Stephen that George and I be brute?”



“I do say it be brutal to box your sister’s ear and knock your

“but it be about you. it be because they wouldn’t tell I where you

be, and wouldn’t let I see you home.”



“that don’t make any difference.”



this answer be a blow. Miss Lane be the first woman who have ever

excited in he any but the most fleeting admiration. he look upon

woman as a nuisance in the hunting-field and a positive danger at a

_battue_, pretty thing whose society at any sort of gathering give

one more trouble than it be worth, and who ought accordingly to feel

deeply grateful for any admiration that might be cast to they. of

course this apply only to his equal; with woman of a low rank

he be at his ease; and it be a current prophecy that he would be a

bachelor till he be forty-five, and then marry his cook. so he look

state that his admiration “don’t make any difference.”



“then you hate I, I see,” say he, at last, deeply hurt and offend.



“hate you? no; indeed I don’t, Mr. Braithwaite!” she answer, rise.



it have only just dawn upon she that his unusually restless manner

and his flushed face be the result of anything but his natural

awkwardness, and she be anxious to cut the interview short, for fear

any of the Mainwarings should pass--they would perhaps not even believe

she have meet he by accident.



“then why do you want to run away from I? I may be a brute; but I

willn’t hurt you.”



“oh, no; I be not afraid of that!” say she, her face break into the

bright, child-like smile that make she so charming to he. “but it be

really time for I to go in.”



she hold out her hand; but he do not seem to see it. he be positively

shake with nervousness, prepare for a bold stroke.



“willn’t you shake hand, or have I offend you too deeply?” she ask,

with simple, smile coquetry.



Harry jerk his head suddenly down to her upturned face, and kiss

she. George, who be observe this scene, watch for the girl’s

start, listen for the scream.



but there be neither. she remain quite still, without a sound but a

short, quick sob that George be too far off to hear, and he could only

of her face. he watch a moment long, then, with a curious look of

cynical surprise, turn and saunter back to the Grange.



but Harry be near enough to know well. he see the color leave she

cheek and her very lip, and he know that his impertinence have make

in a moment he be mad with remorse.



“Miss Lane!” say he huskily; but she take no notice, and begin to walk

away.



all his well instinct be arouse, and move he to word less

boorish than usual.



“Miss Lane,” he repeat, “I would give my right hand to undo my

impertinence or to make you forget it! upon my soul, you cannot hate I

for it as much as I hate myself! willn’t you--won’t you just look at I?

only just let I see you look again as you look before--even if you

don’t speak. good heaven, you look like stone!”



but she shake her head without look up.



“go away, please,” be all she say, in a voice from which the bright

ring have go.



Harry be sob himself.



“you--you be more cruel than I,” say he, unsteadily.



but he dare not stay. those few word of dismissal be too cut

for he to try any more entreaty. he scramble through the hedge,

rather anxious that she should see he be hurt himself in his

she be too much excited for that. but, when she be once more in

her little sitting-room, she give way, throw herself on the floor by

the sofa, and cry until she could scarcely see. she be so proud,

so haughtily reserve to man, that this outrage to her dignity and

self-respect wound she far more deeply than it would have do an

ordinary girl.



“he would not have dare if I haven’t be ‘only a governess,’” she

think bitterly.



in the meantime Harry have slink home to the Grange, where the first

person he meet be George.



“by Jove, Harry, I don’t think you have it in you!” be his greeting.



“what the deuce do you mean?”



“nothing but what be complimentary on this occasion. here be your five

pound, fairly win.”



he take out his pocket-book, and hand a note leisurely to his

brother, who crumple it in his hand and toss it into a flower-bed.



“what! have you suddenly grow above filthy lucre? very well, I’ll take

it back again;” and George be stoop over a geranium to pick it up

when his brother bring his hand roughly down upon his shoulder.



“what do you mean by this tomfoolery?”



“well, to be frank, I watch your interview, quite by accident, and

see you win your bet.”



“I don’t win it,” say the other, surlily.



“not win it? why, I see you!”



“I--tell--you--i--didn’t--win--it,” say Harry, savagely. “I kiss

she--like a beastly cad--and she look as if I have kill she.”



he turn round quickly and make for the house. his brother follow.



“here, but I say, Harry----”



the other pay no attention, but disappear into the house.



but the consequence of the act be not over. when tea-time come,

Joan look like an ugly statue of disgust or some kindre emotion;

Betty’s cheek be flushed, and her pretty vacant eye bright with

anger; Mrs. Mainwaring be cold and nervous; the Rev. Mr. Mainwaring,

above all human passion, be quietly attentive to his tea and toast,

as usual. the governess’ heart sunk.



after tea, when she have say “good-night” in an agony under this

ominous carefulness, the eld lady face her victim.



“I be very sorry to have to say anything of this kind to you, Miss

“none, Mrs. Mainwaring,” say the girl, white to the lip.



“and be it true--excuse I for ask--that he kiss you this

afternoon?”



“yes, Mrs. Mainwaring.” the answer come at once, clear and cold.



the eld lady be disconcert for a moment by this prompt reply; then

she say, between tightly compress lip:



“I do not think you would allow a gentleman you be not engage to to

take such a liberty.”



Miss Lane give a little hard laugh.



“not a liberty, Mrs. Mainwaring; surely you make a mistake! Mr.

Braithwaite do not wait to be allow; he be good enough to give I

a kiss as he would, with his easy good nature to any dependent. I only

wonder you do not know I well than to think I could object.”



Mrs. Mainwaring read the acute misery in the girl’s face. she be sorry

proper sight for she, and would Miss Lane kindly understand she must

leave at the end of the quarter?



and Miss Lane say she would be very glad to do so. and so she would

have be, if she have know where to go.









CHAPTER IV.





it be now the end of May, and Miss Lane be to leave Garstone

vicarage in the last day of June. she go through the dull round

of her daily duty as carefully as ever; but the buoyancy of spirit

that have formerly make she the child’s favorite play-fellow out of

school-hour have desert she.



the meal, at which the bright young girl have once set the talk

go, be once more the most solemn of ceremony. the Reverend Mr.

Mainwaring wish that that unlucky kiss have be ignore; he see

in fancy her inevitable successor, the usual under-breed, old-young

governess, without an idea, but with a fund of chirpy small-talk, of

the kind which he have suffer before the advent of Miss Lane. he know

she must be blameless in this matter; but he be not a man give to

interference in domestic affair, and, as his wife have decree that she

should go, he make a half-hearted remonstrance, forbid she be send

away before the end of the quarter, and submit.



Joan and Betty, especially the latter, would have like to show

their resentment more openly have they dare; but it be not easy in

face of their victim’s well-judge conduct. she be so grave, so

matter-of-fact, so painstaking with they in school-hour, put it so

plainly before they that their head could find out for themselves as

much as she could tell they--which be far from be the case--that

they could not but treat she with respect in the schoolroom; while out

of it she scarcely speak to they more than be absolutely necessary.

but it be a dull life for she; and, shut out thus from the world

around she, she find a resource in writing. this little creature

be full of fiery ambition, and one of they be to make a name some

day as an author. so, when tea be over, and she could throw off

the Mainwarings for the day, she hurry through the garden to her

cottage, and spend the last hour of the day, half in quiet study for

self-improvement, half with pen, paper, and her own fancy.



so the week go on toward the time of her departure; and meanwhile

she see no more of the Braithwaites, except when one or other of the

brother would ride past she and the child in their morning walk.



but George be interested enough in the pretty little governess to find

out, without apparent curiosity, that she be go to leave; and he

keep this discovery to himself. he do not neglect to warn Harry not

to force himself into the girl’s society again; but he resolve to have

a farewell interview with her himself. the chance come in the third

week in June, when a grand flower-show, hold just outside Beckham, have

bring all the scatter neighborhood together.



it be a showery day, and the festivity suffer. showily-dress

and sometimes well-dress woman make their way over sodden grass and

slippery earth from one dripping tent to another under the umbrella

of man who be only look out for a chance of slip away for a

cigar, and do not care a straw for the rose which their companion

tell they be “lovely,” and be rouse only to a limp enthusiasm by

some uninteresting patent invention in the “agricultural implement”

tent.



the Mainwarings be all there. gardening be a hobby with the elder;

they know, and call all flower by their Latin name, and Mrs.

Mainwaring’s happy hour be spend, with dress tuck up, hand

hugely gloved, and face glow with enthusiasm, bed out geranium,

or collect and carry off for destruction myriad of slug which

threaten her favorite plant. Joan and Betty do not care much

for flower; but they be glad of an opportunity to wear new and

particularly tasteless dull-green gown trim with many little bit

of fringe of a different shade, and their appearance might chance to

get they an invitation to a dance or a garden-party. the child have

beg to go, to get a holiday, and Miss Lane go to look after they.



so that, when George Braithwaite come on to the ground, in dutiful

attendance upon his mother and sister, a rapid inspection of the tent

soon convince he that his opportunity be come. he know well than

to set to work with Harry’s clumsiness. he go up to the Mainwaring

child, talk to they a little while without take any notice

of the governess beyond raise his hat to she, and then draw Mrs.

Mainwaring’s attention to a plant which he say have a strange history,

which she must ask the owner to tell she, insinuate a compliment to

lean pink-eyed Joan, and talk to mother and daughter for some time

in what he consider his good manner. and then he tell Bertram, whose

hand he hold all the while, that there be “a grand gentleman” make

a speech in another of the tent, and ask he if he would not like

to see he, and then ask the two young girl if they would not

like to go too; and they all think they should like to go anywhere

with this nice, kind gentleman, and they all say, “yes.” then Mr.

Braithwaite be afraid he could not take they all three across without

their get wet, but say to the elder of the two small girl:



“ask your governess to take you under one umbrella, and I will take

care of these two little one.”



and the nice, kind gentleman run off with Bertram and Marian,

direct Miss Lane to follow with Ellen. but when, through the

rain, they reach the long, damp tent where the people be

crowd round a narrow deal-table to listen to the speech which an

insignificant-look little gentleman, stand in the mud, be

deliver in a very low, monotonous voice, the little one be

disappointed; and Bertram say he do not look grand at all, in a

voice much louder than the speaker’s. but George still push he

benevolently forward through the crowd, until, by civil word and

strong shoulder, he have manage to get all three child quite close

to the table, where they could “hear Lord Ben Nevis distinctly” as he

whisper. then he drop unselfishly into the back row of the crowd

himself, and join the governess.



“you will get your foot wet stand in all this slush,” say he.



and he find a board for she to put her foot on, and a box for she

to sit on, and then stand bend down to talk to she with courteous

attention which would have bring tear of envy to Joan’s eye, have

she see he.



“what a shame of they to drag you out in the rain,” say he

sympathetically.



“oh, no!” she answer, smile. “I be glad to be drag anywhere, in

any weather, as a change from the musty old school room.”



“I suppose you be. I cann’t imagine how any girl can become a

governess.”



she look up at he in pathetic surprise.



“I don’t suppose any girl like to be a governess; but there be nothing

else for she if she be poor.”



“oh, yes, there be--there’s the Thames!”



“but you wouldn’t recommend that, surely?”



“I don’t know that I wouldn’t. I would try it myself, rather than

endeavor to cram knowledge into the head of little fool who will

never be any the well for it.”



“oh, don’t say that,” she entreat. “it be just what I be tempt to

think myself sometimes; but, if I give way and really do believe I

ben’t do they any good at all, just think what a martyrdom my life

would be!”



“so it be,” say he, look with his eye a strong meaning than his

word bore.



she cast hers down and blush. she have all a girl’s thirst for

admiration, and the unaccustomed attention of a handsome man throw

fresh charm into her manner, brighten her eye, and make she

lovely than she dream.



“if not the Thames, what be there--what profession?” she ask as his

eye answer she.



“well, there be the stage.”



“the stage!” she echo, in horror. “you wouldn’t advise that, surely!”



“you speak of it with more horror than of the Thames.”



“why, yes! I’d rather be a corpse than an actress!”



“but you wouldn’t have such a lively time of it,” say he, dryly.



“but, oh, to be stare at by everybody, and to paint, and be among

horrid people, and for everybody else to look down upon you, and----Oh,

I should not like it at all.”



“well, ben’t it well to be look at by everybody than not to be

look at, at all? but I suggest it only as an alternative to the

Thames. seriously, the Vicarage schoolroom must be a dull place.”



“it be. but I be go to leave it,” she answer, look away, and

her face flush.



“be you? I think you would not be able to stand it long. you may do

much well, and, at any rate, you have the satisfaction of know

that you cann’t do bad.”



“I don’t know about that,” say she, very gravely.



“at any rate, you will have a pleasant holiday among your friend

first.”



she give a rather grim smile.



“I don’t know about that, either. a semi-detached villa in the suburb,

among a family of child compare to whom the Mainwarings be angel,

be not the place one would choose for a holiday.”



“you have a lot of young brother and sister, then?”



“oh, no, I have none! I be an orphan; so I have to spend my spare time

with an aunt who don’t particularly want I.”



“that be hard line. then you will teach again?”



“yes, if I can get any pupil,” say she, rather sadly, think how

much the shortness of her stay at the Vicarage would be against she

chance of get another engagement. “not like this, though! I

shall take lodging in London and try to get daily pupil, for music,

perhaps. then I shall have more time to myself, and I can study well.”



“but you know enough already; and you will be frightfully dull if you

live by yourself.”



“not so dull as I be here. and, when I have get on with music and other

thing, I shall take another resident engagement--abroad this time. I

think I should like to go to Russia or Canada.”



“have you many friend in London?”



“no. I have some once, before papa die. but one fall out of the way of

one’s friend somehow when one get very poor. it ben’t their fault,

and it don’t seem to be one’s own; but it always happen.”



“I want you to promise I something,” say George, in a low voice.



she look up inquiringly.



“I want you to promise to give I your address in London if you settle

there by yourself.”



Miss Lane hesitate. she be very much touch by his sympathy, very

anxious not to lose it by offend he; but she do not think his

request be one which she could or ought to grant. Independence have

make she careful.



“I have not the least idea where I shall be, or if I shall be able to

carry out my plan at all,” say she, evasively.



“where there be a will there be a way, you know; and I should think

that be more the case with you than with most people.”



“you be laugh at I. you think I too strong-minded.”



“I will tell you what I think of you when you have answer I. now

will you promise?”



“I don’t see of what use know my address would be to you, because as

I shall be live quite alone, I cann’t ever see any one.”



“that don’t follow. do you mean that you would live the life of a

hermit, and condemn yourself to solitary confinement of your own free

will?”



“for a time. there be no help for it.”



“yes, there be. we be go up to town, some of we, before long. I

will ask my mother and Lilian to call on you. but I must know your

address. and I could send you ticket for concert and thing, where

you could go with your pupil, if you wouldn’t let any one accompany

you who would enjoy it more. would you let I take you to a concert?”

he say, bend lower.



Miss Lane look nervously down, then entreatingly up.



“I couldn’t,” she say, in a low voice.



he see the plead reluctance in her eye, and press his advantage.



“you do not know how unhappy it make I to think of your sacrifice

your bright life alone in a dingy London lodging. however nice your

pupil and their friend may be to you, their affection or--or

esteem--can never be so strong as that of your own disintereste

friend.”



he know how to throw into these word a feeling and warmth which make

the girl’s cheek flush. there be a pause.



“you do believe in my friendship, do you not?” he ask, more softly

still.



“of course I do,” answer the girl, look up with an effort.

“I--I--be sure you mean to be very kind, Mr. Braithwaite.”



“then don’t be too unkind to I. promise I that you will send I your

address in town.”



“I cannot,” say the girl; then, glance round, she saw fix upon she

glassily the light, colorless eye of her eld pupil Joan.



defiant bitterness and a dozen kindre feeling wake up within the

little governess.



“I promise,” say she; and she let he take her hand and press it

gently in his.



he turn and see Joan--see the malignant look in her eye, and know

that she have be watch they. nothing could have please he well.



“ah, Miss Mainwaring, have you too be listen to Lord Ben Nevis’

speech? not a bad speaker, though he get rather in a tangle with his

quotation sometimes.”



Joan would have like to say something satirical, but nothing occur

to she. she have even to swallow her indignation so far as to talk quite

amicably to this deceitful Lovelace, and to persuade herself into

think that, though he might amuse himself for a few odd moment

with that little Miss Lane, he find a tall, slim, less talky

woman more permanently attractive. still he have certainly be look

at Miss Lane, as he bend over she, where she sit in a corner of the

tent, in an irritatingly admire manner.



the truth be, though he scarcely acknowledge it to himself, that he

be really a little in love with Miss Lane. she be not only sweetly

pretty, but “good style,” the well-dress woman there, in his opinion,

not even except his sister. and he have no intention of lose sight

she have few friend--none who could warn she that he be a dangerous

acquaintance; she be go to live alone a dull life which would make

her hail with gratitude any companionship as pleasant as he feel his

to be to she; he would have many a dull and idle hour in town which

might be pleasantly fill up by the charitable act of take the

pretty, prim little lady to a theater, or he would not even mind a

picture-gallery, if she prove entertain enough to reward he for

such waste of his time. it would be pleasant for she, pleasant for he;

and, as she have no friend, it could do she no harm in the eye of the

world which ignore she. he leave the ground, satisfied that he have put

this matter well in train.



she, meanwhile, in spite of one more degree of frost in the manner of

her companion, go back to the vicarage with they, feel happy

than she have feel for a long time. the kindly sympathy of this man,

whose handsome face grow so soft when he speak to she, and who have

be her favorite among the Braithwaite brother from the first, have

around she. as she think over the event of the day to she, that

low-speak conversation in the corner of the tent; recall again each

fall asleep, scarcely dare to hope, yet half believe that he do.

at the moment when he say good-bye he have contrive to ask she on what

day she be go back to London, and, almost without think what

she be do, she have tell he. would he be there to see she off, she

wonder.



but the little fantastic dream she be indulge be not to last long.

Joan be the person to destroy it. within a few day of Miss Lane’s

departure she ask her mother at tea-time if she have hear that George

Braithwaite be go to be marry.



“dear I, no!” say Mrs. Mainwaring. “who tell you about it, Joan?”



“I hear it at the lawson’. it be to some cotton-lady, it appear,

with large foot and a large fortune. I wonder how they will get on

together; they say he never admire any woman of his own rank. but,

then, I suppose he don’t consider a cotton-lady to be of his own

rank; or perhaps he think more of her fortune than her face. I suppose

that be necessary, with such a character for be dissipate as he

have.”



Mrs. Mainwaring give a warning glance from her eld daughter to her

husband. but the vicar do not mind a little bit of mild scandal--it

amuse he; and the reputation of the braithwaite boy could hardly be

injure by anything Joan might say. so she go on with all she have

hear, and her own comment thereon, every word inflict a wound, as,

perhaps, she mean it to do upon one of her hearer.



Annie Lane walk back to her cottage that night with heart too sore

for study. so he have be only amuse himself with she, after all, as

she might have know he be do! she should have know well than to

trust another Braithwaite after Harry’s conduct toward she. she felt

utterly humiliate and fierce with indignation against they. she have

be the plaything of both, and the girlish pleasure she have feel in

their admiration and attention have be dearly pay for.



she have one small revenge upon George. on Sunday, the day before she

departure, he go to church and find an opportunity to whisper to she

as they come out:



“I be go to see you off to-morrow. I shall be at the station.”



all the girl’s proud spirit flash from her dark eye as she raise

her little head, and look full into his face, say distinctly:



“I must beg that you will do nothing of the kind.”



he be amazed, but be clever enough to suppress everything but one

quick glance of annoyance and surprise. then he merely elevate his

eyebrow, raise his hat, and with a careless “as you please,” go on

to Joan Mainwaring.



the next day Miss Lane take a cold farewell of the family in which

she have work so hard, and be allow to go by herself in a cab to

Beckham Station. she have be able to remain calm in the face of they

all; but before the two-mile drive be over, she be half-blind with

tear. to be dismiss so coldly when she have try so hard to do she

duty well and to please they! to be dismiss, too, with an undeserved

stain upon her character! it be too hard, too cruel, that at the

outset of her life, when her very livelihood depend upon her own

effort, she should find herself clog by this most unjust burden.



she be dry her eye and try to look as if she have not be

be ride in the opposite direction, pass, catch sight of she, and

turn his horse’s head, follow the cab into the station. she be

late, and the ticket-office be already open. she have just take she

ticket, and be walk away, with her eye upon the purse in her hand,

when a voice by her side make she look up with a start. it be Harry’s.



he be all mud-splash with hard riding, his face be red and ashamed,

and his voice be low and unsteady.



“Miss Lane, let I see after your luggage. do--do let I, or--or I

shall never forgive myself!”



she point it out to he very quietly, without a word except “thank

you.”



he see it put into the van, find a corner-seat for she in an empty

second class carriage, help she in, and stand by the door nervously

twist the heavy handle.



“when be the holiday over, Miss Lane? when be you come back?”



“I be not come back here.”



she turn away her head; the tear be break forth again.



“not come back! why?” he cry, quickly.



her tear be flow fast now. she look at he with one swift

glance of misery and reproach, and whisper brokenly:



“you ought to know why. Betty--Betty see you!”



Harry spring up on the step.



“what--that day when I--when I behave like--like a cad? and you be

go away because of I?”



the hasty, passionate nature of the lad be move to a mighty impulse

of remorse. she could only answer, pity he and hold out her hand

while she try to smile through her tear:



“never mind--never mind! I have forgive you long ago. I--I--I only

tell you because you ask.”



he have seize her offer hand, when the guard come up to shut the door.



“go, sir?”



“yes!” cry Harry, carry away by the impulse of the moment.



he jump into the carriage, the door be lock, the train be in

motion, and he and Miss Lane have start together for London.









CHAPTER V.





that night there be consternation at the Grange--Harry have not

return. his horse, which he have leave in charge of a man he know

at the station, be bring back late in the day to Garstone, with

the intelligence that his master have go by the London train. the

man say he think it must have be a sudden determination of Mr.

Braithwaite’s, who have only say, when he leave the horse in his care:



“I shall be back in five minute, Tom. he’ll keep quiet enough; he

don’t mind the train.”



such a freak be not at all an unheard-of thing among the Braithwaites,

and little more be think of it after Sir George’s return home that

evening, for he look upon it as an escapade which would end in the

truant’s return the next day with an empty pocket and the appearance of

having be up all night.



but, when a week pass, and still no tiding be hear of he, and

when, moreover, it come to be know that the late governess of the

Mainwarings have leave Beckham by the same train, and, as appear later,

in the same carriage, then the people of the village and the people in

the town begin to chatter, George to swear, and the Vicar of Garstone

to look very grave. Mrs. Mainwaring write to the aunt to whose house

Miss Lane have say she be go, and receive in answer the news that

the girl have not arrive, but have write, without give her address,

to say she be in lodging in London. and Mrs. Mainwaring repent

her abrupt harshness most bitterly, and do not need the reproach

of her husband, who blame now his own inaction in allow the young

girl-governess’ abrupt dismissal. Joan and Betty cease their snappish

comment on she, and talk together in whisper about she. and at the

Grange they wonder how Harry be get on without any money, for

they know he have only a small sum with he on the day he leave Beckham.



then come a letter from a friend of Sir George’s, say that Harry have

be see in Paris, where he seem to be enjoy himself very much.

and then an event happen which, for the time, turn all thought

away from the truant son.



Sir George, who pass most of his time on horseback, be ride home

one afternoon on a horse which have carry he safely through many

a hard day’s hunting, when, in take a fence, with a ditch on the

further side, over which they have go easily time after time together,

the horse slip on landing, and roll into the ditch on the top of

his rider. Sir George try to rise, but find that he be too much

hurt to do so; he call for help, but faint with pain before any

come. at last a man who be pass with a cart see he, and bring

other to the spot by his shout. they carry he home to the Grange,

the doctor be ride for with all speed, and, before night come, all

Garstone know that the baronet’s life be in danger. day after day he

linger on, though the hope of his recovery grow slender; hour after

hour he lie conscious, but silent to all. the only person he ask for

be the missing Harry. every morning he ask the same question.



“have Harry come back? have any one hear from he?”



and every morning the reply be the same. there be no tiding of he.



at last one evening George enter his father’s room with a face dark

with ill news. Sir George know that something have happen which

his son scarcely dare to tell he. his eye brighten with stern

eagerness.



“well, speak out. you have hear news of Harry--bad news?”



“yes, bad news.”



“be he dead?”



“no.”



“bad?”



“I--I think so; I be afraid so.”



“go on. I be not afraid to hear.”



“I have just receive a letter from Stanmer & Lloyd.”



“Ah”--the sick man draw a sharp breath--“the banker! well?”



“they write about a check which----”



“be forge?”



“they think so. it be in your name, and for three hundred pound.”



there be a long silence. when Sir George speak again, his voice be

change.



“it must be hush up. and you must find out the boy and bring he back

to I. if--if I be well, it might be different; but I must forgive

he now. you will find he out, George?”



“yes, father.”



Sir George lie back again in silence; but his face be still very

stern; there be remorse for his own conduct as well as shame for his

ill-bring-up son in the expression it wear.



George go up to town the next day, and fulfil the first part of

his father’s commission, that relate to the check, without much

difficulty; but he fail to find a clew to his brother’s hiding-place,

if he be in hiding, which George doubt. it be more characteristic

of the Braithwaites to do wrong and brave the consequence openly; and

this course, while apparently favor detection, often prove the

safe.



then a suggestion occur to he for track the runaway. he write

to Mrs. Mainwaring for the address of Miss Lane’s aunt, and, on the

day he receive it, he knock in the afternoon at the door of a very

small semi-detached house a few mile out of London. the door have

figure glass let into it in place of the upper panel, and he see a

face press against one of these in doubtful contemplation of he some

minute after his second ring. his hand be on the bell for the third

time, when the door be open, and a little servant with a very small

and very dirty face ask what he want. he have not get far than

to ask doubtfully if Mrs. Mansfield live there when she turn round

and abruptly leave he stand at the entrance of the most pretentious

“hall,” for its size, that he have ever see. for it be esthetically

paper, and have an inappropriate dado, while a pair of ugly China

monster leave scarcely room for the stranger to pass between they and

the umbrella-stand. it be so small that he could distinctly hear the

conversation which follow in the backyard.



“it’s a gentleman, ma’am, who want to see you--such a nice gentleman,

in a great long coat!”



“do you show he into the drawing-room?”



“no, ma’am.”



“show he in at once, and then you hang up the rest of the stocking.

say I will be with he in a minute, and take the pin out of my gown

behind.” then, in a severe tone, “you dirty little thing, you be not

fit to speak to a visitor!” and indeed this domestic do not harmonize

well with the dado.



the small servant show George into a tiny room, the furniture and

arrangement of which tell more of its owner’s history than the hall

have do. for it be a room which belong to an anterior period of

civilization. the carpet be of the aggressive kind, with old fashioned

impossibly-color rose. there be an inlaid round table, much too

big for the room, jut a long way out of one corner; the piano be

worn and old-fashion, the chair be evidently relic of two or

three different suit of furniture. the book be suggestive too--the

“Pilgrim’s progress,” with much gilt on the bind, odd number of

the _Sunday at home_, and the current number of the _Quiver_, two or

three _keepsake_, some little-use volume of miscellaneous poetry,

which look like school-prize, _et cetera_. but the ornament speak

more plainly than anything in the room--large, blue-glass vase on the

mantel-piece, crochet antimacassar, each of a different pattern, over

the chair; and every ornament stand on a wool mat.



he have to wait some time; he hear Mrs. Mansfield go softly past the

door and up the stair, and the small servant follow she with hot
