the door open, and a woman of about forty, dress in rusty black,

much cover by trimming which enhance the shabbiness they be mean

to hide, come in and apologize more than be necessary.



he state the object of his visit as soon as he could. he have come on

behalf of his mother and some other friend of Miss Lane, to find out

her address.



“I could not have give it you myself before this morning,” say Mrs.

Mansfield. “she have write twice to I since she leave Garstone; but

it be only in the letter I receive to-day from she that she put any

address. she be lodge in London by herself, and try to get daily

pupil.”



“be you go to see she?” ask George.



“no, I have no time; she know that herself, and don’t expect I.”



“do you approve of her plan of live by herself? it seem a strange

one for such a young girl.”



“indeed Annie don’t trouble herself about my approval. I cann’t say I

think it a proper thing for a girl to do who have be bring up like

Annie; but she be so obstinate--just like her mother, my poor sister.”



“it be a great pity that she do not consult you more,” say George

deferentially. “have no mother, she ought certainly to defer to you

as her representative.”



“that be just what I say!” cry Mrs. Mansfield, grow confidential.

“I have beg she to come and live here; the house be certainly

small than she be use to, but still it’s a home, and she would be

more comfortable, or she ought to be”--this with some asperity--“among

her own relation.”



“certainly,” say George, with conviction. he have just catch the sound

of child quarrel and scream up-stair, and his thought hardly

go with his word.



“she might go backward and forward to town for her music-lesson from

here quite easily; and why should she not get daily pupil about here

as well as in town, if she have make up her mind to that? then she would

have the comfort of a home to come to in the evening, and she might

amuse herself in her spare time by help I to teach my own child.”



“it would be a delightful arrangement,” say George, with fervor; then,

grow bold--“And, as she be a nice, lady-like girl, I have no doubt

she would soon find a husband among her own friend.”



Mrs. Mansfield shake her head, with her lip draw tightly together.



“I be sorry to say, Mr. Braithwaite, that Annie consider herself too

good for my friend. I don’t wish to say anything against one of my own

blood; but I must say I don’t think such high-and-mighty air become.

it be not as if she be live now as she do when her father be

alive, and when nothing be too good for she.”



“her father be well off, I believe?”



“oh, yes; and, if he have be prudent, instead of spend heap of

money upon her education, he would have leave she a little to live upon!”



“it must be a hard change for she, though. she be so young, and of

course it be so natural to spoil a beautiful girl.”



this rather rash speech cause Mrs. Mansfield to draw herself up.



“well, I cann’t say that I see her beauty myself! I don’t say she be a

bad-look girl; but I don’t think her face be likely to do much for

she: and in my young day gentleman look for something more than a

pretty face in a wife, though to be sure they like a pair of fine eye

too!”



George gather from her manner of say this that she judge she

own vacant, round, bead-like eye to be handsome; and he smile a

compliment, which bring a gratified but not become blush to she

particularly plain face.



before long he succeed in get from her Miss Lane’s address, in

one of the street off Regent Street; and, ponder this choice of a

rather expensive locality, he leave Mrs. Mansfield’s domestic paradise,

and return to town. at his hotel he find the follow telegram:



 “come back at once. Sir George much worse. Harry have return.”



that night he be again at the Grange--not a minute too soon. they tell

he, on his arrival, that his father be not expect to live till

morning, and he go straight up to the sickroom. Harry be there on

his knee by the bedside, very still and grave and unlike himself. Sir

George open his eye as his eld son come in.



“George,” say he, with difficulty, “I have forgive he. don’t let it

be mention again. I cut he out of my will a week ago; it be too late

to alter it. promise I to provide for he.”



“I promise,” say George, in a low voice.



“call the rest. it’s near now.”



and they come one by one softly into the room. an old hound, a great

favorite of his, slip in too, slunk up to the bed, and wag his

tail at the master he have miss for day.



“Hallo, Diamond, come to say good-bye to I?”



and the hound, thus encourage, lick his master’s hand.



“have you forget the old day, Diamond? they be over for I as well

as for you now, my old beauty!” then, gather a remnant of strength,

he give a ringing “view-halloo!”



the hound bound away in great excitement among the silent figure in

the room, then come back, and once more lick his master’s hand. but

he get no answer caress, for the hand be still forever.



the day which follow between Sir George’s death and the funeral be

an awkward time for Harry and his eld brother. the young purposely

hold aloof, and avoid any private conversation with the present head

of the family. only once do George catch he alone, and instantly take

advantage of the opportunity.



“don’t go,” say he, lay his hand on the arm of his brother, who

be go to leave the stable as he enter it. “I have be wait

for a chance to speak to you. our father leave your future in my hand,

you know,” he add, in a tone which, if he choose, the other might take

as a warning.



“well, what be it?” ask Harry, impatiently.



“don’t be so fidgety. it be nothing unpleasant. I only want to know if

you can tell I where to find the Mainwarings’ late governess, Miss

Lane?”



“and you say you have nothing unpleasant to say! I call it

unpleasant--confoundedly unpleasant--to ask I such a question! as if I

have anything to do with Miss Lane! what do you want to know for?” his

manner change from sullen to fierce with this question.



“your manner be a little inconsistent. if you know nothing about she,

why be you so angry when I ask you if you do?”



“I don’t care to be put through my catechism. you ask more question

than my father do.”



“then he speak to you about this matter?”



“what if he do?”



“and you tell he the truth?”



“yes, the truth. I swear it! but I be not bind to answer your

question, and I willn’t. take your hand off my arm; do you hear?”



“only one question. when you have answer it, I willn’t bother you

again. do you know where Miss Lane live?”



a light suddenly come into his brother’s eye, and he answer readily:



“I haven’t the least idea where Miss Lane live; I swear it!”



his brother take his hand sharply off his arm and turn away. he

think it be a lie; but he have no mean of extract the truth. he

be more interested in Miss Lane than the young guess, more anxious

for the interview he be about to seek with the prim little girl than

he have ever be before about a meeting with a woman.



he have to keep his impatience in check until the funeral be over; but

on the very day after, the young baronet go up to town and to the

address Mrs. Mansfield have give he.



“be Miss Lane at home?” he ask of the servant who open the door.

CHAPTER VI.

CHAPTER VII.

CHAPTER VIII.





Annie do not find life at the Elms such a miserable affair as she

have expect. that first evening the key-note be strike of the

conduct of each member of the family toward she. Lady Braithwaite

continue to treat she with distant coldness, or affect to ignore she

entirely. lilian follow suit, except at odd moment of capricious

good humor, when she would treat she like a pretty child to be

tease and caress. George be kind, but instinct make her shun

_tête-à-tête_ with he. she do not see much of Wilfred,

who use to tell she that she make he ashamed of himself and promise

when on his return home he go straight to the sideboard and mixed

same state as usual.



Stephen scarcely speak to she. she soon find out that his admiration

a toy. for Lilian be a headstrong, willful girl, more difficult to

manage than her mother and brother guess.



she have commission to give her cousin which nobody else know of,

“yes, you be a dear, dear good boy, and my good--friend--in the

world.” then more quickly. “just let I finish read this, there’s a

dear, kind fellow!”



there be a pause, and a heavy sigh from the cripple. then Lilian speak

again more brightly:



“now, I can thank you as you deserve. I feel as happy as a bird, and

all thank to you,” she add, caressingly.



but Stephen be sullen.

infernal letter! I wish I have die before I bring it to you!”

same person, all under cover to you?”

sacred to I--they be, indeed, and, if I be to do that, you would

cast I aside like that. what more can I do for you than I have

and then they laugh themselves off the gate.



for this be how the _regime_ of coldness and neglect on the part

see such a lovely woman. for Lilian have take the utmost pain with

the fairness of her complexion. she habitually despise ornament, and

could afford to do so; but to-night a few spray of white azalea and

white heath and delicate maiden-hair fern relieve the somber dress,

and a very small bunch of azalea and fern be fasten by a gold-head

pin in her chestnut hair. and Annie see the girl’s face flush when they

hear the dining-room door open and the gentleman’s voice across the

hall; but when they all enter the room, Colonel Richardson come, in a

few minute, not to that seat near the conservatory, but to the piano,

and tell Annie that Schubert be his favorite composer. for it be a

song from the “Schwanengesang,” arrange for the piano, that she be

play.



Annie look up with irrepressible surprise that he should recognize

it. she be so used to an audience who consider all music above the

level of Offenbach as a not unpleasant noise that her face beam with

pleasure at his very simple remark.



“I will play you another--my favorite,” say she.



and, in her delight at be with an appreciative listener, she play

well than usual, and at the end look up naively for his approval.

he give it without stint; and she go on from these to other favorite

piece, which she know well enough to be able to talk at the same time.



“you must lead an isolated life here, I should think, with no one to

talk to?”



“so I don’t talk,” say she, smile; “I run wild in the field with

William.”



“do you like the life?”



“yes and--no. I like it when I don’t think. I like walk so far and

run so fast and jump over so many ditch that I be too tired at

night to do anything but long for bed-time.”



“but you cann’t pass all your life like that.”



“that be the bad of it. I hate the thought of come back to

semi-civilization when I be too old for my savage pastime.”



“you use to write a little, I think you tell I. have you give it up!”



“quite. I could never make a great author now, and nothing less would

content I.”



he smile; there be something of the simplicity of a child about

this matron. to be a great author one have but to wish it and to be

unmarried. and he linger about the piano a long time, discuss

author and authorship, and now and then hazard a remark make

expressly to bring the indignant fire into her eye and some speech to

her pretty lip piquant in its severity.



at last Lilian could bear it no long; she rise and, with heighten

color, and a dangerous light in her eye, walk to the piano.



“willn’t you sing something, Annie?”



her sister-in-law at once comply, and, before she have finish the

first verse, Lilian have divert the colonel’s attention from all but

herself. the song end, Annie rise, and, her cheek still flush with

the excitement of play she good, slip into the cool conservatory,

murmur the last word of her song still softly to herself. she have

not be there two minute before George join she.



“you don’t mind smoke, Annie, do you?”



“no; besides, I be go back into the drawing-room.”



“don’t go yet. it be much nice out here. and Harry have a quarrelsome

fit on and would disgust you.”



that instantly check her step. Harry’s burst of childish petulance

be among her great trial. she turn with an impatient sigh again

to the flower.



“you play beautifully to-night, much well than you ever play for

any of we.”



“Colonel Richardson understand music.”



“while we understand only drink and fighting; that be what you mean,

ben’t it?”



“oh, no, it be not! you understand a great many thing which I know

nothing about--how to tease a person to death, for instance,” say she,

with weary petulance.



“that be unkind,” say George, quietly. “never mind; I willn’t reproach

you now, when you be tired and excited by your own playing.”



she look up at he with some surprise.



“it be astonishing that such a boor as I should have notice

that, ben’t it, and that I should know the difference between the

half-mechanical playing of pretty tune and music full of passion and

feeling, like that you give Colonel Richardson to-night.”



“I do not know you like music,” say she, in a low, troubled voice.



“you never take the trouble to inquire; do you? but even among the

‘semi-civilized’--to quote some word I hear you use to-night--there

may be capability for something well, may there not?”



Annie hang her head in confusion. he speak quite gently, and look

down at she as if he be hurt, not angry.



“I be sorry--I speak without think,” she say, in an unsteady voice.

“you be right; I be very tired, and that make I cross and--and

foolish. but I willn’t play mechanically to you again. I will find out

what you like good, and learn to play that as well as I possibly can;

and I’m so sorry you be hurt by my rude speech!”



she hold out her hand to he, to see whether he have forgive she; he

take it, hold it in the warm pressure of his, and finally kiss the

little finger two or three time before let they go.



“you be a dear little creature, and I should like you to insult I

every day for the pleasure of forgive you. but that be too much to

hope for; you willn’t do more than ignore I.”



“be that fair? you pretend to forgive I, and then bring another

accusation against I in the same breath,” protest Annie, who do,

indeed, habitually avoid _tête-à-tête_ with he, but who, as usual,

once bring to bay, be perfectly at her ease and able to defend

herself.



“well, I think I have well state all my grievance at once, as

I know it will be a long time before you give I another chance.

seriously, it give I great pain to see you sit silent in my house

or slip through the room like a snub and neglect child, only

wake up into life and brightness when you be out of sight of--those

who be long to see you happy.”



the tear be in her eye. she be touch by the kindness of his

word; but how could she tell he that his own mother and sister cast,

by their coldness, a chill upon she from which, in their presence, it

be impossible for she to escape?



“I will try to be more cheerful,” say she humbly, and rather dismally.



“no, that willn’t do,” declare George, impatiently. “I don’t want you to

pump up liveliness that you don’t feel, or laugh when you feel inclined

to cry.”



“then what do you want I to do?”



“well, when anything amuse you, and you look stealthily at William

with a perfectly stolid face but a laugh in your eye, will you look at

I, too? I can enjoy a joke as well as he.”



“do you notice that?” say Annie, wonderingly.



“yes; you exaggerate my dullness enormously. now, will you promise to

share the joke with I?”



“but William be only a boy. if I be to laugh with you as I do with

he, Harry would think himself shunt and be horribly unpleasant,

as usual. I don’t mean to say anything against Harry,” she add,

hastily. “he be your brother----”



“do you think I feel so tenderly toward he, that I cannot hear a word

of truth about he?” say George passionately. “do you think I cherish

any deep affection for the brute who first rob I of the treasure I

would have die to win, and then neglect she, crush the brightness

out of her youth by his boorish ignorance, insult and disgust she

by his taste and habit?”



Annie be frighten by his vehemence--move too in spite of herself.

he see this, and seize his advantage.



“Annie,” say he, bend down over she with his handsome face full of

passionate tenderness, “it be too late now; but don’t you care for I

a little once?”



with a long sob breath which be almost a cry, Annie bend her head

instinctively to hide her face, and, spring away before he could

detain she, go back into the drawing-room.



Sir George draw himself up again to his full height, and mechanically

put his long-since-extinguish cigar to his lip. he be answer.









CHAPTER IX.





the next day Colonel Richardson go to Scotland, after take a very

warm farewell of Annie, who, so far as she herself be concern, be

that very afternoon she have go into the library to amuse herself

“what be you do among my book?” he ask, with severity.



“I don’t wonder you be astonish to see any one read they,” say

she, look down saucily, with her dull discovery open in her hand.

“I be sure you couldn’t read this, at any rate. it be call ‘extract

read when we be youngster by let we have these entertain

and irreligious parent be always very particular about what their

child read on Sunday?”



“but I be too old to be cure in that simple manner. find I something

nicer, please.”



“come down, then, and sit by the fire, and I’ll find you ‘Clarissa

Harlowe,’ or something else as light and frivolous.”



she come down and sit in the chair he draw on to the hearth-rug, while

after that day she avoid he no long, but treat he with gracious

principle.



Lilian’s coldness to she have grow into more open dislike since Colonel

“Mr. Falconer have propose for I, and George insist on my accept

Mr. Falconer be a rich gentleman of about forty, who have pay Lilian

“yes, he can, practically. the money that ought to have be mine, of

“but, Lilian, that be nonsense! you be the most beautiful girl in the

“do you think I don’t know at the first moment of see you with

“and what if he be?” ask the girl, quietly. “I like he well enough

“soon after Christmas!” echo Stephen, in a low voice.

“yes. and, as it be perhaps the very last service you will ever be able

“it be a very bad service I be do you, Lily. if George be really

“oh, the sense of honor be not so keen as you imagine in our family!”

but he wouldn’t risk hang on your account or mine.”



“but what do you want to say to Colonel Richardson?”



“I want to tell he to come and say good-bye to I before he go away,

“but how can you----”



“he must come on Christmas Day, in the evening. you know how my

leave their society, and I will meet Colonel Richardson at the gate

Beckham.”



“you would risk that? think what you be do, Lilian. Colonel

“he will put himself in peril too, with my wild brother about; so

“Lily, be you mad? I will not help you to do this.”



“very well, then; I’ll risk it without your help--post my own letter,

believe I be mad, I be so miserable!”



“and all for a man who don’t appreciate you, who like Annie well

“it be not true,” say she fiercely. “if I believe that, she should

“I will serve you to the end, for good or for evil, as I have always

Mr. Falconer marry you or somebody else, my wretched life will be

no good to I, and I don’t care how soon I lose it. no one will ever

worship you as I do, Lily, nor for so little thank.”



but she soothe he with sweet word and kind eye. she do indeed feel

the strength of his devotion, and, moreover, he be too useful an ally

not to be worth a few kind speech.



so the letter be send, and the answer come--and the secret be safe.



since the regular hunting-season have begin, Harry’s neglect of his wife

Wilfred turn to his brother.



“why the dicken do you badger the boy like that? he’ll only go and

Wilfred look at his brother keenly.



“I say, George, you’re not play square.”



“yes, I be; you don’t know the game;” and the baronet lounge out of

Dinner that evening begin quietly enough. there be a lull in the

most brilliant mood, talk, laugh, tease her eld brother, and

“ask Annie how she like it,” suggest George.



the young wife do not look up; but all could see that a shiver pass

when they at last come into the drawing-room after dinner, after

“Annie, stop where you be!”

“go, dear--quick!”

“don’t, don’t! I cann’t bear any more!” she say, hoarsely.

“my darling, it be not your brutal husband. don’t you know who it be?”

“I frighten you, Annie! I would not hurt a hair of your beautiful head

“I be much well. let I go, George, please. willn’t you do what I ask

Annie gather all her strength and give one ring cry:



“Harry!”



the bare branch of the shrubbery-tree rustle and crack as a

“thank Heaven! Colonel Richardson!”



George look at he, too, dumb with surprise. but his eye see what

CHAPTER X.





Careless of herself and her own secret, in the burn desire

to be revenge upon Annie, Lilian speed back to the house, not

know that George have see she, and find Harry with the rest

in the billiard-room, still quarrel hotly about the scene in

forbid by she to leave the house that night, and he have be

torture with anxiety on her account ever since he see Annie go into

the conservatory, and then notice a few minute later that George have

also disappear.



Lilian beckon Harry imperiously out of the room.



“I have something important to say to you.”



her wide, glisten eye, pant bosom, and resolutely subdue

manner, check his oath at this interruption. he follow she into

the hall.



“George and Colonel Richardson be in the garden, in the copse at the

bottom, quarrel over your wife. I be sorry if I have startle you;

but I think you have well know.”



“she be the blight of my life,” hiss out Harry, with a bitter

“yes.”

 “MY DEAR ANNIE,--I ought not to have to write to you at all as a

CHAPTER XI.





the first thing Annie have do on arrive at her London lodging have

be to take off her wedding-ring and hide it away in a corner of her

desk. she have give to the landlady the name “Miss Langton,” which she

have resolve to adopt for the future. these be her first step toward

cut herself off from her past life; the next be a bold one.



during these long week when she have lain ill in bed, she have ponder

in her mind how she could live when she have leave her husband, as she at

the very beginning of her illness determine to do. one trial of the

life of a governess have be enough for she, and she could not easily

have re-enter it except in some sort under false pretense. besides,

now that she have throw herself upon her own resource, and stand once

more alone in the world, her old ambition have awaken within she, the

old spirit cry out, the vague but strong consciousness of untried

power turn her thought to a career of art. one form of art alone

seem open to she--the stage. all that she know, or almost all that

she know, of a theatrical life be distasteful to she, and her instinct

would have lead she to give herself up to writing. but she have already

try that, know how hard it be even to get a hearing from the reading

public, and cast aside the thought of literary distinction as take

too long to win.



of course, know nothing about the stage, she fall into the common

error of think that talent make itself more quickly manifest there,

and utterly ignore the fact that it be about as easy for a woman of

high principle, without either money or interest, to attain a good

position in a London theater as for a drummer-boy to become a general.

she know she would have to wait and to work before she find her way to

the front rank; but how long that weary waiting would last, or how dull

that work would be, she have not the least idea. she have unbounded faith

in herself, she have energy, a little patience, and she believe herself

to have talent, and her heart beat fast with the thought that she be

now free to measure her strength against the world.



as for the horror of her husband and the rest of the Braithwaites, if

they ever come to hear of the step she have take, why, she do not care

for their opinion, and their disgust could not humiliate she. besides,

the fact of she having become an actress would effectually cut she off

from they forever and prevent their try to bring she back to they, a

possibility too dreadful to be consider calmly.



for, now that they be over, yet still fresh in her mind, the trial

she have suffer during those few month of married life seem, in

these first day of relief from they, even great than they have really

be. Harry seem more brutal, more ignorant, more dissipated, Lady

Braithwaite and Lilian more coldly insolent, George more selfish,

wilfre more drunken, Stephen more unkind; so that the stage hold out

attraction for she in the social oblivion it involve which it would

have be far from have in her eye in other circumstance.



not once do the thought occur to Annie that she be do wrong in

thus leave her husband without consult he. from the first she

herself of an intolerable burden in the simple manner. she have try

hard to do her duty as a wife, and have succeed only in exasperate

customary excess. in leave he free she think she be render

with a throb of joy, that she be once more able to indulge in her old

dream of ambition and success.



but in this argument with herself she forget one thing--namely, that

she have not leave Harry free. this forgetfulness be the natural result

she to depreciate her duty as they have depreciate her right. it do

the affection be no long possible; for she look upon he as a

brute incapable of any but the low form of love. as for herself,

she do not think herself in danger--she be of cool temperament and

high intellect; her imagination take fire much more readily than her

heart; she have throw herself into the prospect of a brilliant career,

and the idea of lead a loveless life have few terror for she at

first, except in rare moment of depression.



but, though the future be full of charm for she, the present be not

without great difficulty. how be she to enter upon her new life? she

remember that some year ago, in the old day when her father be

alive, when she be still a school girl and theatrical matter have the

charm of mystery, she have be with her father on one occasion when he

very much wish herself out of the group of shabby man--whom she

mistook for actor--with whom she be wait, when at last the manager

come. as his eye fall on she, she step forward, hold his letter

and give her maiden name.



as she have expect, he have long since forget she; but he ask she

to follow he up-stair, and give she a courteous hearing at the back

the rehearsal, after a few night of a suffocating dressing-room and a

draughty stage, she would appear no more, cure of her unfortunate whim.



the rehearsal be a hard trial, certainly. to stand about for three

to be a great actress when nature intend she for a good washer-woman.

insignificant muffle up figure.



the piece be a failure, and do not run long; but she do duty again

in answer she get the follow letter:

 need not be afraid of anybody see your letter. for when I get it

 at Moss’ I take it straight back and down to the willow-pond. I read

 it, and fasten it under the lining of my hunting-cap. so its all

 rigt. there be a shindy when they new you be go. George go to

 your aunt and first he scold mother and Lil and say they ought to

 be ashamed of themselves and your aunt dident know where you be.

 and Harry you should have see he go on. you would have think he

 be a good husbend and you a bad wife if you hear he. he have be

 to London and sell his hunting-wach and bougt you a dimand ring which

 I think you would have like but of course you be write to go away

 and I say so and he punch my head and I punch he back. so he

 dident get much good by his interferring with I. they think I new

 where you be and I say if they think I do they migt just try to

 make I say thats all. so they lissene to reason and Harry drink

 more than ever he be as bad as Wilfred evry bit. and he be allway

 hang about Green’s forge now. Susan Greens come back a pretty thing

 for a man marry like he be now. I only tell you this beco I think

 you ought to know be his wife which be a great pity. they none of

 they know you will never come back except Lil who say you willnot and

 that make George very angry and one evening make Harry cry like a

 great baby inste of try to find you. the place be beesly now you

 be go and if I wasent go to uncle Geralds in Ireland I thing I

 should have to come and dig you out.



  “your afecsionate brother-in-law,



                                       “WILLIAM FITZPATRICK BRAITHWAITE.



 “P.S.--If you could see the black and white rabit now I think you

 would laugh for his leg be alright but so stiff that he hop bout as

 if he be make of wood. Jo bite the pup tail off a fortnight ago.”



this letter make Annie thoughtful. the Rubicon be pass now; she

could not have go back, even have she wish to do so, with what

they would have consider the contamination of the stage upon she.

but what William say about Harry cause she to ask herself for the

first time whether she have not do he wrong, whether she ought

not still to have stay and continue coldly to fulfill her wifely

duty to the letter, whether there have not be more selfishness than

self-sacrifice in give he back his liberty. she feel not one whit

more of affection for the drunken lad who have become the ardent admirer

of the blacksmith’s daughter, but this last fact be too significant

not to awaken her self-reproach. she feel at the bottom of her heart an

unacknowledged gladness that it be no long in her power to go back,

and in the care of her present life she soon forget again those of she

past.



for the few shilling she receive for her work at the theater be

not enough to pay her modest expense for food and lodge without her

draw upon the small sum she have bring with she from the Grange;

William’s money she have resolve not to touch except in case of utmost

need. so she try her strength by live too simply, while she pass,

in spite of herself, at the theater as a “rich” lady, who “come behind”

for a freak. she have clothing enough to last for some time, and before

the end of the summer she be lucky in be able to sell a short

story; and then, after be for a few week out of work and in debt,

and almost in danger of absolute want, she get an engagement at a

salary which be just enough to live upon, but with no chance of more

than a few line to speak.



and this be her life, with now and then a hope of something well

to do, follow always by disappointment and sometimes by despair for

nearly three year, at the end of which time she be still appear

at a fashionable comedy theater, where she have be figure in the

programme for some month on the last line of the list of character,

thus--“Maid, Miss Langton.”



and the brilliant future she have picture once for herself seem

far away than ever. for she have by this time master some of the

secret of success on the stage. the high success, she still know,

fall only to the high talent; and this belief, which be directly

against the creed of most of her companion, she hold to the end. it

be all luck, they say. it be chiefly luck, she think too--the luck

of be somebody’s son or somebody’s daughter, of have good look

and bad principle or wealthy friend, of be by chance on the right

spot at the right time; and luck have be against she.



Disappointment, too, and weary, weary waiting have take the bloom off

her beauty, which be of a type depend very much on expression; and

the look her face habitually wear now be that of a woman whom care

and failure and struggle with necessity have reduce to an automaton.

yet in some respect her position would not have be an unenviable one

to a less ambitious woman. the conscientious care which have formerly

make she a good governess, and later an almost too submissive wife to

her careless husband, make she now fill she very unimportant _role_

with an attention to the most trifling detail which obtain for

she the consideration of the authority in the theater, although it

be of course not possible that her effort to be artistic in her

representation of monosyllabic maid should attract the attention of

the general public or of the critic in front. and her salary, though

not high, be now sufficient to keep she in comfort, which might have

be great, have she be more economical. so that the privilege of

think herself a martyr be almost out of her reach.



she have not quite give up hope, though it be no long join to

bright confidence in ultimate success, when a small part be intrust

to she which enable she to show unmistakable sign of talent. it be

such a very small part, and it would so undoubtedly have improve the

piece from a dramatist’s point of view to cut out the scene it be in

altogether, that the critic take no notice, and the public do not

seem impressed. but it draw the attention of her companion to she;

and Annie, with her heart beat wildly, overheard more than one

prediction that she would “get on.”



with reawakened ambition, her old high spirit come back to she; the

cloud of cold reserve which close over she in spite of herself when

she be unhappy, disappear, and for the first time Annie find

pleasure in her profession. the society afford at that time by the

theater she be in, be some of the pleasantest in London. it include

man and woman who be among the world’s recognize pet--woman of

beauty and man of wit, handsome actor, and two actress of whom

Europe have acknowledge the genius. Annie feel the charm of this

brilliant circle, which be indeed, as theater so seldom be, as

attractive as the outside world imagine it to be.



she be sit in the green-room one evening, between the act, when

two of the actor come in, discuss the beauty of a lady who sit in

one of the box near to the stage.



“I’m sure I’ve see she in the Park,” say one; “and I’ve be tell she

name; but I forget it.”



“be that her husband behind she--the tall man with the eye-glass?”



“don’t know, I’m sure. should think not.”



the other laugh.



“she be the handsome woman we have have in front for a long time--much

well-look than any of the professional beauty. perhaps she be a

professional beauty--eh?”



“no--too good-look.”



it be the other’s turn to laugh; and, when they be call on to the

stage, they be still criticise the unknown fair one and anxious for

another view of she.



Annie’s curiosity be excited, and, contrary to her custom of devote

her attention entirely to what be go on on the stage, she manage,

on her next appearance to say a few line, to get an opportunity of

look toward the box the two speaker have indicate. and she give

one of the slight, most imperceptible of start, for the lady be

lilian, exquisitely dress and look handsomer than ever. Annie

could not see the face of the man behind she in her glance at the box;

but she be anxious to know who it be, and later in the evening she

be satisfy; for a young actor name Gerald Gibson tell another

in her hearing that the lady be Mrs. Falconer, that he have be to

a dance at her house two night before, and that “the tall man with

the eye-glass,” who be one of the other occupant of the box, be a

Colonel Richardson, who have just return from abroad.



all this fill Annie with excitement and anxiety. have Lilian

recognize she? who be the other people in the box? have Colonel

Richardson really only just return from abroad? these and other

question concern her sister-in-law and the rest of her husband’s

family keep she awake that night in a fever of newly awaken interest

in the Braithwaites. the remembrance of her life at Garstone occupy

she very little now, the long, solitary hour of daylight, when she

be not engage in rehearsal, she fill by write, her old taste for

which have revive to console she for her otherwise monotonous life.

after the exchange of a few letter with William, she have hear no more

from he, and it be now more than two year since she have receive his

last. during all this time no news have reach she, of her husband or

his family. she have say of late bitterly to herself that, if they have

care to do so, they would have find she out long ago, and she have

begin to wonder whether she would ever see any of they again, when this

unexpected, yet most natural event, show she again the one of all the

Braithwaites whom she least care to see.



Annie like Gerald Gibson, as everybody in the theater do--a grave,

quiet, thoughtful-look man, whose reserved manner impress those

around he with respect, even though it be often merely the result of

his have nothing in particular to say. he might have be the son of

a cheesemonger, but he be as perfect a gentleman not only in look and

manner, but in mind, as if he have be the son of a duke. Annie know,

though she have know he only a few week, that she could speak without

reserve to he. on the evening after she have see Lilian, therefore,

she find an opportunity, when they be on the stage together, but not

immediately concern in the business of the scene, of allude to the

beauty who have make such a sensation among they the night before.



“I think I hear you say you be fortunate enough to know she, Mr.

Gibson,” say she, her interest peep out from under the indifferent

word.



“I don’t know she well. I be introduce to she about ten day ago, and

somebody get I a card for an ‘at home’ at her house.”



“she be very beautiful, ain’t she?”



“yes, very, for those who admire massive beauty.”



“then don’t you admire she?”



“yes; but I have see woman I admire more.”



“I don’t like such frosty enthusiasm. be she nice, pleasant, amiable?”



“I don’t think ‘amiable’ be quite the word for that type of woman. but

she be very brilliant, very charming.”



“I use to know she once before she be marry,” say Annie in a low

voice. “I be glad to hear she be happy.”



“I be scarcely able to judge of that. lady act so well, even when

they be not on the stage, and they be often charming when at heart

they be very miserable; so the novelist say.”



“you don’t think she be miserable, do you?” ask Annie, anxiously.



“indeed I have no reason for think so. she seem to have everything

she can want, beauty, wealth, position, a good husband.”



“then Mr. Falconer be nice?”



“he be generally popular, I believe; but I have scarcely see he.”



“ah!” escape suddenly from Annie’s lip. she think those last word

significant.



she could not bring the conversation round to Colonel Richardson now

without excite his suspicion, so she merely ask he not to mention

that she have ever know Mrs. Falconer.



“I wish to remain _perdu_ to my old friend until I have get on--if I

ever do get on,” she add, sadly.



“you will get on, Miss Langton. how can you doubt it!”



“how can I do anything but doubt it? I have wait so long, and seem no

nearer the end.”



“but you must be near the end.”



“ah--but what end?”



she turn away with a little shrug of the shoulder, and his eye

follow she with interest. she be not massive and he find more

attraction in her face than in those of all the professional beauty.



a few evening later, as he be leave the theater when his share of

the performance be over, he see Miss Langton in front of he walk

down the quiet street where the stage-door be. a gentleman stand

on the opposite pathway cross over and raise his hat to she. Gerald

Gibson see she start, stop, hesitate, and finally put out her hand.

Gerald pass they, but neither notice he; and he recognize the

gentleman as Colonel Richardson, whom he have meet at Mrs. Falconer’s.



“that be the reason of her interest in Mrs. Falconer then!” think

Gerald.









CHAPTER XII.





Gerald Gibson have not go many yard far down the street, after

see the meeting between Miss Langton and Colonel Richardson, when he

be overtake by a fellow-actor, Aubrey Cooke.



“do you see who little Langton be talk to?”



“some friend of her, I suppose. I don’t notice.”



“it be Frank Richardson, the man there be all that scandal about a

few year ago--Lord Berwick’s wife--don’t you remember?”



“well?”



“well, I’m sorry he have get hold of little Langton, that be all.”



“you be sorry without cause, then. Miss Langton be a long way above

his level. she cann’t refuse to speak to he, for he know her people

well year ago.”



with unerring certainty Gerald Gibson have jump to this conclusion.

the other look surprised.



“oh, you know all about it, then? you be the favorite one for whom

Miss Prim open her lip. well, I really be glad to hear it, for she

be the flag I always hold out when old lady tell I there be no

virtuous woman on the stage; and, if she be to go I don’t know where

on earth I should look for another.”



“you be too cynical, Cooke.”



“don’t shy long word at I. if I deserve they it be because I be

lead away to a meeting of the Society for the Mutual Improvement

of the Clerical and dramatic Professions this afternoon. capital

institution--the parson look happy and the pro.’s look good. but

that cann’t last. good-night.”



Aubrey Cooke be not at his good with Gibson; the two man have too

little in common. but he be a clever fellow. he have a plain, silly

face, a bitter tongue, and a manner which find favor with most woman.

he adore woman. those, however, he worship the most deferentially

would scarcely have approve of the manner in which he speak of they

among other man in their absence, for there be a strong dash of young

Paris in his adoration. he be too shrewd to make many mistake; and no

man know well the exact tone in which to address any particular woman

of his large and varied acquaintance. he bear Miss Langton no ill-will

for repeat unmerite snub; the caprice of woman be infinite,

pretty and less prim woman abound, and he could revenge himself

so easily by an epigram--not a slanderous one, but none the less

cutting--in the dressing-room.



when Annie first recognize Colonel Richardson as he cross the road

toward she, her impulse be to walk on; but anxiety to hear something

about the family at the Elms change her intention, and she stop,

shake hand with he, and allow he to walk down the street with she.



“I know you the moment you come on,” say he. “it be a happy thought

to go on the stage; I admire your courage.”



“I don’t think it be courage that send I on; and at present I have

have no reason to congratulate myself on my attempt, I assure you. do

Mrs. Falconer know I?”



“no. she do not care for the piece, and be not pay much attention

to it. she do not know you be on the stage, for she tell I she

think you have become a governess somewhere. you have do well than

that.”



“yes. and the rest of the Braithwaites? have you see any of they since

your return?”



“no; but Mrs. Falconer give a very bad account of some of they.”



“what do she say? tell I quickly, please.”



“it seem there have be quarrel among the brother lately,

about money matter, I believe. Sir George and Harry be the chief

disputant, and Mrs. Falconer never know what the next news about they

may be. but I be pain you----”



“no, no; I want to hear everything. will you tell I all you know about

my husband? be he well? be he no steady?”



“I believe he be well now; but he be ill some month ago.”



“Ill. what be the matter with he?”



Colonel Richardson hesitate.



“you know his habit be rather irregular, and he have ride too much

and excite himself too much, and I believe he be ill from the effect

of overexcitement. but why do you wish to know these thing? you be

happily spare the wrangle and disturbance of that unlucky household

now. you have the interest of your own career to occupy your mind; it

be much well for you not to concern yourself any more with the doing

of that barbaric crew.”



“don’t say that. every word you say make I reproach myself more. I

be not heartless, though I see now how selfish it be of I to sneak

away as I do. you will hardly believe that I think I be do what

be good for my husband as well as myself. I think he be too young

to be burden with a wife. we do not suit each other; I seem to

irritate he to bad brutality; we be spoil each other’s life

and our own.”



“you be quite right to come away. he would only have crush your

life out by his coarse cruelty before now, if you have stay with he.

how could you, with your sensitive feeling and cultivate taste, bear

with that uncouth boor? I use to wonder at your patience with he when

I first know you in town with he.”



“I be wrong, though,” say Annie, gravely. “if I think I could do

he any good I would go back now.”



“I beg you not to do anything so rash,” say Colonel Richardson,

hastily. “your husband be bad than an uncouth lad now; he be a

coarse, savage-temper man. Lilian--Mrs. Falconer--his own sister, be

afraid of he; and you know she be not meek-spirited.”



“what do he say of I? do he never speak about I? do you know?”



“the last time his sister see he he tell she that, if he ever meet his

wife again--and he use language which neither she nor I could repeat

to you--he would ‘crush the beauty out of the face that make a fool of

he.’ forgive my repeat his word to you; I think they will be the

good warning I can give you to keep out of his reach.”



Annie sigh.



“you don’t make I afraid of he; you only make I pity he as I would

a fierce hound who have be unwisely treat. if Harry be to crush my

face, as he say, in a fit of passion, it would be the one thing which

would make he treat I tenderly ever afterward.”



Colonel Richardson look surprised.



“you almost make I bold enough to wonder----”



“why I leave he? I suppose my strong reason really be that he be

unbearable to I. his tenderness be odious as his anger, and bad

than his neglect. I should dislike he more than ever now; but I should

know how to treat he more wisely.”



Colonel Richardson understand woman too well to say more on that

subject. he turn the conversation.



“Mrs. Falconer expect her brother William next week,” he say. “shall

I bring he to the theater and see if he know you?”



Annie catch eagerly at the idea of see her favorite William again.

she have nothing to fear from his knowing where she be, and she be

anxious to find out whether he be grow into a less worthless man

than his brother. he be now eighteen. she be anxious, too, to learn

whether he still retain the affectionate remembrance of she. so she

last word to Colonel Richardson be a repetition of her injunction

to bring he to the theater without any warning that he would see she.

she do not doubt that he would know she, especially as he be the one

member of the family who know she be on the stage.



the season be nearly over now, and night after night she scan the

audience anxiously in the hope of see those two face she know; but

it be not until the very last night of all that, as she come on to the

stage, she see a tall young man in the stall half rise from his seat,

with the exclamation, just loud enough for she to hear--“Annie!”



at the end of the street William meet she, and could hardly be

restrain from embrace she, regardless of appearance. he be

broad, manly in figure; but in his manner to she he be exactly

the same as before. she be thankful to see that he do not look

dissipate, and he hasten to assure she that he have observe all her

command, that he read a great deal and “quite like it.” he have not

live much at Elms, having pass most of his time with his uncle, his

mother’s brother, in Ireland.



“and, Annie, I’m not go to lead an idle life. I’m go to be a

soldier.”



“well, that be the next thing to it.”



“no disrespect to the army, I beg, madam. it be very hard work to get

in at all nowadays. no Braithwaite ever have to study so much before

as I shall have to do to pass the exam. I’m sure to be ‘pluck’ the

first time, of course, and very likely the second. I must get through

the third time, you know, or else it will be all up with I.”



“you must get through the first time,” say Annie indignantly. “if you

don’t, I will never speak to you again.”



“oh, yes, you will. if I don’t pass, you will have to console I, and,

if I do pass, you will congratulate I. oh, Annie, I wish I have be

old enough to marry you, or that you have marry George, so that you

might come back to the Elms again.” no suggestion that she should go

back to Harry, however. Annie look up at he quickly.



“how be Harry? he be not anxious for my return, I suppose?”



“oh, to think of your be his wife be intolerable! he be not worthy

to look at you. sometimes he be sorry, in a maudlin sort of way, that

he cann’t see you, and complain that you have desert he, and that

you be the only woman he ever care about. but that be all nonsense,

and he say it only when he be drunk. he drink bad than Wilfred.

and a few month ago----Well, never mind that! you mustn’t trouble your

head any more about he.”



Annie listen in silence, her heart ache with remorse. she know

well enough now that she have do irretrievable wrong in leave she

husband, whom at least she could, at the entire sacrifice of herself,

have keep from this. but it be too late now, she tell herself. if she

return to he now unbidden, with the feeling of repulsion toward he

a thousand-fold strong than ever, she could not expect a welcome, she

could not even repress the disgust she feel.



she tell William that she be go to leave town and travel with a

theatrical company, to gain experience in well part than she could

hope to play in London yet. he walk all the way home with she, and,

look at she gravely as he stand say the last word to she, he

complain that she be thin and pale.



“do you know, Annie, you be so much altered I should hardly have know

you. you have lose all your pretty color, and your eye be not half so

bright as they use to be. it be all that beast Harry, make you have

to work for your living!” he break out, passionately. “he deserve to

be kick!”



“come, be reasonable, William; that be not Harry’s fault. woman must

expect to ‘go off’ in look, you know, as they grow old.”



“but you be not old. that be nonsense.”



“I be two-and-twenty. when you last see I, I be not nineteen.”



“well, you ought not to have change so much in less than three year.

never mind,” add he affectionately, see that his word seem to

depress his sister-in-law--“I love you just as much as ever; and you

will soon get back your color when you get out of London and forget all

about Harry again.”



and he kiss she and bade her good-bye most unwillingly; for the

follow morning he have to go back to the Elms, to see George about

the expense of a “coach” to cram he for the examination he would have

to go through.



Annie go up-stair to her room--she could afford to have a

sitting-room now--feel ashamed of the pain his remark upon she

look have give she. it be a fact she have know for a long time now,

that her beauty have fall off, so that there be barely trace of

it leave. a thin, brown face, without a tinge of pink in the cheek,

and with scarcely more than a tinge in the lip, eye from which the

brightness of hope and joy have go, and a weary, wear expression,

be what less than three year of lonely work and disappointment have

leave of her youthful prettiness. no woman, and especially an actress,

can suffer the sense of lose beauty to be suddenly bring home to she

without a pang, and Annie’s vanity be strong enough to make her cry at

William’s evident regret.



“perhaps Harry himself would not know I,” she think to herself, “and

would be disgusted if I be point out to he as his wife.”



so she cry herself to sleep.



when William arrive at the Elms next day, he be even less inclined

than usual to meet his brother Harry on friendly term. for he look

upon the latter as be the cause of Annie’s exile--so he choose to

consider she voluntary flight--and therefore as the cause also of all

her struggle and the terrible alteration in her look. so the lad

avoid his brother as much as he could until dinnertime, when there

be no help for their come in contact with each other, as their

place be set side by side. an unlucky accident bring the name of

the half-forget wife into the conversation. Wilfred rally his

young brother, who have not be at the Elms for some time, upon

be “so confoundedly abstemious.”



“one would think little Annie be still here read you sermon

across the table with her pretty eye,” say he.



the blood rush to the lad’s face, for Harry utter an oath at the

mention of his wife.



“I wish we have never frighten the dear little thing away,” Wilfred

go on, in a maudlin manner. “she be our little bit of righteousness.

it make I take to bad course, she go away do.”



this be not a happy speech, and it be follow by a minute’s silence

on the part of all three of his brother; Stephen be not there.



“why don’t you hunt she up, Harry?” go on Wilfred, who either wish

to irritate his brother or have less tact than usual. “I wouldn’t let

my wife leave I in the lurch, if I have one, and go tramp about all

over the world, amuse herself without I.”



“she may go to the deuce for what I care, if she ben’t go already!”

burst out Harry.



William clinch his fist and try to keep still. the injured husband

go on:



“a little, sly, vagabond governess, glad enough to entrap a gentleman

into marry she, and then cut away and bring disgrace upon his

name!”



“Disgrace!” cry William, turn with flash eye upon his brother.

“as if any wife could disgrace you! as if Annie, who be a thousand

time too good for you to black her shoe, could have any bad

disgrace than to be your wife!”



“you hold your tongue, you young cub!” say his brother, doggedly. “I

say she don’t deserve a decent husband.”



“well, she don’t get one”--this from Wilfred.



“she don’t deserve a decent husband, and she couldn’t be expect to

stay in a respectable house.”



“what respectable house?”--Wilfred again.



Harry go on without notice the interruption.



“it be natural that her vagrant instinct should get the well of she

again, and she should take the first chance of go off on the tramp.”



“you infernal liar!” shout William, too much excited to be careful.

“she be no more a tramp than you be. and, as for her ‘vagrant

instinct,’ you stupid ass, they have lead she into much well society

than she would ever have get into with you at her heel!”



all the other be startle, and William check himself as he be

go to say more. Harry bring a rough hand down on his shoulder.



“so you be in the secret, be you? come now, out with it; where be

she?”



“out of your reach, luckily for she.”



“yes, but you be not, unluckily for you!” say Harry, thickly, rise

to his foot and stand threateningly over his brother, not heed

Sir George’s voice cry, “sit down!”



“now, then, where be she?”



William thrust away his chair and face his tipsy brother steadily.



“I would not help to put she in your power again by tell you where

to find she, even if I know, if you be to tear I to piece!”



he step aside quickly to avoid the lunge Harry make at he, and leave

the room.



“Bravo, young un!” say Wilfred.



the baronet afterward try gentler and subtler mean to find out

Annie’s hiding-place from the lad; but William keep the secret safely.



meanwhile, the fugitive wife be prepare for a new experience. she

have, as she have tell William, resolve upon leave London for awhile,

hope that practice in the country might mature her talent and enable

she at the end of a few month to take a high position than she could

aspire to at present. she know very well that, once out of London, it

would be by no mean easy to get back; but the feeling that she be

advance no far, and could not hope to advance far without

more experience, prevail over every other; and she think herself

fortunate in get an engagement, in a travel company, just about

to start on tour, to play second part in old comedy. it be not go

to what be consider the good town in a theatrical sense; but it

be a good company, and Annie have hear that one of the actor of the

theater she have just leave would be in it too.



she have hear Gerald Gibson speak of go into the country, and have

come at once to the conclusion that he must be the actor allude to;

she be very glad of this, for he be one of her favorite.



when, however, she get on to the stage of the theater which have be

engage for their rehearsal, which be as dark as most stage be

in the day-time, she see no face she know among the people assemble

there, except that of the manager who have engage she.



“I think you say I should meet one of my late companion,” she

remark to he when he shake hand with she.



“yes, Mr. Cooke be here somewhere,” he answer.



“oh, Mr. Cooke!” she echo, in a tone of evident disappointment.



now Aubrey be stand in the shadow only a few foot away from she. he

be always particularly quiet when he be not remarkably noisy, and,

have nobody to talk to at the moment, he have be still as a statue,

and have hear every word of this short colloquy, and notice the tone

devote his attention to she during the tour.



but, after this unconsciously administer rebuff, he have to resort

to the other alternative--of bask in the more easily win smile of

great, win, velvety brown eye shade by long, black lash, and

that she be very badly dress, apparently in odd and end from her

stage wardrobe.



they be rehearse “she Stoops to Conquer,” and Miss West play Miss

Hardcastle, while Annie herself be Miss Neville. Annie discover

the rest of the woman be unintereste. there be a common but clever

the manager’s niece.



on the day of the last rehearsal, before the tour begin, Aubrey Cooke

“I beg your pardon,” say he shyly--Aubrey be very shy sometimes--“I

“oh, yes! she be suggest that we should lodge together, as it be so

“she mean to be kind, I have no doubt,” mumble Aubrey. “but I don’t

then, of course, after that you would know all about everybody, and

Annie have scarcely time to thank he for his advice before he have

lead lady’s beauty show a difference of taste between they which

CHAPTER XIII.





before the company Annie have join start on a tour, she have hear

“oh! do you know she too?”

“I can guess which side you take.”

handsome panther-woman of the lady novelist.”



“I expect something more complimentary than that. I don’t call that

advantage over most of my rival in one respect--my appearance. I hear

stupid. she be ask a man name Colonel Richardson who I be.”



“Colonel Richardson?”



“yes. he be a gentleman whom I always meet at Mrs. Falconer’s, a very

old friend of the family, I believe.”



now Aubrey Cooke have note well, without appear to remark it, the

Annie, therefore, choose to live alone, the only girl of her own

Aubrey Cooke have room above Annie’s in this house, and, on reach

Annie meanwhile have be prowl about.



“do you think you could open the kitchen window, Mr. Cooke?” she ask,

it be a small window, just low enough for he to reach the fastening

“what have you do?” ask Annie, anxiously, as he appear again,

“I’ve fall into a lot of thing, it seem,” say he, “and I believe

I’ve sprain my ankle.”

“I’m afraid it be do for now,” answer Mr. Cooke, as he remove

you a chair to help you up.”

“it be one of Mrs. Briggs’ prehistoric chicken, and it would want a

so they hunt again, and, not find anything but bone and Mr.

“the baker’s wife lend I this; so now we can have fifteen course

however, the coffee be make at last, and of course Aubrey say it be

“then we shall have to wash they up.”

he be suit the action to the word when Annie stop he.

he do not want to break up this _tête-à-tête_, in which Annie, for the

“there be once a very dreadful ogre as bad as he be ugly--he have a

“I be laugh at something I be think about, Mr. Cooke. you cann’t

“be this the way to treat two member of a profession which number

“I be sure, sir, I be very sorry. but you look pretty comfortable

“and so we be, Mrs. Briggs--at least, I be. as for Mr. Cooke, some

and she run away laugh to her sitting-room, while Aubrey go

after that afternoon in Mrs. Briggs’ kitchen, Miss Langton and Mr.

with which dark suggestion Aubrey blow out his candle and go to

CHAPTER XIV.





Annie feel half incline at first to request the manager, on the plea

of illness, to let his niece, who be her “understudy,” play her part

for the week the company be to spend at Beckham, and take her chance

of his allow she to rejoin they at the next town they visit. the

incompetent little niece be eager, as Annie know, for such a chance,

and there would probably be little difficulty as far as that part of

the matter be concern.



but, besides the fact that she could ill afford to lose even one week’s

salary and risk the canceling of the rest of her engagement, she feel

sure that there be one person whom the plea of illness would in no

way deceive. Aubrey Cooke’s attention have already be awaken to

her reluctance to visit Beckham, and he be far too sharp a young man

not to be dangerous if she be to give he involuntarily a clew to a

secret she do not want to trust he with.



and the secret of her marriage she wish to keep from all her present

associate. the miserable tie seem to be less binding when all around

she be ignorant of it. for a long time she have almost forget it

in the unfettered life she have lead since she leave Garstone; but the

remembrance of it have begin lately to irritate she strangely. there

be now not even mingle with pity. and she be to be bring against

her will to the very town which he and his brother visit almost

daily.



but, after long reflection, she decide that the risk of her be

round about Beckham, as be usually the case with country town, very

seldom visit the theater--the Braithwaites never. upon William’s

authority, she be so much altered that, with the help of a veil and

other such simple disguise, she might pass unrecognized even by people

among whom she have live. when the young man from the Grange come into

Beckham, they be almost always on horseback or driving, so that it

would be easy for any one on foot to avoid they; and, above all, she

be on the alert to escape they, while they have not the least suspicion

of her come. in the town itself there be very little fear of she

be recognize by the inhabitant. she have not be in it much at

any time, and be very little known there. the mere change of name

would be enough to prevent their identification of “Miss Lane” or “Mrs.

Harold Braithwaite” with “Miss Langton.”



so, when the company arrive at Beckham, Annie be still with they. no

one notice any difference in her manner from her usual rather stolid

there before. she be too wise to deny it when he ask she carelessly

consequence.



Aubrey have not yet find the golden opportunity he seek, for Annie

declare that there be nothing in the least interesting to be see in

seize upon this excuse to decline walk with he. the third day of

their stay be the fifth of November, and a friend of the manager have

which include a bonfire and firework, after the performance. on

the same night, Miss West, the lead lady, have invite Aubrey to

supper, and, on his pleading a previous engagement, she say to he

with some pique and in no very subdue tone that she know whose charm

outweigh those of any society she could offer he, and warn he

emphatically that the pleasure he prefer be far more dangerous

than those he reject.



“your little prude will throw you over some fine morning when you least

expect it. I know what those quiet little woman do. and you willn’t be

able to console yourself so quickly for her defection as I can myself

for your.”



and Miss West march away to bestow the charm of her racy speech and

artistic complexion where they be well appreciate. for indeed

Langton, whose promise presence at the house he be go to visit

that night have more charm for he than firework.



the lady and gentleman who give this entertainment be delighted

with the good nature of Mr. Cooke and the two brother-actor of his

who be present, when they take the rocket and catherine-wheel out

midnight festivity. but the young man certainly condescend to enjoy

fire squib and burn his finger and his clothe with great spirit.

“let I go back and give it just one more toss,” plead she earnestly

authority in small thing very submissively.



“no, I cannot allow it. this jump through the fire be a heathenish

custom highly unbecoming in an enlightened young lady of the nineteenth

century.”



“oh, yes, it mean something, don’t it?” cry she, interested. “the

canaanitish child be pass through the fire to propitiate Moloch.

and I have hear of a lot of irish and german superstition about

bonfire.”



“yes, they be all about luck and love. if you want to see whether your

love will be fortunate, you set a blaze hoop roll down a hill,

and, if it reach the bottom still alight and be not catch by any

obstacle, then you know she love you back.”



“where do you find out that? have you ever try it?” she ask

lightly.



“no,” say he, in a whisper; “I should not dare.”



they be both silent for a moment; the fire have fall into mere smoke

Aubrey stand still, without attempt to follow or detain she. she

could not have misunderstood he, and she shrink away; that be enough

he have count upon she give he a hearing, and this abrupt repulse

sting he to the quick.



he do not stand there long watch the flickering light and shadow

cast by the burn pile in front of he. he spring through the fire

his place as the move spirit of the throng with great zeal than

ever.



and, when they have all grow weary, and have burn their clothe and

do not look at she.



when they break up, between two and three o’clock in the morning, the

“you, Aubrey! thank Heaven!”



“my dear child, who do you think it be?”



“I think it be that tipsy man!” she whisper, shudder.



“the clumsy brute don’t hurt you, my darling, do he, when he run up

think it be he who be run after I, and I be frightened.”



“that be all because you be a silly girl and be too proud to let I

see you home. it be a ‘judgment.’ why, you be shake all over still!

I don’t think you be such a little coward!”



he soothe she tenderly, with a very happy remembrance of her delight

and Aubrey be right. she have feel just what her face express, that

for, in the half tipsy man who have stagger down from Miss West’s door

she walk on with Aubrey very quietly, very silently, her hand on his

“Annie, you do--you will trust yourself to I, willn’t you?”

“good-night, darling!” he whisper back, manage to give one last

he go home to his lodging utterly bewilder, but resolve to get

but the next day, when he call upon she, he be tell Miss Langton be

long she would hear her own cue, and must be on the watch for it.



“good-evening, Miss Langton.”



“oh, good-evening, Mr. Cooke!” she give he her hand; it be tremble

a little, and she do not look up into his face.



“I have not have an opportunity of speak to you before. you will let

I see you home?”



“not to-night; I have promise to go to supper with Miss Norris.”



“you be put I off, I see. be it fair, Annie? be it right? be I

not worth an answer?”



“an answer to what?”



“to what I say to you last night. you cann’t have forget so soon.

if I be a stranger, if I be the most contemptible wretch living,

if you have always treat I with open dislike, you could not have

misunderstood or forget what I say to you last night.”



Annie turn and look up at he, pale under her _rouge_.



“I have not forget, nor understand--at least, I think not. I think

you too would have understand--that I try to avoid you, because I

fear, I know my answer, if I must answer, would give you pain.”



“then you don’t like I?”



a ray of vehement passion flash from her dark eye.



“don’t torture I! you know I like you; but I can’t--I cann’t do more! I

don’t know whether I have do wrong--I never mean to lead you to feel

“why should you avoid I? why should you not love I?”



she do not answer; but there be no mistaking the misery on her face

for coquetry or caprice.



“be you bind by some other engagement, Annie?”



she shudder. before he could speak again, she turn quickly to he.

“do you mean that you will marry for ambition? you, Annie? Wait, wait a

you be only torture I, and all to no end. I cannot marry you; I

cannot love you!”



“you could if you would, Annie. I could make you love I; you be

always happy when you be with I.”



his word move she, and she stop he abruptly.



“Happy? yes, for the time. we have be good friend, that be all. but

there be something more in life than you can give I.”



“what be there?”



“Fame, position, the mean of get on.”



“be that what you care for most?”



“what if it be?”



“it be not; but, if it be, I would get those for you easily enough.”



she laugh, but not merrily.



“I think you overestimate your power.”



Aubrey’s face look in that moment as if carve in wood, save for the

steady shining of his light eye. he say, quietly:



“oh, I do, do I? well, you shall see.”



they be both silent for a few moment, and then Annie hear her cue

and go on.



this conversation take place on a Thursday evening, and during the

next two day Annie avoid Aubrey still, and he do not again seek an

interview with she, but content himself with simple greeting, and

with watch she quite unobtrusively. she miss his companionship

keenly, far too keenly. she do not dare to leave the house all day,

fear as much to meet he as to meet any of the Braithwaites, yet

hold her breath when there be a knock at the front door, in the

hope that he at least have come to ask after she. but he do not come.

on Saturday night, as she be leave the theater, Aubrey come out,

follow by a boy carry his portmanteau. for the first time for

three day, he run after she.



“good-bye, Miss Langton; I be go to town.”



Annie start.



“what! you be go away?”



“only till Monday. I be go on business. you will wish I good luck?”



“with all my heart!”



he wring her hand and run on without a word. they could not trust

themselves to speak again. the next day Annie leave Beckham with the

rest of the company.



on Monday night they meet once more at the theater. Aubrey be look

paler and plainer than usual, and give as a reason for his alter

appearance that he have not be to bed for the last two night.



“may I see you home to-night, Miss Langton?” ask he, as soon as

he find a chance of speak to Annie. “I will not say a word that

could offend you. I will not touch upon the--the forbid topic,” he

whisper, earnestly.



Annie could not refuse; but it be hard work for she to hide she

agitation--and her pleasure--when she once more find he wait for

into similar calmness.



“I think you would like to know how I get on in town,” say he at

once, in the most matter-of-fact tone. “I go up about a London

engagement--at the Regent’s Theater--and I’ve get it!”



“I’m so glad,” say Annie, coolly.



“well, that be not all. I’ve get an offer of an engagement there for

you too.”



“not really?”



“I have, though. I know there be a part in the piece they be go to

play which would suit you down to the ground, so I mention that there

what I know would attract attention about you; and it happen that the

be pretty sure to get it if you write.”



“oh, Mr. Cooke, I don’t know how to thank you!” say Annie, in wild

delight, for more than one reason.



“don’t mention it, Miss Langton,” say Aubrey, in his old, deferential

manner; then he turn the conversation. “I meet an old favorite of

yours last night--Gibson--at Mrs. Falconer’s.”



“oh! how be the beauty?”



“well, she affect great distress about one of her brother, who be

ill, and not expect to live. it appear he fall down as he be

don’t think she be as much afflict as she would be if mourning don’t
