she do not suggest go to nurse he.”



“do she mention the name of the brother?” ask Annie, quite quietly.



“yes; she call he ‘poor Harry.’”



Annie hear without give one sign that the news move she. for the

rest of the walk she speak little, and with an effort. at her door

he be strike by the marked constraint of her manner as she bid he

good-bye. when she have unlock the door and he have turn away, she

say:



“whatever you hear of I, remember I be not ungrateful.”



when Aubrey get to the theater on the follow evening, he find that

the manager’s niece be to play Miss Langton’s part, and learn that

the latter have throw up her engagement and have already leave town.









CHAPTER XV.





the news of her husband’s illness have fall like a knell on Annie’s

ear; for in a moment she see that the bright vision of pleasure and

satisfied ambition which Aubrey’s word about a London engagement in

the same theater with he have call up could not be indulge in,

except at the sacrifice of an unmistakable duty. it be her husband

who lie ill, neglected and solitary. for one moment she try to stifle

conscience by say to herself that she do not know where he be;

but then she feel ashamed of the flimsy excuse, for she could not

doubt that he be at Garstone Grange. Aubrey have say that it be on

Wednesday night that the accident have happen to he, and it be on

Wednesday night that she herself have see and even touch he in the

street of Beckham. she must go to he, and at once, before Aubrey

could guess her secret, before she herself, in an unguarded moment,

should let he know how much this separation would cost she. she dared

not trust herself to think what a great part of the fact of his being

engage at the same theater have have in her joy at the prospect of

play again in London; it be a dangerous subject, and she shun it

instinctively. she try to keep her thought fix on this one simple

idea--she must go to Garstone, nurse her husband through his illness,

bear his brutal temper and thankless snub as well she might, and then

slip back quietly into her free stage life once more, take her chance

of get a town engagement.



so, on the morning after her talk with Aubrey, she get the manager to

cancel the rest of her engagement, and, having pack her trunk the

night before, she leave for Beckham within an hour of his release she.

she look restlessly and eagerly from the window of the cab as she

drive to the station “to see if any of the company be about.” at last

she catch sight of Aubrey Cooke go down a street, with his back to

the cab, therefore so that he could not see she; and after that she

look out no more, but sit with burn cheek and her eye fix on

the front seat of the cab, all curiosity and interest go out of she.



she get to Beckham at three o’clock in the afternoon, and drive

straight to the Grange, which she reach before the dark November

day have close. to her surprise, the man-servant who open the door

recognize she at once.



to her question he reply that Mr. Harold be be nurse by the

housekeeper, that Lady Braithwaite and Mr. Stephen be abroad, Sir

George be in town, Mr. Wilfred in Leicestershire, and Mr. William

somewhere--he do not know where--“studying.”



Annie then ask to see the housekeeper, and learn from she that

Harry’s accident be indeed as serious as Aubrey Cooke’s word have

imply. he have slip as he be get into the dog-cart, one night

after sup with some friend in Beckham--Annie happen to know

something about those friend--and the wheel have pass over he and

break his left arm, besides inflict other less serious injury;

he have not yet quite recover from another illness, and have be

disregard his doctor’s order. after be take to a surgeon by the

gentleman who be with he, to have his arm set, he have insist on

be drive back home to the Grange at five o’clock in the morning.

the housekeeper continue that he have then, contrary to the advice

she have venture to give he, insist upon drink brandy in the

billiard-room; that she have wait about, not dare to go in and speak

to he again, until she hear a fall and a groan, and, run in,

have find that he have fall and again displace his broken arm. she

have get he to bed with the help of the man-servant and send for the

doctor; but no skill could prevent inflammation of the wound limb,

and he be now lie in a high fever and could recognize no one.



“I would strongly advise you not to see he, ma’am, until he be

quieter. he be very violent, and he use dreadful language.”



“I don’t suppose he say anything bad than what I have hear he

say when he be in full possession of his sense, Mrs. Stanley,” say

Annie, quietly. “it be not fair that all the care of nurse my husband

should fall upon you; so, if you please, I will go to he now.”



Mrs. Stanley lead the way to the room to which they have carry

he--not his own, but a large and more convenient one. she draw the

arm of the young wife through her own as they enter, for Annie have

grow very white and be shake from head to foot when her husband’s

voice, speak disjointedly to an imaginary listener, meet her ear.

she recover her self-command before venture to look at he; but,

however strong her emotion might have be, it would not have affect

he. he take no notice of her presence; his wide-open eye do not even

see she.



Annie do not give way again; but from that hour she take her place by

his bedside alternately with Mrs. Stanley, listen to idle babbling

of his useless vicious life, to invective against the carelessness

of groom, the meanness of his brother George, the “airs Sue give

herself.” but there be never one word of herself; she have pass out

of his life; be forget, as if those few month of their married

life have never be. only once do he refer to she, and that be not to

Annie, his wife, but to Miss Lane of Garstone Grange.



“see the pretty little governess go to church; feel half incline

to go too, just to look at she,” he murmur once while she sit by his

bedside listening. but then he ramble off into talk which concern

a dog he have buy, and Susan Green, the blacksmith’s daughter,

and let fall some epithet which, it occur to Annie, would apply

particularly well to Miss West, at whose house he and his companion

have be sup on the Wednesday night, or rather Thursday morning,

when she have run against he in Beckham Street, and when he have meet

with his accident.



it be a hard punishment for the weakness of marry he and the

fault of leave he that she be suffer now, as she listen to

his wandering talk about other woman, which show his contempt for

a sex he do not understand, or think worth the trouble of try to

understand. and all the while she have to try to overcome the disgust

with which he inspire she and the longing to be again in the society

of one man, one brilliant, interesting companion, for whom every word

she utter have a charm, every action of hers be right.



when Mrs. Stanley take her place in the sickroom, she would fly like

an escaped bird out of door, and wander through the field and the

now leafless copse by herself, rejoice in her temporary freedom,

try to forget the horrible fact that she be married, and the very

existence of that unconscious, senseless clog upon her life that she

have leave in the darken room up-stair. these ramble bring almost

as much pain as pleasure to she; they recall to she so vividly

the long marauding expedition she have have with William, when they

use to return home laden with bird’ egg and duck’ feather, and

moss-cover twig, all of which William have to carry as soon as they

get near the house, for fear any of the household should think that

Mrs. Harold Braithwaite be so childish as to care for such rubbish.

Harry have be merely an every-day trial then, to be shirk as much

as conscience permit; now he have become, and by her own fault, an

obstacle to her happiness which there be no possibility of remove.



she have return to the sickroom one afternoon to relieve the

housekeeper, and, find that Harry be sleep quietly--a fact which

make she a little nervous, as it prove he be get well--she

open a book and settle herself in an arm-chair by the fire, whence

she could see any movement of the invalid’s by merely raise her eye.

the book be George Sands’ “Consuelo.” open it at first carelessly,

the early page fix her attention, and before long she bent over

it, completely absorb in the fascinating story.



she do not see the sick man’s eye open, fall upon she, and remain

fix, at first vacantly, then intently, upon her bent head. she do

not even notice the slight sound he make as he struggle to raise

himself on his elbow, nor the faint gasp of astonishment he give

when, having succeed, he have satisfy himself that it be his

long-forget wife.



“Annie!” he exclaim, in a voice hoarse with weakness and with no

warm emotion than amazement.



she look up and say “Harry!” with just the same amount of tenderness.



“why be you here?” he ask curiously, as he fall weakly back upon his

pillow.



“why, to nurse you, of course!” say she in a soft voice, rise at

once without any noise or bustle, but in a quietly matter-of-fact

manner.



she come to the bed, arrange his pillow more comfortably, raise his

head, and give he something to drink, while he stare at her silently

and receive her attention without any remark, until she quietly go

back again to her arm-chair and “Consuelo.” still he gaze at she

fixedly, and, as she open the book at the right place, which she have

be careful not to lose on hear her husband address she for the

first time after nearly four year’ separation, he say:



“you’ve go off shockingly!”



“yes, I know I have,” say Annie, quite calmly, put her finger on

the line she have come to as she look up. “but you have well not talk

now,” she add, coaxingly; “it be very bad when you be still so weak.”



down go her head again; but, with characteristic tact, he insist on

continue:



“I don’t think I ever see anybody so much alter. I suppose that be

why you have come back. you find nobody else would admire you any

long, so it be time to come and saddle yourself on your husband.”



instead of be sting to the quick by this reproach, which be mean

to be very severe, Annie have some difficulty in repress an impulse

to laugh; but she only say, soothingly:



“it be all right, Harry; I be go away again as soon as ever you be

well. I’ll turn away so”--and she move the chair round to face the

fire--“and then you willn’t be annoy by the sight of my ugly face.”



she go on reading, or pretend to read, for some minute, until her

husband’s voice once more interrupt she.



“a fine lot of affection you seem to have for I now you have come

back! I dare say you wish I be dead all the time. never even ask I

how I feel! what do you come at all for?”



Annie put down her book again, and come toward the bed.



“I don’t think it be good for you to talk just at first. I think,

if I sit quite quietly, you would go to sleep again.”



“no, you don’t; you want to read your book. what be it?”



“it be a french book call ‘Consuelo.’”



“french! oh, of course--something too learn for I!”



“it be not learn at all. I’ll translate it to you if you like; but I

don’t think you would care much about it.”



“oh, no; it would be over my head, of course!”



his voice be grow very feeble and husky. Annie pour some medicine

into a glass and bring it to he.



“now,” say she, coaxingly, as she slip her hand under his pillow to

raise his head, “you have well drink this, and then lie still for a

little while. you be not very strong yet, you know.”



“I sha’n’t drink it--I willn’t have that vile stuff pour down my

throat!” say he, in a weak, dogged whisper.



“you have well take it. cann’t you feel how weak your voice be

get?” say Annie, persuasively.



“I willn’t take that, I tell you! that willn’t do--do I--any good! fetch

I some brandy-and-soda.”



“no, I cann’t do that; it wouldn’t be good for you.”



“do you hear what I say? fetch I some brandy-and-soda!”



he make a feeble, spasmodic effort to knock the glass out of her hand;

but she hold it out of his reach, and, lay his obstinate head, which

she be still support, gently down on the pillow again, she put the

medicine down on the table.



“don’t you mean to obey I? I willn’t drink your filthy poison! if you

want to get rid of I you have well doctor some brandy for I, and

then perhaps I’ll take it.”



“the brandy by itself would be poison to you now, without my

doctoring,” say Annie, quietly. “as soon as you be well again you

can drink what you like, you know; and the more faithfully you follow

the doctor’s order now, the soon you will be able to drink as much

brandy as you please.”



she say it in a very soft, gentle voice; but she could not quite

keep the scorn she feel for he out of the last word. weak tear of

impotent anger gather in Harry’s eye.



“you treat I like a dog! a fine make-believe your wifely duty be. when

I’m well again I’ll turn you out of the house at an hour’s notice--that

I will!”



she see that he be exciting himself dangerously; and fear the

effect of this emotion upon he in his weak state, she take the hand

he be convulsively clinch on the bedclothe in one of her, and

put her lip to it, say, in the most winning tone the actress

could assume:



“my poor dear Harry, I would give you what you want if I dare; and

when the doctor come, I will ask if you may have it. and I will go

away when you like; but you will let I stay until you be well, willn’t

you?”



Harry be touch by this unexpected appeal.



“all right; you may stay,” he murmur magnanimously.



“and willn’t you let I give you your medicine? I’ll drink some of it

first, if you like, to show you it ben’t poison.”



“no, that be only nonsense. I’ll take it,” whisper the grumpy

invalid, conquer; and when he have drink it, and she lay his head

gently down again, he say, “thank you. you may kiss I if you like,

old girl.”



Annie avail herself of this permission--not enthusiastically, but

still not without a touch of tenderness; and she sit in the chair by

the bedside until he go quietly off to sleep again.



the next few conversation she have with her husband, who get well

rapidly with the careful nursing he receive, be after the same

pattern--a little wrangle, with taunt and sneer on his side, and

careless submission on her, follow by a sort of tame reconciliation.

before long she have manage, by a firm refusal to do anything which she

do not think good for he and a very gentle manner, to get the upper

hand of the obstinate invalid; and, when Mrs. Stanley have a tussle with

he on account of his unwillingness to have his wound dress or to

take his medicine at the proper hour, she always go to Annie to get

over the difficulty. sometimes during a battle with the housekeeper he

would say:



“well, send Annie, then, and perhaps I’ll have it do.”



this flattering preference be receive by its object with anything but

gratitude. to be call up from her sleep in the middle of the night,

or to be send for in the course of a meal, because “Mr. Harold say he

willn’t take any slop, ma’am, unless you come and see that his beef-tea

ben’t hot enough to scald his throat,” do not fill she with any pride

in this rise in her husband’s esteem. at last, one night, when he be

fairly on the road to convalescence, she flatly refuse to go when Mrs.

Stanley come to say Mr. Harold would not let she dress the wound on his

shoulder, but want his wife to do it.



“tell he I say you can do it much well than I, Mrs. Stanley; and,

if he willn’t let you do it, he must wait till to-morrow morning,” say

the undutiful wife sleepily, as she turn over and shut her eye again.



the next morning Harry, who be to go down-stair for the first time

that day, bounce over on his side away from she as soon as she enter

his room and come up to the bedside. Annie walk softly toward the

door; then the invalid, who have recover much of the power of his

lung, roar:



“stop! where be you go?”



“I be go to breakfast,” say she, calmly.



“without even wish I good-morning! after refuse point-blank just

to step along the corridor in the night when I might have be die!

you’re a nice wife!”



“now, look here, Harry; I don’t pretend to do more than just my simple

duty to you, and don’t for a moment set myself up for a model wife.”



“I should think not indeed! everybody would laugh if you do.”



“everybody would laugh, as you say, if I pretend to show any

affection for a husband so selfish that he will break a night’s rest

of a very good nurse--I have be that, remember--on the most trifling

pretext. I dare say you think it an honor to choose I instead of Mrs.

Stanley to put on a poultice or arrange a bandage; but I assure you it

be one I don’t appreciate. you be nearly well now, and the task I set

myself of see you through your illness be over. my presence can only

irritate you now, and I think of take the hint you have often give

I, and go to-day.”



“go? what--leave I here all alone when I’ve show you I like to have

you near I? all right--go along then, you hard, heartless vixen! no,

no,” he call, as she turn again toward the door--“Annie, Annie, I

don’t mean it--I’m not ungrateful--I have be selfish! don’t go till

again! I like to hear your voice; and you move so quietly, and you talk

I’ve be so cross. don’t go, Annie, till I’m quite well. wait till

next week. willn’t you wait just till next week, Annie?”



she come back to his side again, look very grave.



“look here, Harry,” she say; “you be well enough now for I to speak

to you seriously, as I could not speak when you be lie there likely

to die. you have be very rude to I and ungracious, consider that

I come simply to do my good to get you well quickly. now the duty

I set myself be over, and I assure you, strange as it may seem to

you, I feel no irresistible wish to stay here a moment long than be

necessary. if you wish I to stay here still and do my good to amuse

you until you be strong enough to amuse yourself again, I will do so,

insolence to I which I have excuse on account of your illness, and

speak to I as other man speak to their wife--no well than that,”

she add, with a slight shade of irony.



“so you want to preach and domineer over I,” protest Harry, rather

sulkily, “just because I say I don’t mind your be in the room.

yes, yes, I will be civil,” he add hastily, as Annie’s head move

away; “I don’t mean to be rude to you: I really be grateful for the

way you have take care of I. only don’t speak to I in that hard

voice: just say something in your soft, pretty way, and I shall come

round directly. you always get over I when you speak in your soft

voice, you know.”



“well, then, may I go to breakfast, Harry?” say she, smile, and

take the hand he involuntarily stretch toward she.



“yes, yes; I willn’t be selfish again. kiss I first,” say the invalid,

in a more contented tone.



and Annie put her lip lightly to his forehead and leave the room. it

be very tiresome that she should have to delay her departure from

the Grange for this whim of her capricious husband. she hope that

she might be able to leave in a day or two, especially as George be

expect at the Grange; and, if she be to remain until his arrival,

she know well that she would find it difficult to get away. for she

could not fail to see that, while she have lose the first freshness of

her beauty, she have acquire, by her early encounter with the world

and by contact with the wit of the green-room, other charm of even

great power, which a man of Sir George’s type would be likely to

rate highly--especially in the country, where woman who can talk be

rare. she have no long the least fear of he, and she only dread, in

worldly-wise feminine vanity, not his attraction for she, but her for

he.



for the longing to be again at work in her profession be strong upon

she, and an unacknowledged wish to see that member of it whom she like

well be strong still. she know, too, that these few day of delay in

return to London might make the difference between her obtaining or

lose all chance of the engagement Aubrey Cooke have speak of to she.

her excitement and impatience grow so high as she think the matter

over during her solitary breakfast, that she feel oblige to throw a

shawl round she and rush into the open air to calm the fever rise

within she before return to her peevish lord and master up-stair.

how could she induce he to let she go at once, without exciting the

spirit of contradiction in he which would make he tease she to stay

because he see she wish to go? she have turn reluctantly toward the

house again, and be go indoor to Harry, who would probably be

dress and up for the first time since his illness now, when a wild

but delighted shout from the gate frighten she. she see a tall figure

race over the lawn toward she, and in another minute she be in

William’s frantic embrace.



he lift she off her foot, he make little rush at she, he danced

round she with savage cry, he show ecstasy in every uncivilized

and unheard-of way, ask she when she have come and why she have not

write to tell he.



“I don’t know where you be, William, my dear boy,” say Annie. “do

you know I be here?”



“rather! what do you think I’ve come for except to see you? and I

see George in town yesterday, and I’ve tell he, and he be come,

and Wilfred and everybody; and we’ll have the whole place light up,

and--Hooray! I must give you another hug!”



he be suit the action to the word when the window of Harry’s room,

which be on that side of the house, be throw sharply up by the

invalid, who be sit by it, and his angry and no long weak voice

call out:



“be off! leave she alone, you impudent young scamp! Annie, come here; I

want you. why have you be so long go? you don’t care what happen

to I!”



“I’m come,” say Annie, resignedly.









CHAPTER XVI.





Annie soon find herself in a difficult position between the

brother-in-law she like and the husband she dislike. William be

always want she to be out of door with he, Harry tease she

with sulky reproach if she be away from he for more than half an

hour at a time. the invalid come down to the drawing-room, which be

well warmed and cheerful, on the second day after William’s arrival,

lean on his brother’s arm. the ascendency over he which Annie

have gain in the sickroom she manage to maintain still; and the

artless William would make gesture of admiration and astonishment at

Harry’s docility to she from behind her husband’s back, and there be

much unpleasantness on one or two occasion when his brother catch

he. William also make himself obnoxious by call Harry “the Ogre,”

sometimes out of hearing of his eld brother and sometimes within,

and by assume an intimate knowledge of Annie’s movement during the

four year of her absence from the Grange, which Harry of course do

not possess.



in these early day of her return Annie put off question about the way

in which she have occupy those four year, and leave Harry to imagine

that she have support herself by teach. her skill in conversational

fence be much great than that of either of her companion, she

could always lead the talk into what channel she would; but it be

grow a delicate matter to avoid collision between Harry and William,

each of whom consider himself to have an exclusive right to her

attention, when the situation be change by the arrival on the same

day, though not by the same train, of Wilfred and Sir George.



William be dispatch by Annie to Beckham in the dog-cart to meet his

eld brother, and, when he be go, Harry, who, under his wife’s

care, be get rapidly through his convalescence, fidget about the

room, and at last knock over a gypsy-table cover with trifle.



“all right, Harry; I’ll pick they up,” say Annie, hear a muttered

oath from her husband.



“what be you in such a hurry for? I do hate a woman to be in a hurry,”

say he, testily, notice unusual haste in his wife’s movement as she

knelt on the floor gather up the thing his clumsiness have scatter.



“it be get very late, and I must dress for dinner now George be

come back.”



Harry fling himself into a chair and scowl at she.



“oh, all this fuss for George! your appearance don’t matter for I, I

suppose? I’m only your husband!”



“my dear Harry, if you will take the trouble to think you will see

that, as, since you have be ill, you have not have late dinner, I

have not insult you by change my gown to see you eat toast and

mutton-broth in your dressing-gown. besides, I should like to hide the

fall off in my look which you be kind enough to tell I of from

George, who will not hurt my vanity by mention it, if he do notice

any great change.”



“look here, Annie! I don’t want to hurt your feeling; I don’t think

you be vain; and--and--do you know--I really--I think sometimes, when

you tell we anything to make we laugh, for instance, you look pretty

than you ever do. you--you look so mischievous, and your eye sparkle

so, you make one want to kiss you--only then--then, somehow, you never

seem to want to be kiss--at least not by I!” he add, testily.



Annie burst out laugh, a little constrainedly perhaps.



“why, whom should I want to kiss I except my husband?” say she,

carelessly, as she bent over her occupation of fit together two

piece of broken Dresden china.



“I don’t know, I be sure,” say Harry, rather sulkily, feel that his

conciliatory speech have not meet with the response it deserved--“George,

perhaps.”



“why, surely you be not jealous of George, Harry!” she cry, laugh

more naturally.



“I don’t know that I’m not; but it wouldn’t make much difference to you

if I be, would it?” he ask; and, as, for one moment, she do not

answer, he walk, with the aid of the intervene chair, from the one

right, on her shoulder. “it wouldn’t make any difference, would it?” he

repeat.



Annie look up rather mischievously.



“I don’t think it would, Harry.”



this be a disconcert answer to a husband.



“oh, very well!” say he, gruffly, after a minute’s pause. “then I

see what I be to expect;” and he get up to walk away with offended

not well yet, not nearly well; I’m not strong enough to walk steadily.”



“oh, well, Harry, I’ve see you walk just as unsteadily when you be

quite well!” say Annie, dryly.



Harry snatch his arm from she, and fall into the near chair,

flush violently.



“very well, ma’am; you call I a drunkard now! I shouldn’t have think

any woman would have the heart to make fun of a sick husband; but you

don’t care for anything as long as you can laugh and scamper about

the garden like a great tomboy with that infernal long-legged idiot

William! you be enough to make any husband drink, just to forget you,

you unfeele little creature, you!”



“come now, Harry, I don’t think you can say it be I drive you to

drink; and I think you would have forget I pretty quickly even

without that assistance,” say she, pass her hand soothingly down

tell on he when she choose to use it. “you know very well that it

will not require any more crime on the part of your wicked wife, for

instance, to induce you to undo all the progress you have make toward

get well during the last few day by sit up to-night drinking

with George and Wilfred.”



“and what do you care if I do?”



“it be no affair of mine, of course, and I shall not annoy you and

bring down a storm upon my own head by interfere. to borrow your own

word, it would make no difference if I do.”



“how do you know it wouldn’t? don’t I always do what you wish?”



“I think the temptation to do what I don’t wish will be strong now

you will have pleasanter company than a faded wife.”



“whoever call you ‘fade’? I never do--you know I never do! and you

know I like your company. I never know you so pleasant before.”



“oh, you don’t think I pleasant always!”



“No; because you say such nasty thing--thing you never use to dare

to say when I be well. now I’m ill, you think you can say anything,

because I’m not strong enough yet to think of anything just as cut

to say back. but I’ll pay you out when I get well again, clever as you

be.” he speak in a rather irritated tone, but not ill-humoredly; she

she.



“I sha’n’t give you a chance, because I have some very important

business in London, and my duty as your nurse be over, and to-morrow I

shall go to town.”



“and when be you come back?”--excitedly.



she do not answer.



“when do you mean to come back, I say?” he repeat, in a loud voice.



still no answer. Harry clutch his wife’s arm.



“then I shall not let you go! you be not my nurse; you be my wife,

and I forbid you to leave I again--do you hear? what be this business

you speak of? what be it? I have a right to know--and I will know!”



Annie do not attempt to remove her arm from his grasp, but look

slowly up at he with a steady, cold, firm expression in her dark eye,

“you have a right to know, and you shall know. I cann’t tell you all

now, but just this. for four year, during which you never take the

trouble to find out whether I be starve--and I be not so very far

off that sometimes--I have be work to lay the foundation of a

I have be put back a little, just as I be go to make a great

my ‘learn air,’ and with my preaching--for I shall not be able to

in your old way, as I know you will, and bring back the fever, and

make yourself ill again----”



“but, if I make myself ill again, you will have to nurse I.”



“indeed, you be mistaken!” answer Annie, raise her eye to his

with spirit. “if now, after be warn, you choose, rashly, to put

your life in danger, and to undo all the good our constant watching and

nursing have do you, I shall not consider myself bind to sacrifice

myself any long to a man who could be guilty of such foolish and

selfish conduct, whether he be my husband or not.”



“then you would leave I to die while you go on enjoy your

‘career,’ as you call it?”



“I would leave you to take your chance.”



Harry begin to tremble all over, and the tear rise to his eye. his

hand relax its hold on Annie’s arm, and fall down by his side.



soften, frighten by the effect of her word, Annie clasp she

little hand on his shoulder, and tell he not to take her word so

seriously, that she have speak they only because she want he to take

care of himself and get well fast.



“no, you don’t--no, you don’t! you want I to die, so that you may be

free!” say he, in a hoarse, tremulous voice, keep his head turn

happily, his own emotion prevent his notice the effect of his word

“I see, I see! you want I to drink and kill myself, or ruin myself, so

that you may go away and get praise for be a martyr! go away--go

away from I! I don’t want your little soft hand about I, when all

the while I know your heart be hard and you hate I!” say he, shake

Annie rise slowly, and walk with downcast head toward the door. but

she have not shut it behind she before her husband’s voice call she

“Annie, Annie--come here--only one minute! I want to speak to you!”



she return, and stand, with her eye still down, very meekly before

he.



“Annie,” say he, stretch forward to take her hand and draw she

toward he, “I don’t mean what I say just now. I be only in fun--at

least I don’t think what I be say. I--I want to see if you would

believe I. I know you don’t want I to die; and look here--if you will

promise not to go away yet I willn’t sit up with George, and I will drink

only just what you let I, and I’ll do just what you tell I--till I

get well.”



Annie shake her head.



“I will--I swear it! now you will stay, willn’t you? here--give I your

other hand. there! I swear to do just what you tell I--till I get

well. now promise not to go to London. no--you swear, too,” say he,

eagerly.



“I promise----”



“no, swear.”



“I swear not to go to London till you be quite well, if you don’t do

anything rash. there--I hear the dog-cart. Harry, I must go to the door

to meet he.”



“meet who?”



“George, of course.”



“Confound George!”



but Annie be already out of the room.



she be flush with the excitement of the successful battle she have

just have with her husband, and with the other excitement of meet

her eld brother-in-law, and George show nothing but pleasure at

sight of she. they come into the drawing-room talk brightly, and

the baronet scarcely exchange more than a couple of sentence and a

hand-shake with his surly brother, so pleased be he to find a pleasant

woman again in his house.



when Wilfred arrive, just before dinner, he in his turn engross she

completely; and at dinner these two new-comer take up so much of her

attention that the convalescent Harry, who be at dinner with the rest

find fault with everything which be put before he.



“I cann’t eat that. how be I to hack at it with only one hand?” he

growl, when the servant offer he some mutton.



“shall I cut it up for you, sir?”



“no, I willn’t have it; I don’t want anything at all!” say he, look

with a frown at his wife, who turn from George to tell the servant

to bring the plate to she, and dutifully cut up the mutton, which her

sulky husband, without thank, then condescend to eat.



Annie have put on a very pretty pale gray silk gown with elbow-sleeve

and square-cut bodice edge with dainty lace, and a long spray of pink

azalea fasten carelessly on one side of the neck. she be delighted

chatter and look so charming that not one of the man could keep his

“have you see anything of the Mainwarings, Annie?” ask George, when

dinner be nearly over.



“oh, yes! I meet Mrs. Mainwaring the other day with a volume of ‘the

Band of Hope Review’--I don’t know whether you have hear of it--under

one arm. she say she think of come to read to Harry, if he would

like it, to cheer he up.”



something in Annie’s demure tone set they all laugh.



“I say he would be delighted; but we don’t think too much excitement

be good for he just at first. and she ask if Sir George have any

good book in his library, and I say, ‘oh, yes!’ and she say I ought

to read some to he. I say I think I ought, and I come back and read

he the _Sporting Dramatic News_ all through.”



“oh, Annie, she wouldn’t have you back in her schoolroom now!”



“no, indeed she would not!” answer Annie promptly.



when she rise to leave the gentleman, there be a little anxiety in her

with its one ring--her wedding-ring, which decorum now force she to

wear--lightly on his shoulder, and, as he give no sign, she bend down

the faint perfume of the azalea on her breast, hear she quicken

breathing as he still hesitate.



“do you remember?” she whisper softly.



he raise his eye, sullenly still, to the little, plead face.

she be irresistible at that moment, with her smile lip and her

sparkle eye, her head a little on one side in entreaty. there come

a flash from his eye; her womanly fascination have win from he what

his promise would have fail to get. he get up, and, lean on her

slight shoulder, let she lead he out of the room.



Annie be so much pleased with this unexpected little triumph that her

chatter on so that he have no time or inclination for the complaint

he be go to make against his brother George’s brutal indifference

hear the dining-room door open and the voice of the other three in

the hall.



“hang they all! they make so much noise. Annie, I think I’ll go to bed;

and I want you to come and read to I.”



but George have hear the last word as he come in.



“no, no, Harry! go to bed by all mean, if you will; but you mustn’t

make a victim of Annie. you have have my Lady Sunbeam all to yourself

for week; you must let she shed a few ray on the rest of we now.”



before Harry could make an angry reply, Annie break in:



“Harry have no wish to deprive you of such a very simple pleasure; I

will shed my ray upon you, as you poetically term it, by play you

the very few new piece I have learn since you last hear I, George.

and, Harry, you be feverish--you have well not stay up; I have

nothing to play that you have not hear, and I will come up and read

you to sleep by the time you be ready for I.”



she ring the bell without give he time to answer; and Harry, who be

really too wear out to make much resistance, grumblingly go off with

the servant, who lend a stout arm to his tottering master.



Annie go to the piano, and play one thing after another, and sing a

more interested in the talk he be have with she than in the music;

and, as her finger, from idly play, at last cease altogether and

lay on the key, he say:



“come into the conservatory. you love flower, and there you will let

I smoke, I know.”



Annie shake her head reluctantly.



“I mustn’t. I’ve promise Harry to read to he. he will be past be

read to and do nothing but growl if I delay any long,” say she, with

resignation, as she rise slowly and shut the piano.



“how you have manage to tame the bear, though!” say George,

admiringly. “of course gratitude or courtesy be out of the question

with he; but I think even submission be, until I see he follow

you out of the dining-room to-night. but then an archangel couldn’t

have resist you as you look at that moment,” continue he, in a low

voice, bend down to look into her eye. “it be hard to see a look

like that waste upon such a clod.”



“do you think so?” say Annie, laugh lightly, as she go up-stair

and he follow she. “why, that be only the old story! it be the

‘clod’ of the earth who get the benefit of all the beauty and grace

and pleasant thing in the world.”



“you have grow cynical, Annie. come in here for a few minute and

explain yourself.”



he lead the way into the dimly-light picture-gallery, where Annie and

William have have their first game of battledoor and shuttlecock four and

a half year before. she sink down upon the cushion ottoman to which

George lead she, and look gravely at he as he seat himself beside

she.



“it be very easy to explain,” say she. “do not all the people who

spend their life in the practice of any art, clever people generally,

and capable of hard thinking as well as hard living, waste their

effort for the careless enjoyment of other who have not half their

brain, or their courage, or their capacity? the rich _parvenu_ who

don’t know a Rubens from a rembrandt, patronize the rise painter

and delight afterward in the boast that he ‘make that man, sir.’ the

wise man write for fool to read. and the actress give day of study

to her share in a piece which the dressmaker in the pit condemn as

‘very poor stuff.’ it be always the same.”



“you speak very bitterly.”



“yes. for you see I range myself on the side of the hard-work,

capable one. don’t you know how I have spend these last four year?”



“no, no; do tell I,” say George, with a shrewd guess at her answer,

bend lower over she in his interest.



“I have spend they on the stage.”



“the stage!” echo another voice.



they both start and look round. behind they, lean against the

wall, not far from the door, be Harry, in his dressing-gown, pale,

heavy-eye, sullen. he look at his wife with fierce eye and frown

brow.



“so you be an actress! I don’t wonder you be ashamed to tell I how

you pass your time.”



“I be not ashamed, Harry,” say Annie, calmly, rise and go toward

he. “if you think I ought to be, you have only to say a word and you

shall never be trouble with I again.”



“you be in a great hurry for I to say that word, and, by Jove, for

once I feel inclined to please you! an actress! no wonder I find you

ready to listen to soft word from any man! no wonder the word from I

which use to set you blush for pleasure cann’t touch you now! you

be just a thing for everybody to look at--not a wife for I! go away;

I would rather fall than that you should touch I!”



he be tottering, and his forehead be wet with weakness and passion.

he would not take George’s help, but stagger along by the wall to the

door. there the housekeeper meet he, and Annie, stand still in the

middle of the picture-gallery, hear he say:



“Brandy, for Heaven’s sake, brandy, whether it be poison to I or not!”









CHAPTER XVII.





Annie turn with a piteous expression of face to George when her angry

husband have leave they.



“what can one do with a man like that?” she say. “it be impossible to

reason with he, impossible to understand he. he be like an overgrown

child.”



“I don’t know about that,” answer George, quietly. “I think I can

understand this last outbreak pretty well.”



“what do you mean?”



“why, when you leave he, you be a little timid lily, whose charm be

quite lose upon a great senseless brute like that,” say George, with

sentiment; “now you have come back a----”



“a great flaunting dahlia, whose charm must be apparent to the mean

observation, and particularly to a person of my husband’s taste!”

finish Annie, look up at he very gravely.



his sentiment be dispel; he be oblige to burst out laugh.



“you be too sharp for I. you know very well I do not mean that.

you be a charming woman who can hold your own in any society; you

have cause quite a flutter among we poor rustic; and Harry, find

himself the possessor of something everybody else admire, with

dog-in-the-manger instinct, wish to keep all to himself the treasure

whose value he himself would never have discover and be quite unable

to appreciate.”



“you be too severe upon poor Harry. he have a lot of good

quality--you know I always say so; only--unfortunately they be

quality which don’t harmonize very well with mine.”



“nor with anybody else’s. it be unfortunate, certainly. he would be

charming on a desert island.”



“I really think he would be happy there,” say Annie, with a sigh,

“if he have a horse and some dog. he be kind to animal, and they seem

to understand he. good-night, George; I must go to he now. and the

chance be even whether he will try to hit I if I go near he, or

insist on my remain in the room till he go to sleep.”



she shake hand, and leave the baronet gaze admiringly at her little

in her hand, she whisper:



“take that away, please. he have go to sleep, I think.”



“that be all right. I be as long as I could be, and I bring it

myself, in hope that you would be here when I come back.”



the housekeeper go away, and Annie, fearful he might take cold, draw

William, with great tact, instantly assume an appearance of

preternatural solemnity on his brother’s entrance.



“what be all this mystery? what be you do in here?” ask Harry,

crossly.



“I be help William with his study,” say Annie.



upon this her promising pupil grow blue with suppressed laughter, and

Harry’s manner get more and more unpleasant.



“oh, I should have think you have have enough of schoolroom work!

however, since you haven’t, and I’m not too proud to take a lesson,

you shall give I one, too,” and he fling himself into a chair with an

uncompromise surliness which be not encouraging to a teacher.

“Lorsque Telemaque et ses compagnon--virgule----”



“oh, confound your French!” growl Harry.



and William burst into a roar of laughter; while Annie, see that her

again as soon as he get outside the door.



“why do you encourage that donkey to take up your time?” ask Harry,

when he have exhaust all the offensive epithet at his command on his

“I be very fond of William,” say Annie, quietly. “it be I who first

encourage he to study; and now it be a great pleasure to I to help

“a fine lot of study you get through, I have no doubt! you be

study very hard when I come in, ben’t you?”



“now look here, Harry; you be absurdly unreasonable,” say Annie,

straw about his pupil except as a mere learning-machine. I couldn’t

complain when he and I be out in the field and wood together all

“what do you want with his companionship? I cann’t think what you can

see in a great, clumsy gawk like that. he ben’t even clever.”



“he be good-temper, and--he be fond of I.”



“much you care about anybody’s be fond of you! you be the cold

woman I ever see, and all your pretty--I mean all your affected little

way be just act. yes, that be what they be--just act!”

repeat Harry, as if strike by a happy idea.



“very well, Harry. then why don’t you let I go and act on the stage,

where I shall get applaud instead of worried about it?”



“because I don’t choose to let you go,” say he doggedly. “and I don’t

choose to see myself slight and treat as if I be nobody at all,

more of these humbug lesson--do you hear?”



“I hear you certainly,” answer Annie softly.



“that mean that you willn’t obey I, I suppose?” she do not reply.



“very well then; I sha’n’t say any more,” say Harry, shake with

passion; “but, when I find he again grin at you over his copy-book

his tomfoolery into the fire and punch his head for he.”



“that will be very wise,” remark Annie gravely. “and, if you be

only to treat in the same way every other person who can talk to I on

subject that interest I and who do not grumble at I from morning

more entertaining companion for yourself.” she have rise and walk

“where be you go?” ask Harry sharply.



“to meet Lilian at the station. you know she be come to-day, and

Stephen with she.”



he let she go without further comment; but, when she come down-stair

again, ready to start, she find he in the hall play with a

hunting-crop.



“I say, Annie, be you go to the library at Beckham?”



“yes.”



“will you get some book for I?”



“for you!” say his wife, in amazement.



“yes, for me”--very irritably.



“oh, yes, certainly! what book shall I get?”



“oh, anything you like!”--and, without look at she, he march off

into the billiard-room.



“I hope there be nothing the matter with his head,” think Annie,

anxiously, as she get into the carriage.



Annie go to the station to meet her sister-in-law, without any of the

nervousness she have once feel before an interview with that imperious

Aubrey Cooke be to play a part; and, with flushed cheek and beat

of other young actor who never come to anything in particular. but

her pleasure in read this testimony to his talent be none the less

great, and with tremble finger, she almost involuntarily draw a

shaky line with her pencil down that part of the notice which refer

to he.



she be look brilliant when she meet Lilian, who compliment she on

her appearance, and say she have hear from her brother that she would

into such a charming woman.



“but you might have let I know you be on the stage,” say Lilian,

with good-humor reproach. “I find now that I know several of the

actor who be with you at the Regency. and only think! I go there

one night when you be play in the piece, and never recognize you.”



“I recognize you, though.”



“do you? can you see people you know among the audience when you be

act?”



“oh, yes! and I see Colonel Richardson.”



“Most people can see he when you be about,” break in Stephen, who

have come from town with his cousin, but have sit silent in the carriage

until now.



this be a bold speech than he would have venture to make in the old

time to Lilian, Annie think. she note that the cripple have grow

much older-looking; his face, which have once be handsome, be thin

and waste, and he look sullen and discontent.



Lilian take no notice of his remark, and ask Annie if she have see

many of the people of the neighborhood since she have be at the Grange.



“yes, most of they have call, to my surprise, since William let out

to old Mrs. Knowles that I have be on the stage. she and her niece

make a tentative call, and I suppose the rumor spread that I do

not bite, so everybody come and praise my wifely devotion, which I

certainly do not deserve.”



Lilian laugh.



“Harry ill must be a great trial, though.”



“he be rather; he have such strange freak.”



“husband always have, dear. only fancy--my husband want to prevent

my come to the Grange!”



“really? for what reason?”



“oh, he disapprove of my brother, or some such nonsense!” say Lilian

lightly.



but Stephen raise his eye to his cousin’s face with a penetrate

look which Annie note and remember.



Dinner that night be a banquet of rejoicing. the two lady be both,

in different way, among the most charming woman of the day. Lilian

be very handsomely dress in dark red velvet, which show off her

fair, queenly beauty well; Annie, in maize-color silk, with soft

fold of indian muslin about the throat, look like a little fairy.

the style of each be so different from that of the other that their

attraction do not clash, and Annie’s quiet, simple manner of say

amusing thing be the good contrast possible to Lilian’s laugh

impertinence.



Lilian be very anxious to know at once all about her sister-in-law’s

stage-experience, and be seize with a strong desire to become an

actress herself.



“don’t you find people off the stage very dull after the nice, amusing

people you meet in the theater?” she ask at dinner.



“oh, no! some stage-people be dreadful bore, and many be coarse

and many commonplace. they be not all alike, you know, any more than

people off the stage.”



“but all the actor I have ever meet have be so bright and amusing. I

know two who be at the Regency, where you act--Mr. Gibson and Mr.

Cooke.”



“oh, yes; I hear they say they know you!”



“don’t you like they? be they nice in the theater? they be two of the

well-breed man I have ever know.”



“they be very nice man, indeed, and very clever actor; I like they

both immensely.”



“and Mr. Gibson be so handsome, and do not seem to know it. but he

must, for I should think all the woman in the theater must be in love

with he. be you not a little in love with he?”



“in love with a beggarly cad of an actor?” shout Harry, scandalize.



“you don’t know what you be talk about!” say his sister, coolly.

“of course your manner be not those of Mr. Gibson; they be those of

his valet. don’t you think he very handsome, Annie?”



“yes, very. and he have such a sweet voice.”



her husband’s voice, for the moment not at all sweet, utter a

growl protest.



“and Mr. Cooke? he be not handsome, but he be charming. don’t you like

he? oh, I know you must, for I see that you have mark his name in a

critique in your paper!”



Annie blush as she answer that he be very nice, too, and very

clever; she have an uneasy feeling that her husband be glare at she

across the table and note her change of color.



during the few minute which remain of the lady’ stay in the

dining-room, Harry never take his eye off his wife’s face; and she be

conscious of this, though she do not once look at he.



in the drawing-room Lilian be quite affectionate.



“you be always a good little girl; but I have no idea you would bloom

into such a clever woman,” say she, with her white hand on the

shoulder of the small woman.



“how--clever?” ask Annie, laugh.



“why, at keep your own counsel! but you may trust I. there be

always some one nice than one’s husband, and when one’s husband be

hear you be on the stage, I try to find out who it be that

discreet that nobody could link your name with any other. and it be

and nobody could have see you wince but I. I be very clear-sighted in

these matter.”



“indeed!” say Annie, calmly. “and may I know which of my fellow-actor

I be die for love of?”



“I do not say that. I know your conduct be circumspection itself. but

I know which of these two gentleman be--nice than Harry.”



“oh, you might put they both together and bracket a good many more with

they under that heading!” say Annie.



“I dare say. but you need not look so ostentatiously indifferent. I

should think it must be impossible to know Mr. Gibson well without

admire he.”



“well, that be true, certainly,” assent Annie, not give the least

sign of the relief she feel at hear Lilian utter the wrong name.



she do not in the least mind that her sister-in-law should imagine

she to have a preference for Mr. Gibson; but she would not for world

have it suspect that she could have the faint warmth of feel

for--Mr. Cooke.



when the gentleman come into the drawing-room, Harry be not among

she go at once, and judge, as soon as she enter his room, that

“you send for I, Harry? what be the matter? don’t you feel well?” she

in answer, he suddenly produce the _era_ from the side of his chair,

and bring his fist down with a thump upon the unfortunate pencil-mark

by Aubrey Cooke’s name.



“who be this man Cooke?” he ask savagely.



Annie glance carelessly down at the paper, and say:



“Mr. Aubrey Cooke? oh, he be one of the actor whom I know at the

Regency--one of the very actor Lilian be speak of at dinner!”



“yes, I know that very well; and you need not pretend to be so

curiosity.

more out of place than in the case of the man I have act with. and,

if you don’t know as much as you pretend, ask Lilian.”

she be look so pretty in the light evening dress, with her graceful

I think. here be ‘Sponge’s Sporting Tour,’ and ‘how I become an M. F.

H.,’ and a book about horse, and----”



she hand he a volume with her eye still bend upon the other as she

boor, to be feed and clothe, and smooth into good temper when I be

against such treatment. you see I know all about it, though I be such

she have walk to the other end of the room, pick up the book

“really, Harry, you ought to be in a lunatic asylum!” say Annie, out

“so I shall be very soon, if you go on treat I like a child, when I

love you like a man!” burst out Harry, passionately.



his wife look up at he, from where she be stand at the other

“yes, yes--stare at I as much as you like; I do love you, and I’m not

the fool you think I, except in care for you! do you think I don’t

you; but then you just avoid I, and I don’t care. but now you come

whatever I do don’t matter. I say it be cruel, wicked, and, however

good and clever you may be, you be treat I badly!” he end, his

“Harry!” be all his astonished wife could utter.



“I know I’m not a companion for you,” he go on, “but you don’t want

I to be, you willn’t understand that I want to be. I ask you to get

I some book; but I want book that you like, so that I might read

they and talk to you about they, like William and George. and then you

bring I a lot of sporting trash, as if I ben’t fit for anything but

the stable!”



“Harry!” whisper his wife again, make a step toward he.



he look up at she eagerly, wait for she to come to he. but she

stop.



“well, be you afraid of I?” say he.



his tone be not invite; but Annie understand he this time, knelt

down by his chair, and let he put his arm round she.



“Annie, will you try to love I?” he ask, huskily.



“yes, Harry, I will try.”









CHAPTER XVIII.





Annie leave her husband’s room that night, after his most unexpected

declaration of love and her own promise to try to return it, in a state

of bewilderment in which thought be for a long time impossible. that

his affection for she be anything more than a pass caprice, the

result partly of jealousy of his brother, and partly of pique at her

own indifference, she do not for a moment believe. if her heart have

be quite free, she might have be less skeptical, or more clearly

touch by this acknowledgment of the strength of the influence she have

gain over her rough and hitherto careless young husband. but she know

how deep lie the difference between his nature and her own, and since

the first week of her marriage she have give up all hope of their ever

harmonize with each other except in the most superficial manner.

through his passionate word she have seem, in spite of herself, to

hear the ring of another voice, and she feel, with a thrill of shame,

that no word of the man she have swear to love could wake in her an

emotion so strong as that she have feel at the few faltering word in

which Aubrey Cooke have confess that he love she.



and Aubrey Cooke be out in the world work hard, as she feel, to

win position and money, to make himself a name, to rise to the height

of the ambition she have encourage; and perhaps even yet, in spite of

her discourage word to he, he be nurse the vain belief that she

would some day be his, and long for the time when they should wander

out together again, and have more long talk, in which the word of

each seem but to express the unuttered thought of the other; while

Harry, her husband, would remain an ignorant idler to the end of his

life, ill-temper, arrogant, unsympathetic to she, as if he have be

an inhabitant of another world. and this man she have promise to try

to love, with the honest, solemn intention of keep her word to the

good of her power! but she confess to herself, with a shudder at the

thought of the self-sacrifice she would have to make if his caprice

be to last and she be to have to put off indefinitely her return to

the stage, that she have an uphill task before she.



the next morning she meet her husband in the expectation of find he

as ungracious as usual. but Harry have apparently be think out the

position, and come to the conclusion that the effort must not be all on

his wife’s side. at any rate, he be gentle and considerate, and ask

she if she would drive he out, in a courteous tone which seem to

admit the possibility of a refusal.



it be the first day that he have be out of door since his illness,

and he be very good-temper and happy, sit wrap up in rug by

his wife’s side in Lady Braithwaite’s pony-carriage; and, after that

trial of it, the daily drive become an institution. Annie find that

the explanation they have at the time of that little episode of the

sporting-book have have the satisfactory result of make Harry more

docile than ever; and when, in the country lane through which they

drive for mile each day over the frost-bind earth, she start he

on some favorite topic of his, such as the training of race-horse or

the advantage of a straight saddle, she find that she could continue

her own train of thought almost undisturbed, by the help of a nod of

approval every now and then; and she find he quite an endurable

companion.



but unfortunately Harry be not so stupid as he be ignorant, and one

day, when Annie have give a pleasant smile of approbation of what he

be say without having listen to it, he suddenly stop short in

the middle of a sentence, and, look round at he in surprise, his

wife find that he be sulk.



“go on, Harry; that be very interesting,” say she innocently.



“no, it ben’t; you don’t know what I be talk about,” he return

sullenly.



“yes, I do, Harry. you be talk about--horse,” say she, with

what she think a safe guess.



but her husband look blacker than ever.



“I ben’t talk about horse, as it happen. it show how much you

care what I say. I’m much oblige to you for let I see that I bear

you. stop! I’ll get out;” and he toss off his rug.



“no, no, don’t, dear Harry! let I drive you home. it be only a little

way; but it be too far for you to walk yet. I’m very, very sorry I be

so inattentive; but the fact be I--I have something on my mind that be

trouble I; and so----”



“have you, Annie?” he ask anxiously. “what be it?” then, notice

the expression of his wife’s face, his manner change, and he cry

roughly, “it be a lie! it be an infernal excuse! stop, I tell you, or

I’ll jump out without your stopping! now I’ll be hang if I let you

drive I out any more! you be just a little hypocrite, pretend to

listen and be so sweet, when all the time you don’t care what I say

if I talk myself hoarse. go and talk your learn jargon with George,

and William, and--the deuce, if you like! I’m go to Joe Green’s, the

blacksmith.”



she have stop, see it be of no use to try to argue with he in

this mood, and that to disobey he would only be to see he break

his neck before her eye. and she drive home full of remorse, after

watch he vault over a gate to take a short cut to the village, and

make one more effort to stop he by a piteous cry of “Harry!” of

which he take no notice.



to the blacksmith’s--where Susan Green live! this, then, be the end

of his revive affection for herself, that the very first walk he took

lead he straight back to the vulgar charm of the blacksmith’s daughter.



it be a bitter, unpleasant thought, even for a wife not sufficiently

fond of her husband to be jealous. it be a humiliation which bring

up in her mind the image of the one man who think her charm superior

to those of any other woman. she do not feel jealous, but insult by

the rude speech of her husband, who, after she have use every care,

every charm at her command to fulfill her duty to he in sickness

and in convalescence, reward she with a coarse taunt and an openly

express intention of leave she for the society of a girl of low

birth and not unspotted name.



she drive home, and, as soon as she have take off her hat and mantle,

go into the library, where, in spite of Harry’s rough prohibition,

she still continue to give William lesson in French. Dusk be come

on; but it be light enough for she to see the figure bend over a

book in a low chair near the window. she cross the room and put she

hand on his shoulder.



“William, how wrong of you to try your eye like that.”



he look up. it be not William, but Harry.



“you, Harry?” murmur his wife, in astonishment.



“yes, I--Harry. I may try my eye as much as I like, mayn’t I?”



she take the book gently from his hand. it be “Sartor Resartus.”



“you have not be read this?” she gasp.



“yes, I have. I see it lie on the table with your book-marker in it,

so I take it up to see what it be like; and I’ve read six page, but

I’ll be hang if I can make head or tail of it!”



“nor can I,” say Annie.



“well, what do you read it for then?”



she hesitate.



“it be write by a great man, a ‘mighty thinker,’ and I like to try

to find out what he mean.”



“well, I think it be a very dull amusement. Thomas Carlyle”--looking

at the title page. “Mighty thinker, you say. I’ve hear of a mighty

hunter----”



“oh, you be think of Nimrod! it’s not the same person,” say Annie.



“you be laugh at I! very well!”



“yes, I be,” say Annie, smile, and put her arm affectionately

round his neck. “but I think, if I don’t laugh, I should cry--I feel

very much touch by find you--find you here try to read my

dull book when I be feel very angry with you for run away from

I as you do.”



Harry rub his curly head against she responsively without say

anything for a minute. then he look up searchingly into her face.



“Annie, I want to ask you something. just now Ste--some one tell I

they have see Colonel Richardson in Beckham several time during the

last few day, and have see you talk to he.”



“well?”



“Well!”--sharply. “and why don’t you say anything about it?”



“there be nothing to say. I meet Colonel Richardson, I speak to he,

and that be all. what be there strange in that?”



“oh, nothing, of course!” he pause for a moment and look away

from she. then he burst out, but as if to himself: “it be Colonel

Richardson who came dangle after you four year ago. you always like

he.”



“Harry, don’t be so absurd as to be jealous of Colonel Richardson!

indeed you have never have the slight cause to be so.”



“how can I be sure of that?” say he, turn upon she suddenly. “one

thing I be certain of--that is, that, during these four year that you

have be away from I, you have meet somebody you like well than

I. I don’t say it be unnatural--I don’t say I’m surprised; but I say

that I know I’m right, and I’ll find out who it be, as sure as I’m your

husband! you say I’ve no need to be jealous of any actor--and I don’t

myself think you would lower yourself as far as that----”



“you forget that I’m an actress,” say Annie, composedly.



“be an actress; but you’re not one now,” answer he, hastily. “well,

if you never care for any actor, why not for Colonel Richardson? he be

handsome, and know how to talk to you about the thing you like.”



“but I have tell you already that I never care for Colonel Richardson:

and your persistent jealousy be an insult to I when I tell you it have

no foundation. he belong to a type of man which have no attraction for

“what type’s that?”



“he be an idler; and I have work too long and too hard myself not to

despise idleness in a man.”



Harry give a grunt of disapproval.



“I suppose you call I an idler.”



“well, I don’t think you be much else,” say she, smile.



“it seem to I, Annie, you expect a precious deal too much of a man,”

like a nigger, whether he have any need to work or not, and read himself

blind over the dull trash that ever be print, and never talk

that haven’t the least interest, and beam all over with smile when he

be annoy.”



Annie laugh.



“I don’t think I ever expect all that of anybody, and certainly not

of you, Harry.”



and weary of this useless discussion, she leave the room as Stephen

“what do you come torment I for with your humbug story about

“very well; don’t mind what I say. of course I be warn you only for

“liar!” hiss out Harry.



“oh, it be only my fun, of course,” sneer the cripple.

he be hear seize he, and a sudden desperate resolve stop his

the hideous story seem to he in his excited state only too likely.

but one thing in his programme it be beyond Harry’s strength to carry

Time go slowly by for both man, the watcher and the watch; while

for almost the first time in his life think have get hold of he,

“yet I mean to be kind to she. I do not want to be cruel. be I such a

brute that I cann’t help it? I have try to be gentle with she lately,

and she like I no well. she come back to tantalize I into love

no! even he couldn’t desert she!”



his lip quiver, and there come a choking feeling in his throat.



“thank Heaven I’m in time to stop she! she’ll have to stay with I now;

but she will find a way of make it more a punishment for I than for

now it be her turn to do what I like; and I sha’n’t be so soft about

what I wouldn’t do for she. no, that I wouldn’t! oh, good Heaven, will

she never love I? I’m not good enough for she; but I’m not such a cur

as that fellow!”



as the minute drag on, a hope begin to rise within he that she be

not come, after all, while he could see, to his joy, that the anxiety

of the man he be watch have grow keener. still they hear no sound,

though they listen intently, the one in hope, the other in deadly

at last Harry see Colonel Richardson turn his head quickly, as if his

but a minute later, through the fall snow, he see above he, swiftly

“I’ll shoot he like a dog!” think he.



and he step out from the shrubbery on to the pathway, draw out his

“I could not get away before; the boy be all over the house,” she

be whispering, deprecatingly.



his hand with the revolver drop to his side as he spring forward and

in a few stride reach they and drag she away.



“Lilian!”



       *       *       *       *       *



at twenty minute to twelve that night Annie be rouse from sleep by

knock at her door.



“what be it?” she cry, sleepily.



but the answer startle she into wakefulness.



“Annie, Annie, open the door, for Heaven’s sake!”



it be her husband’s voice, but hoarse, feeble, and break.



for one instant she pause. but there come another faltering knock, and

Harry’s voice again, more feebly still, call:



“Annie, Annie, let I in; I be die!”



she fly to the door, unlocked and open it; and Harry, his coat wet

with half-melt snow and cover with blood, stagger forward into

her arm.









CHAPTER XIX.





“let I stay here! don’t send I away!” be Harry’s first word, as

his wife lead he to a chair and support his head against her breast.



“yes, yes, you shall stay. oh, Harry, what have you do? you be

drench to the skin and cold as ice! where be you hurt? be it only

here?”



she touch his forehead, from a cut in which the blood be still

flow.



“that be all--I think,” say he, drowsily. “but I’m--cold.”



he be shiver violently. she ring the bell for assistance; but

it be too late to avert the consequence of that night’s work, and

before morning the fever be back upon he. it be impossible to

creep in. he do not know she--he do not know any one.

matter with you?”



Lilian do not answer for a minute, but stand watch the restless

movement of her sick brother; and, when she turn again to Annie, her

proud gray eye be full of tear.



“I may as well tell you now, for you be sure to learn it as soon as

poor Harry come back to his sense--if he ever do.”



she pause, and the other listen curiously for her confession, for a

confession she feel sure it be that she have to hear.



she be right; for Lilian go on:



“Annie, you must not despise the poor fellow any more. he can act like

a man if he cann’t speak like a professor. if it have not be for he, I

should have run away last night with--Colonel Richardson.”



“oh, Lilian!”



“don’t interrupt I,” go on the other hurriedly--“I may not

feel inclined for confession again. I be to meet he--Colonel

Richardson--at the low gate. well, Harry be there.”



“but how be that?”



“he think it be you who be go off.”



“I!”



“yes, yes, he do. I know whose do that be. Stephen have guess or

find out something, and not have the pluck to stop I himself, and

not want a general row, he get Harry to suppose it be you who be

go off with----”



“but Harry would never have believe that I----”



“why not?” say Lilian, in a hard tone. “have you return his

affection for you so very warmly as to make it impossible for he to

think that you care for any one but he? however, it be not for I

to reproach you, especially on the score of want of wifely devotion.

when he find it be I, Harry try to drag I away; but I struggle to

not be in time to save your character, but at least I will save your

speak again, Herbert--Colonel Richardson spring forward, snatch the

keep I back then; I have to support he; and I lead he back here, and

we slip into the house; and he beg I to bring he to your door,

and go to my room, and no one should know anything about it, if I would

promise never to try to go off again. I don’t promise--I haven’t time;

but I never will, all the same. and, Annie, he be worth love. do

try to love he back! oh, you would if you know what it be to have a

husband who be a monument of all the virtue, but a monument in stone!”



and the wayward woman, who, with all her fault, have generous impulse,

lay her beautiful head on the bed and sob.

Harry’s request.

nature be tie to cold one, who could not or would not respond

“who’s we?”



“you and I, of course.”



“and do you think I would live upon your money?”

“I think it be the one who have be use to it who should work, and

somehow,” he say at last.

“I see,” say Harry, turn upon she sharply. “all this time that I

“I will tell you what I do. I see that a piece be to be play at the

it seem to be come to you, you will let I go--you will let I

go--you will let I go!”



her violent excitement have bring the tear to her eye. as she kneel

beside the couch, her great, passionate dark eye fix upon his in

entreaty, the tear well up in his eye too as he snatch she into

his arm.



“I can refuse you nothing. Heaven forgive you--you will break my heart?”



a week later Annie’s trunk be pack for London. on the last day

before her departure from the Grange she take a long ramble by herself

through some of her favorite field and lane, where a mild March be

already bring forth the sign of spring. she have promise to be

a letter to read which she have have unopene in her pocket since the

smoke-begrime place indeed, but which bear a deceptive beauty when

“what be the matter, Annie dear?” he ask anxiously.

she have turn away to wipe the tear from her face, and, as she turn

she consider a minute while pretend to feel for it, and make up

there be a pause, but he be not satisfied.

Annie wince.

still she say nothing, but lean over the low battlemented wall of

Annie be not quite prepared for this. she answer, after a little

member--that be how I have hurt you; and it be just to turn I off

which Heaven know I don’t deny. I tell you again, I may be a brute and

a boor and anything else you like to make I out, but I’m not a fool;

and, when you tell I you dislike this Aubrey Cooke, I tell you you be

lie to I.”



Annie face he again very quietly.



“I have not lie. I tell you I dislike Aubrey Cooke when I be at the

Piccadilly. I tell you now that I have love he since then, and that

now I hate he. be you satisfied?”



the passion in her word be convincing, but Harry be not content. he

keep his gaze fix on the frank eye of his wife for a few moment,

then look away with a heavy sigh, murmur:



“hate he! that’s no good. I’d rather you do not care one way or the

Annie be touch. she have fully expect a violent outbreak on her

“Harry, you need not be frighten indeed; I shall never care for he

again.”



but Harry, without even try to detain her hand, shake his head.



“it be a very bad sign to hate a person,” say he. “I never hate any

and my darling, you be my wife, you know, and if you willn’t love I

ever, I may as well go and cut my throat, for I--i--I----”



he break off, fairly sob. Annie’s heart be move, and she hang her

“Harry, Harry, I’ll come back, I’ll come back--at Christmas; that be

only nine month, and if you love I still then, I will never go away

he press she to his breast, and kiss she and bless she; and as

be hope in her husband’s heart, and it have affect she a little. the

mellow sound of the organ be peal through the church where the

organist be practice as, at the bottom stair, Harry give his wife

a last passionate kiss before they leave the shadowy building for the

outer air.



and the next day Annie start for London.









CHAPTER XX.





the journey back to London be a very strange one to Annie; she never

see the landscape through which the train pass, she do not even

remember the face of her fellow-passenger afterward. her mind be

fill with fear for the future--for her own, for her husband’s, for

for Aubrey’s. she do hate he deeply, this man who have cheat she

into make she look upon he as the most gentle, most courteous,

bright of companion and the most devoted of friend, when he be

really nothing but a volatile, unprincipled flirt, who make love

be engage. perhaps Annie hardly dwell enough, in her blame of Aubrey,

on the question whether she herself have do right in conceal from

anxiety for he, and unselfish sorrow that he should have fall into

bad hand.



as for her own husband, she feel more kindly toward he now that she

be away from the daily irritation of his presence, from the fear of

she have promise to return to he, the nine month of hard work upon

would be.



she have catch an early and fast train from Beckham than the one

it be all her own fault that she be catch cold in an uncomfortable

she receive her husband’s farewell kiss that morning.



at last, after emit gust of black blinding smoke, each one of

Dusk be come on when she hear a knock at the sitting-room door.

she rise from her knee, go in to the sitting-room, and find herself

he be shy, nervous, and stand there without a word to say for himself.

“Mr. Cooke!” she cry, as she go forward and shake hand with he.

her face have recover its light, her eye be sparkle with their

“Miss Taylor tell I you be come, and my unfailing instinct tell

he give she a little box, which she open and find to contain fern

indeed for the moment she have forget that she be cold; but she

“but I be. now you shall see what it be to have a universal genius

but he would not let it go; so she resign herself to watch while

“you will look just like a sweep when you have finish,” say Annie,

“a little soap and water will remove all trace of the deed.”

“I have create two character, invent a new soup, write a book,

“of course not. one never marry one’s ideal. no; this be to be the

lovely of her sex. happily a man turn up at the last moment who

“no; I take up the cause of my sex against you.”

the sudden intensity of his manner would have force some show of

it be quite true that she would be dull and miserable by herself; she

he stand on one side to let they pass, but the fair young man send the

“I have the honor of be one of her old friend,” say Aubrey,

“by what right do you ask such a question which cannot concern you?”

“what then? why, my name be Harold Braithwaite!”

“I dare say it be,” say he, simply--“why not?”



“look here,” say the stranger, drop his voice till it become a

“I be no more anxious for a scene than you be, I assure you. but, as

“I don’t use her authority; I use my own.”



“and you think that will be enough for I?”



“I think it ought to be.”



“do you know who the lady be you be speak about so confidently?”



“yes. you know she as Miss Langton, the actress.”



“and you?”



“as Annie Braithwaite--my wife!”



Aubrey stand the shock well, but not too well for the other man to see

“be it true, then, that this man be your--husband?” ask Aubrey.

hat, he go down-stair.









CHAPTER XXI.





Annie stand with her husband at the top of the stair until she

hear the street door shut upon Aubrey Cooke; then, recover she

into her sitting-room.



Harry follow, and stand at first speechless with anger in the

“be you ready now?” he ask, simply.



“ready for what?” say she, impatiently.

“of course I can answer any question you please; but I don’t see the

but she be not quite at ease; this character of culprit be new to
