the bad for you and for he too.”



Annie’s gaze sink under the fierceness which blaze in his eye and

recall to her mind his old savagery at the Grange. he lower his

voice again as he see her shrink.



“Annie, don’t let I fancy you have anything to tell I bad than

I have think,” say he, with a tremor in his voice. “you need not

be afraid of I; I will listen calmly to whatever you have to say. I

haven’t always be a good husband to you, and I feel it quite as much

as you do. but I have be fond of you, and good to you lately, and you

might trust I a little, if only for the sake of that. now tell I! you

do like this Mr. Cooke, don’t you?”



“yes, of course I like he, or I should not let he come and see I.”



“and he like you?”



“if he do not, he would not take the trouble to come.”



“and if it have not be for my existence, I suppose----”



“you have no right to suppose anything,” say Annie, impatiently;

“there be nothing to suppose. you be the only person who have ever

find the slight fault with my conduct. there be no cause whatever

for your trifling jealousy, any more than there be at the Grange,

where you tease I to death with your absurd suspicion.”



“but you treat my jealousy differently then. it be trifle and

tiresome, I dare say. but you just laugh it off lightly then, while

now you grow impatient and restless under it.”



“you see I have be leave in peace lately, and be not consequently in

such a high state of discipline as when I be at the Grange. I should

have be well prepared if I have guess that your jealousy would

bring you up to town.”



“it be not my jealousy which bring I, Annie, but something which

I believe you care about just as little--my love. I get a letter from

you yesterday--you seem to have forget all about it, or perhaps you

write it just as a blind--I don’t know--and you say in it you often

think of the Grange, and you suppose by this time I could ride again

as well as ever, and have nearly forget all about such a trifling

thing as a wife. I get the letter at breakfast, and I say to myself,

‘the little jade be try to pique I! then she do care about

whether I forget she or not!’ and I make up my mind directly I’d come

and see you all unexpectedly, and see what you would say. and I don’t

make too sure you would be glad; but, by Jove, I don’t expect quite

such a cool welcome as I get!” and Harry’s voice give way just as he

reach the last word, and he lean his elbow on the mantel-piece and

drop his head into his hand, with his back to she.



Annie be touch, and she rise, with tear in her eye, and creep up

to he, and take his other hand. but he shake she off, and remain

quite unsoftened by her tearful eye.



“don’t come and hang about I now, Annie, and speak to I in your

cooing voice, when I know you wish I a hundred mile away, or I shall

think your caress be never worth have,” say he, passionately.

“and I think I could trust you; I think you be so good, so pure!

even when I be jealous, I never think you would pass yourself off

as an unmarried girl, just that you might be make love to by other

man--and when you know all the time how fond I be of you, Annie!”



“Harry, Harry, do listen to I! I be not fond of anybody else--I have

not be make love to. why willn’t you believe I? look at yourself in

the glass, and see if you be not more likely to please a woman’s fancy

than--than Mr. Cooke--or anybody.”



he have turn to look wistfully and reproachfully down at she, and she

have seize the opportunity to fasten herself coaxingly on his arm, and

to raise her other hand to his face to try to turn it toward the glass

over the mantel-piece.



Harry be not vain, and his own face have no particular attraction for

he; he give a glance at the reflection of the little white finger

which be hold his chin, and then he take her hand gently from his

face and look at she.



“I don’t set up for a beauty-man, and lot of the actor you meet be

handsomer than I, I dare say. but it be more than I can understand how

you could like an ugly, wash-out, long-nosed, lank-haired hunchback

like that fellow I meet outside! it be rather hard to be shunt for a

man who ben’t even straight!”



Annie wince under the speech; but she say:



“then how can you be so absurd as to be jealous of a man who

stoop--you, who be as straight as an arrow?”



“ah, my limb be all right; it be my head you complain of!” answer

poor Harry, pitifully. “I believe my heart be all right, too, only that

don’t seem to matter to you clever woman. I suppose that stoop

fellow can talk by the yard.”



“Mr. Cooke can ride and drive, too,” say Annie, quietly. “man who talk

well can do other thing, too, very often.”



“he can stick on a Park hack, or drive a dog-cart a couple of mile

without come to grief, I dare say,” return Harry, in a loud

voice. “but do you think he could break in an animal that have throw

every groom in the stable, or ride as straight as I can across country,

or train a racer?”



“I don’t suppose he be as much at home in a stable as you be,

certainly,” say Annie, coldly, “or that any of the actor I know be

so well able to beat a groom at his own work. I must do you so much

justice.”



“thank you. it be very clever of you to snub I like that; and I dare

say you think, if I have any proper pride, I ought to go away after you

have so plainly let I know how my vulgar stable-talk bore you. but I

sha’n’t,” continue Harry, doggedly. “I be foolish to let you go away

from I, and I be foolish to come after you; but, now I be here, I

mean to stop.” and he fling himself down into a chair.



“you mean to stay here!”



“yes; and, when I go away, I mean to take you with I.”



“oh, indeed! against my will?”



“I hope not--not if what you say to I a little while ago be true,

Annie;” and he lean forward on his elbow, with such wistful

earnestness in his face and voice that his wife be force to listen.

“you say you be not fond of anybody else, you say nobody else have be

make love to you, and you tell I I’m so handsome that I need not be

afraid of anybody else. well, if all that be true, and I’m such a nice,

good-look fellow, and you be so anxious to cling to my arm and

caress I and introduce I to your friend, why on earth, as soon as I

turn up, do you want to be rid of I again?”



“I don’t want to be rid of you. but I be not go to be treat like a

child, as if I could not be trust alone.”



“well, I don’t think any woman can, when she have a husband whose duty

it be to look after she.”



“oh, your opinion of a husband’s duty be not always so high, I think!”



“no, it ben’t. but I be all the more bound to fulfill it well now,

when I have neglect it so long. Annie, don’t be hard. why do you

come to I when I have got use to be without you, if you only mean

to show I what a brute I be, and then repulse I when I try, for

your sake, to be something well? you don’t know how you have hurt

I this afternoon by show I how sorry you be to see I again; I

don’t think I ever feel so knock over as when, after I have meet that

fellow and know who he be--for I’m not such a booby as you suppose,

and I know you like that ugly Maypole well than I--you just say

‘Harry!’ without a smile or the least sign of pleasure when you see I.

I feel as if you have stick a knife into I.”



he stop for a few moment, his voice all husky.



“and then see how good I’ve be to you! I’ve never even say a harsh

word to you, though I know many husband who would have say horrid

thing to their wife if they have catch they like that. but I swear to

to see I. I don’t know what make he guess you wouldn’t be; but I’ll

just punch his head for be so clever when I get back. and haven’t I

keep my word? if I have be so clever as these man you know, who can

do everything, I should have be sarcastic; and, instead of that, I

have let you be sarcastic, and I haven’t even swear at you;” and Harry

look up at his wife pleadingly, yet proudly, as if the force of

conjugal affection and manly self-restraint could no far go.



“Harry, indeed I be glad to see you, and sorry you be still so thin. I

should have tell you so long ago if you have let I. but you make such a

furious onslaught upon I at once.”



“very well then; we’ll let by-gone be by-gone, and you shall

come back with I, and we’ll be as happy as cricket,” say he,

affectionately, as he jump up from his chair and be on his knee

beside she, with his arm round she, in a minute.



“but, Harry, I cann’t do that. I be under an engagement now which I be

bind to fulfill. and, remember, we be not at all like happy cricket

when we be at the Grange together.”



“no, the Grange be a beastly old place, and nobody could be happy

there; I don’t wonder you get mope,” he answer, hastily. “now in

town it be different. there be so much to be do in London, such a lot

to be see, so--so many book and--and picture-gallery and pretty

dress and clever people.”



“but you don’t care for those thing, Harry.”



“yes, I do--at least, I shall when I’ve be with you a little while.

and I’ve quite take to reading, and----Oh, I shall get on capitally!”



“but what would you do without your dog and your horse, Harry?”



“do you think I cann’t get on without dog and horse,” say he

impatiently. “I suppose you think I cann’t be happy unless I be loaf

about a stable with my inferior--only you wouldn’t call they my

inferior!”



“how silly you be, Harry! when have I say anything like that to you?”



“you do only a few minute ago.”



“I do not mean it. I think it be a pity for you, who be devoted to

the life of a country gentleman, to give up all your pleasure just to

settle down to a life which would not suit you.”



“but it ben’t just for that, Annie; that be where you’re wrong. if I

care for nothing but the country, I should stay there. I can get on

without horse, though I be fond of ride and driving, as you know;

and I can get on without dog, though I miss old Ponto every other

minute; but I cann’t get on without you, Annie. I have try, but it be

no good; so, as you willn’t come into the country with I, I must come to

town to you.”



Annie be silent, more puzzle by, than grateful for, this devotion.

then she say, in a low voice:



“I cann’t accept such a sacrifice, Harry.”



“then will you come back to the Grange with I?”



“I cann’t do that. I have accept an engagement, and I must go through

with it.”



“what make you so much more particular about the engagement which

bind you to act so many time a week for a certain manager than about

the one you be under to I, your husband?”



“that be not fair. you allow I to make this engagement.”



“well, I don’t ask you to break it. all I ask be to let I stay with

you and take care of you.”



“but, Harry dear, you would be very uncomfortable here. the room be

so small and so shabby----”



“well, come with I to the Bingham Hotel; they have nice big room

there, and we shall be very comfortable.”



“but they be frightfully expensive!”



“never mind that. George fork out this morning; he have keep I very

short for a long time, so he give I a check, and tell I it be the

last I should see of his money--with a black look, to prevent the

pleasure from be too much for I. that be just like George, you

know.”



“but perhaps he mean it; and, if so, you ought to be careful.”



“so I will be careful, and you shall help I. I’ll give it all to you

to take care of as soon as I get it cash. fifty pound will last a

long time.”



“and before that be go I shall be earn a well salary than I ever

have in my life!” say Annie.



“but I sha’n’t live upon your money. do you think I would sponge upon

my wife? I be not go to give you a chance of despise I again.”



“then what will you do when the fifty pound be go?”



“write to George for some more, of course.”



“but suppose he could not or would not send you any more?”



“supposing the sky be to fall? go and pack up your trunk, my

darling, and we’ll go off and have a new honeymoon--only this time you

shall have a kind husband instead of a cross one!”



there be no resist he in his imperiously love mood; and Annie,

scarcely yet understand this new situation of affair, go, with

the husband’s hand gently push she, into the next room; and while

she be busily fill her trunk, she hear he ring the bell, order

the week’s bill and pay it; then he burst into the room, throw his arm

round she, and give she a huge hug as she be close the last box, and

whisper:



“this be tremendous fun--run away with one’s own wife!”









CHAPTER XXII.





Annie feel a curious and altogether new sensation as she submit

to be carry off to the hotel by her husband, whom success in this

small enterprise have restore to the happy and most affectionate of

humor. it be the first time since the early day of their marriage,

when the privilege have soon pal, that she have go about with he

as a protector, and the pride and pleasure in the position which he

ingenuously show, surprised and amuse she.



they be, as he have predict, very comfortable at the hotel. with

astonishing tact, Harry forebore to press his grievance against his

wife, and devote himself to banish the remembrance of the “clever

man who could do everything” by take she to theater and picture

gallery, and to the park, and to expensive dinner at the good

restaurant, with an assiduity which could not fail to touch she.

indeed Annie do not quite know what a happy passage in her life this

be until after it be over. she wish to be, and she think she

be, torment a little by self-reproach cause by her bad treatment

of Aubrey Cooke; but the feeling be not strong enough to outweigh

the delightful sense of repose she begin to feel in the consciousness

that she be surround by a great love. her husband be so watchful,

so affectionate, refrain so consistently from exact demand of

demonstrative fondness from she, that she have no time, no excuse for

such a sentiment as real regret. he insist, against her will, upon

take she to and from the theater to rehearsal, and ask she, when

she object, whether she be ashamed of he.



“if you be, say I be an old servant of the family,” say he, proudly.



but Annie silence he imperiously; and the confession she make in the

theater that she be marry, and that the handsome young fellow who

bring she backward and forward be her husband, while it bring down

upon she some accusation of coquetry, send she up in popular opinion

as the possessor of such a tall, well-breed-look lord and master.



Life have go on very smoothly in this way for nearly a week, and it

be the day before the opening night of “Nathalie,” when Harry, find

himself at the end of his ready money, think of change his check.



in the evening Annie notice that he be rather preoccupied during

dinner, and when she ask whether he have get some glove he have

promise she, he say he have not be able to get they yet, but she

should have they on the morrow.



“do you change the check, Harry? you say you would give the money to

I to take care of,” she suggest, laugh.



“no, I haven’t change it. the fact be,” he continue, see a look of

perplexity on his wife’s face. “George have overdraw his account a good

deal, and they willn’t cash such a big check until they have hear from

he.”



“but fifty pound be not such a very large sum; and your family have

bank there for year and year, I know. don’t it seem rather

strange, Harry, that they should refuse when they know you so well?”



this be a rather unfortunate suggestion, as the character of the

Braithwaite boy have not always stand high in money matter; but Harry

only say:



“oh, it will be all right, of course! I write to George this afternoon,

and I shall get an answer to-morrow or Monday. don’t you feel awfully

nervous about to-morrow night?”



“indeed I do. if it be not for the way you take I about and divert

my thought, I believe I should make myself ill by the way I worry

myself about it. I must go now; rehearsal begin at seven, so I shall

be want at half-past.”



this be the night rehearsal, on the eve of the production of the new

piece. for a week the manager have not sleep, weight with doubt about

the success of a performance which have cost he month of thought,

care and actual labor of body and mind. he be a popular man, and the

member of the company sympathize with he, though their own less

responsibility sit far more lightly upon they, and the green-room during

the last rehearsal, when doubt far outweigh hope regard the

piece they be all at work upon, ring with laughter as the foremost

wit of the company make cruel joke upon the “governor” and his

trouble.



the next morning, just as Annie be start for the last rehearsal of

all, a telegram come for her husband. he read it and thrust it into his

pocket without any remark and without any offer to show it to she. she

be getting use to quiet self-reliance on her husband’s part: but this

action surprise she.



“from George?” she ask rather diffidently.



“yes. Stephen be come up this morning to see I, with statement from

George--not very cheerful one, I fancy. but don’t trouble your head

about that, darling, or you will be unfit for to-night. we shall pull

through right enough, never fear!”



“why, I be not nearly so anxious as you be, Harry! I shall get my

salary next week--six guinea--and then, if we only live a little more

economically, we shall get on splendidly.”



“yes, yes; it will be all right. there be the hansom outside. I must

send you alone this morning, my darling, for I must stay at home to see

Stephen when he come. good-bye--good luck to you, Annie.”



he put she carefully into the hansom, give her hand a tender

lover-like little squeeze as he help she in, and go back into the

hotel for his cigar-case, to pass away the time with a cigar as he

walk up and down outside, wait for his cousin.



when she return from rehearsal, in a hansom by Harry’s order, she

find Stephen wait outside the hotel to receive she. he be look

pale and anxious, and she ask he hurriedly what be the matter.



“come in and I’ll tell you,” say he.



he lead she into the coffee-room, which be empty.



“you have bad news, Stephen, I be sure! what be it? where be Harry?”



“he tell I to break it to you. he have quite give way under it. you

will try not to be very much shocked, willn’t you? it be about George.

He----”



“not dead?” whisper she, white to the lip.



“oh, no; he be quite well! but he have smash up.”



“poor fellow!” say she sympathizingly, but much relieve. “be he

really quite ruin?”



“yes, I be afraid so; he have be in difficulty for a long time now,

you know. the Grange will have to be sell, of course; but it and the

land be so heavily mortgaged that that willn’t relieve he much. he have

expect the crash for a long time, and Wilfred and I have some notion

of it too; but Harry never dream of such a thing, and it have knock

he over altogether.”



“but why do he take it so much to heart? he will be well off than

anybody now I’ve get such a good engagement.”



“it seem he want to persuade you to give up act and go and live

with he at the Grange; he tell William and I so just now.”



“William!”



“yes: I bring he up with I, and he be with Harry now, unless Harry

have turn he out of the room; for, when your husband say you be

grow fond of he, William say that be nonsense, and I have a lot of

trouble in get they to leave each other alone.”



“but it be true, and it be very wrong of William to contradict he.

he have be very kind to I, and I be quite glad that at last I can do

something for he.”



“frankly, Annie, I don’t think he’ll let you. he be very obstinate,

you know, when once he get an idea into his head; and he have take to

think that it would be beneath his dignity to live on your earning.

and really, you know, I think he be right.”



“but how be he to live any other way?”



“I don’t know, I be sure; I think that be what be bother he, and

the thought that he will have to leave you.”



“but he mustn’t do that.”



“then you have well go and tell he so; he have be cry about that.

he say, just as you be begin to like he, all his work be undo

again, and you will call he a loafer.”



“I will go to he,” say Annie; and she leave Stephen, and go

up-stair to her sitting-room, where William rush at she directly she

open the door.



she see that Harry be lie on the sofa, with his face in the

cushion; but she could not get at he at once, for “the child” be

expressive grimace, to intimate that his brother be in a bad temper

and have well be leave to the solitary enjoyment of it.



“he will only snap at you,” whisper he, as Annie push past he

gently and go toward the sofa; and William, with his soft whistle,

go out of the room.

kiss he without a word.

long that it be come; but William be provide for, as your uncle in

Ireland be look after he, and Stephen have a little money of his own

and Lilian be all right, and you and I will have plenty of money next

week.”



but Harry bounce up from the sofa at this point say that it be

luncheon-time, and she must be starve after her long rehearsal; and

ten minute later they, with William and Stephen, be sit together

at table, try to divert their thought from their gloomy prospect

by talk of the piece Annie be to play in for the first time that

night.



as soon as luncheon be over, Harry insist upon make his wife lie

down to get some rest before the exciting duty of a “first night”

begin. Sleep be out of the question for she; she lie repeat

the word of her part, which she have know for week, in a fever

of unnecessary anxiety, lest the word should slip from her memory

at the last, or lest, in the excitement of the all-important first

performance, she should hurry her speech unduly--a fault to which she

be prone.



Harry softly open the door from time to time and creep in, sometimes

without she even hear he. he always find she engage in the

same way, softly go over her line to herself, and each time he

retreat, look harass, and rather disappointed.



they have dinner early, for she have to be at the theater at half past

seven. Harry go with she, and, as they drive along together in

she notice that he be shake like a girl.



“you be not well, Harry,” she say, anxiously. “what be the matter

with you?”



“it be only about you,” say he, in a low voice.



“oh, I shall be all right; through all my excitement I feel sure of

that! why, you be more nervous for I than I be for myself! look here,

Harry--I be sure you be not well; the shock you have this morning have

be too much for you. don’t come for I to-night--indeed, there be no

need; I will send for a cab and come back as safely as possible.”



rather to her surprise, he say quickly, as he help she out of the

hansom:



“yes, yes, that will be the good; I be not very well, I think William

shall bring you home.”



he have pay the fare, and they have reach the stage-door together.

two of the actor be outside, and they raise their hat and begin

speak to Annie. without pause in her talk, she give her hand

lightly to her husband, as he stand there still, anxious to be with she

as long as he could. she feel again that his hand be tremble, and

she turn to he to say:



“don’t watch the piece, Harry; it will make I more nervous than ever

some slip.”

to wear that night, she raise the heavy white rose and the sweet

stephanotis to her lip before she fasten they in the front of the

cream-white muslin dress in which she be first to appear.



the audience at the fashionable comedy theater be not, as a rule,

demonstrative; but, when Annie come off the stage, after her good

scene that night, she know that she have make a “hit.” it be the first

distinct, noteworthy success of her career, and her heart beat fast

the future seem to be clear before she. she do not for a moment

think she have get to the top; she know quite well that struggle and

some failure lay still in her path; but that a good beginning toward

a prosperous artistic career have be make be a fact which set the

blood tingle in her vein and bring the fierce light of hopeful

ambition into her dark eye, when, her share in the work of the evening

which she have come to the theater, and go down from her dressing-room

to the green-room to wait for the end of the performance and the final

verdict of the first-night audience upon the piece.



it be a favorable one; and Annie find her way to the stage-door, on

her way out, with congratulation ring in her ear and the knowledge

that, as certainly as certainty be possible in theatrical matter, the

long week of anxious and tedious rehearsal be to be reward by a

calm and prosperous run of the new piece.



at the door she find William dance about, having be with

difficulty restrain by the hall doorkeeper from rush through the

door which lead on to the stage. he drag her arm through his, and

in high glee help she into the hansom, and, as he fling himself in

“oh, Annie, you be splendid, you be immense! I don’t think you

could act like that. it ben’t like act at all, I’m sure, the way

you take that toffee! oh, well, it be just like life, just like the

way you use to go on with I at the Grange! poor, old Grange! I wonder

if I shall ever see it again?”



“I use to think of you sometimes at rehearsal, when I come to that

bit. be Harry sit with you?”



“yes; he nearly go off his head. he keep say, ‘ben’t she perfect?

ben’t she lovely?’ and I have to keep he from jump up two or three

“dear old boy! how nice of he! I be so glad he be pleased with I.”



“well, I don’t see much merit in that. he couldn’t help be proud of

you when all the people about be say how good you be. if he have

be a decent sort of husband, he would have wait himself to take you

home, instead of tell I to do so and prance off himself goodness

know where.”

“no; he know well than to tell I, because I should have just give

he a bit of my mind about it; but I’ve no doubt he’s go off to

“William, how dare you talk like that? do you know you be speak

about my husband?”



“oh, yes, of course I know! why, Annie, you be not really angry, be

and make himself quite ill between his nervousness for I and his grief

over the shock he have this morning, you take the first opportunity of

abuse he to I, his wife.”



“but, Annie, you haven’t grow fond of Harry, have you?” say William,

with pity and fear in his voice.



“yes, I have--very fond. I couldn’t help it,” sob Annie,

apologetically. “he have be so kind to I lately.”



“poor girl!” say “the child,” with compassion. “never mind; you will

soon get over it when he leave you alone again, and you be full

of your success on the stage, and people will crowd round you and

compliment you and tell you what a great actress you be, and----”



“when he leave I again? what do you mean?”



“why, he will, Annie--on some excuse or other, he will. he will never

stay quietly in London without anything to ride. I know Harry.”



but she be too indignant to let he go on, and for the rest of the

drive she maintain a frigid attitude of offend dignity toward her

indiscreet brother-in-law; and she repulse he freezingly when he

try to kiss and be friend.



on reach the hotel, she run quickly up-stair, anxious to find

her husband and prove to William that she have not overestimate his

devotion. but in neither room be he to be find. on her dressing-room

table, however, she discover a note direct to she in her husband’s

handwriting. she tear it open; but she be for some minute too much

excited and frighten to read it. it run as follow:



 “MY DARLING ANNIE,--I donot know whether you will think I be do

 something very wrong and cruel or whether you willnot care a straw. I be

 go away though I love you with all my heart just as much as ever

 and it hurt I awfully not to say goodby to you even but if I do I

 know you would ask I to stay and I cannot do that and be a loafer and

 live on your money you would be quite right to despise I if I do

 and say I be nothing but an idler like I use to be. I be go to

 work. I donot know quite what I be go to do but I cannot try any

 what be call gentlemanly occupation because you know I would be so

 bad at they but I will make some money somehow and I willnot steel it I

 promise you that but you must not be too particular how I make it as

 long as it be honestly--will you now. I have pay the bill and go

 to your old lodging and pay the rent for a week and the glove will

 be send tonight and I leave you all the money I can to go on with. I

 be go to see you act tonight and I know you will be successfull

 becaus you be so clever and so pretty and all the man will try to

 turn your head but donot let they my darling Annie becaus all the time

 I be work for you and mean to get rich for you--and now you see I

 love you so and only go away becaus I do. I think you will try not to

 like _anybody_ else--but to think how I can be nice to you and make

 you happy even though I be not clever like some of the man you know.

 I donot know exactly where I be go just at first--but if you write

 to I and give your letter to Stephen I shall get they and he will

 let you know how I get on. you will not see I again yet because it

 would knock I over just at first and I know you would not like what

 I be go to do and you might talk I out of it. but I shall see you

 very often you may be sure as long as I have a shilling to go in the

 gallery with



  “your ever love husband



                                                                “HARRY.”



she find a sovereign inside the letter and the hotel-bill receipt.

she do not cry, but go into the sitting-room where William be

wait for she.



“Annie, you be ill! you be so white! you have overexcite yourself.

sit down and let I get you some brandy-and-water.”



“no, no, I be not ill. you be quite right, William; Harry have leave I

already.”



the young fellow stand before she, shocked, silent.



“never mind, Annie, you have your old brother,” say he, as soon as

he could speak, in a soothe voice. “perhaps, after all, it be good

that he should go soon, before you have got use to he, and might have

miss he. now I have an idea, Annie, that we might be very happy if

you and I be to take a cottage--now we be poor, it willn’t run to more

than a cottage--and you might keep house for I, as lot of sister do

for their brother; and of course I couldn’t be always at home because

of my military duty very soon,” say he, proudly, “but I could be

always run down there, even when I be away, and we should be so

jolly together.”



“my dear William; what be you think about? I be not really your

sister, you know, and such an arrangement wouldn’t be think proper.”



“Annie, I be afraid--I begin to think--you be really fond of Harry.”



“yes, William,” say poor Annie, while the tear roll down her

cheek, “I be afraid--I begin to think--I be.”









CHAPTER XXIII.





Annie wake the next morning with a dull, uncomfortable sense of having

receive a great blow which quite counterbalance the ecstasy of she

first stage-success. she reason with herself over this feeling,

but could not argue it away. she have indeed suffer two shock

yesterday--the news of George’s ruin and the threaten sale of the

Grange in the morning, and the letter which announce her husband’s

departure at night. but the first be an event which have long be

impending, and George himself could scarcely be more unhappy, now the

crash have come, than he have be during those long month when he have

feel that ruin be hang over he; and, as for the last, a week

ago there have be no event she have so much dread as the possible

appearance of her husband in London. it could not be that she be so

weak-minded as to have change in a week from dread her husband’s

presence to desire it. certainly Harry have be most surprisingly

nice, good-temper, and kind, quite different from the bear he use

to be at the Grange; she have catch herself turn to he for an

opinion now and then, lead away by the authority he have somehow assume

less imbecility than her former contempt for his ignorance have lead she

to expect. but then this state of thing could not have go on much

long in any case; such a very new phase as Harry’s angelic patience

would surely never have last more than another day or two, and the

reaction would probably have bring on a terrible fit of savagery.



“yet I wish he have stay till then,” she think, regretfully. “he do

not seem to have grow tired of be nice to I, and he be so very

sweet while it last. I don’t think I be ever happy than I be last

week, in spite of the fatigue and anxiety of rehearsal. I wonder where

he be? I dare say I should be very much disgusted if I know. after a

week of no society but mine, I should think he must be pine for some

groom or coachman to talk to. very likely he be enjoy himself in

some stable at this minute.”



but she little think how shrewd a guess she have make.



in a wistful and restless state of mind she go back to the apartment

in which her husband have find she. what few friend she have begin to

her success in “Nathalie.”



this recognition of her talent be very pleasant; but it just miss

the reason of this slight disappointment, it occur to she that there

be one person who ought to have hasten forward with the rest of her

acquaintance to offer she the natural matter-of-course homage of a few

complimentary word upon the hit she have make in the new piece. this

person be Aubrey Cooke.



she have not see he since that unlucky meeting with her husband; and,

though, in the few bright busy day she have pass with Harry, she have

Harry’s inopportune appearance.



why do he not come to see she again, and give she an opportunity of

explain her silence concern her marriage? he have let fall no

be under any engagement or not. be he irretrievably offend? if he

Annie feel so entirely heart-free that no further scruple about Harry’s

receive he so often; and, now that her other friend and acquaintance

dependent upon he for companionship. so she write a note to he, as

she have often do before, ask he to meet she at the “store,” and

help she with her shopping. she do not expect an answer, for these

little civilly entreat note he always take as command, and she

know he would look upon it as an appointment. so, when she arrive at

the “store” the next day, she be not at all surprised, or in any way

agitated to find he there wait for she.



but she have be but very few minute in his society before she notice

dignity in his manner, no coldness in his tone; but now she begin to

cleverly; but Annie feel frightened, and, while he give she no further

loophole for offense, she be constrain in her manner and dismiss

he as soon as she could.



she know what she have do, that the discovery of her deceit about her

marriage have change Aubrey Cooke’s estimate of she, and that he have

receive this last note, write, as he must have find out, after the

departure of her husband, in a very different spirit from the frank

_camaraderie_ with which he have respond to her former appeal to he

to come and help she with her marketing. she know that she have deserve

this severe wound to her self-respect, and she go home miserable and

ashamed.



but this difficulty be not yet over. at the theater a beautiful

bouquet be bring to she with a note--a lover-like note--from Aubrey.

she tear up the note, and give the flower to the dresser. but on the

follow night she receive another bouquet, another note; and on

the third night, this attention having be again repeat, she get a

little tease by one of her fellow-actor, who know Aubrey and have see

other bouquet of his and other note.



she go home mad with shame and anger, and write Aubrey a curt note,

ask he to call upon she; and when the next day the time she have

but he be experience in flirtation as well as in love, and he have far

“I get your note only just in time, Mrs. Braithwaite: I be go down

to Kirby Park to see some horse a friend of mine have in training

there.”



“I be sorry if my note interfere with your day’s arrangement. you

should have send I a line to say you be engage.”



“I be never engage when you send for I. you must know that by this

time.”



Annie raise her head haughtily, while he continue:



“it be more than eight month now since you tell I I be the only

person in the world you could depend upon, and I have never fail you

yet.”



this allusion be embarrassing, and Annie could only murmur:



“you have always be very kind.”



“and you have put my kindness to some hard test, haven’t you? you have

snub I, you have confide in I--at least you appear to do so:

you have encourage I to love you----”



“no, Mr. Cooke; I be quite innocent of any such intention.”



“then your innocence serve you well than any coquetry could have

do, Mrs. Braithwaite. having innocently encourage I to love

you, you innocently allow I to tell you so, with only such vague

suggestion of ‘an obstacle’ as serve to make I more anxious to win

with far more reason I might summon you--if you be not a woman and

therefore above law of justice and humanity--to account for your

against I.”

she have indeed be innocent of the depth of the emotion she have

“Annie, Annie, never mind what I have say! I do not want to be harsh,

only to let you know--what I ought to have keep from you, I suppose.”

advantage which his remorse at having cause her tear give she.

you don’t tell I you be marry--because you think it would hurt

I, and you hate he, and want to forget his existence. well, you

be quite right; I could see at a glance that he be an ill-temper

brute, and that you be afraid of he.”



“he be not ill-temper,” flash out Annie, with sudden fire. “and all

that I be afraid of be that he willn’t come back to I, that some one

himself before I can let he know it be untrue.”



Aubrey be silent for some minute. he detect in this speech the ring

“no, I won’t--I cann’t, Mrs. Braithwaite!”



“do forgive I, Aubrey!”



he hold out only one second long, then take her little hand and

her fault be punish she bitterly now. she throw herself upon the

she have deserve every pang which could torment she. she have trifle

she and treat she as she deserve.



when the first excess of her grief and humiliation be over, her

surprised at the persistency with which her thought return to the

husband whom she have so dislike and despise at the time when no

self-reproach at the fault in her own conduct have rise to disturb

the placid superiority she feel over he.



she have begin to fret herself into a fever of anxiety at the thought

that she would never hear from he again, when, on her return home

gentleman be in her sitting-room.



“they do not come together, ma’am. the lady come first, and presently

the gentleman; and, when they hear you be out, they both say they

would wait for you. so I show they both up-stair, ma’am.”



in the sit-room Annie find Stephen, whom she have rightly guess

to be one of the visitor, and Muriel West, whom she certainly neither

expect nor wish to see.



this lady, whose coarseness have in the very first day of their forced

Miss West have dye her hair a new color since their last meeting, but

the dye be wear off; her face be thin and ghastly, her glove

appearance suggestive of hard time and even of scanty fare. she

“ah, Miss Langton, you’re up, and I’m down! I hardly dare to come and

call upon such a howl swell as you have become. you be not sorry to

see an old friend though, I see.”



“I be very sorry to see you look so ill, though,” say Annie,

sincerely. “you use not to look like that in the country. you want

change of air.”



“no, no, my dear; you’re wrong there. no actress want change of air

when once she’s get to London. it’s an engagement I want. I’ve be

out for six week, and see no prospect of be in again. I don’t know

whether you can help I; but I’ve come to ask your advice on one or two

matter.”



“I will come in and see you presently, Annie,” say Stephen, go

toward the door. “I have nothing much to say to you, and I come chiefly

to see whether you have any commission for I.”



“yes, yes, I have! I have a letter for you to take, and I want to see

you most particularly. come back and have tea with I, will you?”



he promise to do so; and Annie, who be die to hear all he have to

tell she about her husband, be oblige reluctantly to let he go, and

to listen instead to the long list of grievance and complaint against

London manager and thing in general which Miss West proceed to

entertain she with in language much strong than be necessary.



Annie have notice upon her first entrance that Stephen and Miss West

engross by his companion. he now turn with eagerness to she again,

return. but Annie do not invite Miss West to stay to tea. so he leave,

cast at the very last moment an ardent and expressive glance at the

object of his evident admiration.



the two woman have not be many minute alone together before Annie

chance she have pine for, while Miss West, who she feel be really the

an opportunity of distinction, as she herself have so long be.



Miss West have not be go more than a few minute when Stephen

return, and Annie ask anxiously for news of Harry, which his cousin

“cann’t you tell I where he be and how he be, Stephen?” she ask

“I cann’t tell you where he be, because he be travel about, and I

don’t know myself where he be at this moment. but he be quite well, and

I haven’t see he in such good spirit for a long time.”



“oh,” say Annie, her face fall involuntarily. “I be very glad to

“oh, dear, no! you know Harry hate town; he be not like the same man

now he have get back into the country again, and to--”



here Stephen pull himself up short and Annie say quietly, with

tighten lip:



“go on, Stephen. Harry be happy now he have get back to--what?”



“oh, I only mean the country air and the country people! you know he

be a regular rustic, and londoner don’t suit he.”



Annie gulp down the tear this unlucky speech bring to her eye,

and say, with forced cheerfulness:



“yes, he be, of course, much happy in the country.”



“of course,” admit Stephen, guardedly. “he have send you this letter.”



she tear it open. it be only a short note, very affectionate, but with

no definite word concern his own movement. a sudden impulse of

answer.



they be not long over tea, as Stephen seem anxious to get away, and

Annie herself be late for the theater. when he have go, she dress

the end of the second street she have to pass through, she see Stephen

and Miss West stand in earnest conversation. she have to pass they;

but they be too much absorb in what they be say to notice she

approach.



when she be near to they, she hear Stephen say bitterly:



“of course you like Harry well than I, because he’s such a tall,

straight, handsome fellow!”



“Handsome be that handsome doe. I like he because he like I. you

tell he so, give he my love, and say he’ll see I before very long

if he’s a good boy;” and Miss West, with a laugh and a roguish glance,

hurry away; and Stephen, without turn round to see Annie, follow

slowly in the same direction.



Annie walk on steadily, with the hot tear burn in her eye.



this be what Harry’s desertion mean; and this coarse woman, whom she

have just be assist, be the enchantress who hold his heart for the

time.



“what an idiot I be to imagine for a moment that he be capable of

lasting affection, and for his wife! I will never think about he

again!”



but she think about he all the way to the theater, and cry herself

to sleep over her dislike of he and her contempt for he.









CHAPTER XXIV.





the nightly duty Annie have to perform at the theater be all that

save she from a serious illness, as the result of the acute misery

she suffer for some time after the eventful day on which the

discovery of her husband’s faithlessness have succeed to Aubrey’s

reproach. how wise she now feel herself to have be in mistrust

the profession of affection which Harry have make on his recovery, in

the hope of induce she to remain at the Grange until his pass

fancy for her society be quite wear out! if she have yield to his

entreaty, she would have lose the chance she have have in “Nathalie,”

and would have be now entirely at the mercy of her careless husband,

who have take the first pretext he could find for free himself from

the restraint of her society, and, under the pretense of work for

she, return to more congenial companionship--perhaps to that of

Susan Green, the blacksmith’s daughter. and he have be so lose to all

sense of decency as to use the same messenger to she and to Muriel West.



Annie be wise now than she have be when she first come to London

alone, after the few miserable month of wed life which have end

in such a terrible fiasco at the Grange. then she have give way to

grieve in secret over the wreck of her life; but now, with the

philosophy which come of a riper knowledge of the world, she hide

away her regret as well as she could, and throw herself into the

life around she, which present many attraction to the rise young

actress.



all her effort to find out any of the member of her husband’s family

be unavaile. she could not leave town, or she would have return

to Beckham, to see if any of they be haunt the old place yet.

she hear from William; but he be in Ireland, and have hear nothing

certain about the movement of the rest. she want to know how George

have bear the crash, and what have become of Wilfred, and whether the

shock have sober he. but she be force to wait until Stephen, who

have give she no address that she could write to, should again call and

fulfill his promise of keep she informed at least concern her

husband’s health.



she have begin to wonder whether he have forget all about it, or

whether Harry have forbid he to hold any further communication

with she, when Stephen make his appearance in her sitting-room one

afternoon, look very haggard and unhappy.



“how ill you be look, Stephen! you have not be take proper care

of yourself. have Lady Braithwaite see you lately, or Lilian?”



“Lilian wouldn’t care if she do,” he answer, sullenly. “all she

care for be herself and her own comfort; and, when that be secure,

all the rest of the world may get on as it can.”



from which speech, and still more from the way in which it be

deliver, Annie come to the conclusion that the lame man’s infatuation

for his cousin be at an end. his release do not seem to have make he

any the happy, however, and it be evident from his appearance that

he be in a deplorable state of ill-health.



“you have bring I news of Harry?” she ask, presently, when she have

make he rest on the sofa and bring he a cup of tea.



“yes; but there be not much to tell. he be get on, but he have not

write this time.”



“not write! why be that? he might surely have send I a few line by

you, if he do not choose to write by the post. I have be expect

to hear from he every day for at least a week. Stephen,” she go on

earnestly, draw her chair nearer to the sofa, and speak with

all the soft persuasion she could put into her voice, “there must be

some reason for this--some reason that you know and can tell I if

you choose. do let I know what it mean, Stephen. you would not keep

anything from I that I ought to know, would you? I be sure you could

not be so cruel. he be ill, and you don’t like to tell I so.”



“no; he be quite well--upon my honor he be! it be only that he be not

get on so fast as he wish to, and he be too despondent just now

to write.”



“but how do he live? I be sure he have no money, and he be use, poor

fellow, to have it for the asking.”



“no, indeed--it take a good deal of ask, and of a very pressing

kind, to get money out of George lately. but it be always difficult for

a man with no capital to get on.”



“look here, Stephen. I have some money that I have save; you must take

it. if Harry willn’t have it when he hear it be mine, you must tell he

it be his share of the proceed of the sale at the Grange. poor old

Grange! I read about the sale the other day. I cann’t think what have

change Harry so much; he use not to be overproud in money matter,

and now he be as tiresome as possible the other way. tell he any story

you like, so that you make he take it.”



“I sha’n’t be able to, Annie. he be a great deal sharp than you

think, and he would guess who send it directly.”



“you must say nothing about it for a few day, as he will know you have

just see I. but in about a week you can spring it upon he suddenly,

and he will be off his guard by that time and believe you. now don’t

raise any more objection, for you must take it; and I can spare it

quite well. I know you be a man of property,” say she, laugh--for

Stephen have a little money of his own--“and would be offend if I

offer to lend you money; but, if you ever should want ‘a little

check,’ you must remember that I, too, be a person of property now--at

least, as long as my engagement last; and I have just sign for

another two year at a high salary.”



and, before he go away, she put into his hand a little packet

contain ten pound, which he take reluctantly, bind by a solemn

promise not to let Harry know whom it come from. she send a little note

to her husband, too, beg he to write to she, tell he all about

the renewal of her engagement, cheer he by all the encouraging

word she could think of, entreat he not to despond if he be not

immediately successful in the work, whatever it might be, which he have

take up, and say all that a wife could think of to a well husband

than Harry. she refrain from sneer or sarcasm, for she have make up

her mind to take her husband as he be, to do her duty as his wife as

well as he would let she; and she try to throw all her thought and

all her hope into her own career, so that she might escape from the

regret which would arise in moment of depression at the thought that

no home happiness would ever be possible for she.



that week, during which Harry have devote himself to prove that

happiness be possible for they together, have leave deep result

than he guess; he have pay she back in her own coin for tantalize

he during his convalescence by a kindness which be not mean to

be more than a temporary effort. it be not for some time that the

thought flash into her mind that this have be a deliberately plan

revenge on his part for her obstinate refusal to stay at the Grange

with he. such a refinement of vengeance do not seem in keep with

Harry’s character; yet it seem scarcely more improbable than the wild

inconsistency of love she devotedly one week and be perfectly

happy without she the next.



she try to solve the problem by acute questioning of Stephen when

she see he next; but he be more cautious and reticent than ever,

seem uneasy under the fire of her inquiry, and she soon see that

a continuance of they would only result in his have recourse to

falsehood in reply. so she have to content herself with learn that

Harry have take the money; but she understand from what his cousin say

that he be in want of more, and with ready generosity she send he all

the rest of her saving.



“be you sure you can spare all this?” ask Stephen, uneasily, as he

stand hesitatingly with the money in his hand.



“quite, quite sure. you need not look so downcast about take it,”

say she, laugh. “you be to tell Harry I have plenty, and whenever

he want more you have only to come to I.”



“tell Harry?”



“oh, don’t he know it be from I?”



“no, no; I do not dare to tell he! you tell I not to. he would not

have take it.”



“you be quite right. I have forget. well, say that George have

some more for he, and will give it to he when he want it. or

stay! couldn’t you say it come from Lady Braithwaite?” ask Annie,

brightly, more pleased than she know to find that her husband be still

too proud to accept money from her hand.



“he would not believe that. my aunt have only just enough to live upon.”



“Lilian?”



“Lilian be abroad. I don’t know whether she have hear anything about it

yet.”



“well, say what you like, as long as you make he take it.”



“and you be quite sure the want of it will not inconvenience you?”



“you be as sensitive for Harry as he be for himself. look at the

luxury I be surround by,” and Annie point gayly to the bouquet

and fruit on the table. “don’t all this speak for itself? the money,

you understand, come from somebody else; but you may take he this

from I;” and with nervous, tremble finger she pull out from their

companion a spray of jasmine and a crimson azalea, fasten they

together, and put they into his hand as he leave the room.



“I be afraid that poor fellow be go to die,” she think, as she

listen to his slow footstep and the thud of his crutch upon the

stair; “I never see he look so ill as he do to-day. I wonder where

he live? he cannot be in want--I know he have money enough to keep he,

and Harry even, with the money I send he, would have enough for they

both. poor fellow!”



she and Stephen have never be very good friend--indeed at the

Grange he have dislike she, and she have never feel for he any warmer

sentiment than pity, mingle with contempt for the slavish nature of

his devotion to Lilian. his unselfish worship of the cold, proud girl

have its nobler side, she know; but she could not forgive the meanness

of the action to which he would stoop for his cousin’s sake. but, now

that Lilian have cast he aside like an old glove, and he appear

before Annie break in health and forlorn, the tear come into her eye

as she think of his waste life, and she would have do anything in

the world to smooth his rough lot for he by her sympathy or her care.

but he shrink from both, and leave she each time deject but stubborn,

with the shy reserve which characterize his attitude toward most

people even more marked than usual in his conversation with she.



she be feel rather heart-sick at her inability to do anything for

the member of her husband’s family, from most of whom she have receive

great kindness, when one day she see Sir George get out of a hansom

in Piccadilly. he be look careworn and harass, Annie think; but

he seem glad to see she; and, when she beg he to come to luncheon

the next day he say he should be delighted, but she must be prepare

to find he more of a bore than ever.



“well, if you bear I, I shall take the privilege of an old

acquaintance and go to sleep,” say she, laugh.



the next day he appear punctually in her sitting-room, and she be

even more struck than she have be on the previous day by the deep

line in his handsome face and the cloud which seem to hang over

he. she exert herself as she have never do before to be lively and

amusing; she have prepare the daintiest of luncheon, and before it be

over she have the satisfaction of hear he laugh like a man without

a care. not a particle of this delicate welcome be lose upon the keen

man of the world, and, when luncheon be over, he say:



“that be the first meal I have laugh over for more than two

month--since you leave the Grange, in fact.”



“be it?” say Annie, carelessly, as she refill his glass.



“yes; and I suppose you know that as well as I do. you have the _ar

celare artem_, like the accomplished actress you be off as well as

on the stage; but I know you inveigle I here to-day with the base

intention that your wit and your wine should get into my head, and make

I forget for a little while my care and my difficulty.”



“and, if wit and wine never fulfil a bad mission than that, they

would not be so ill speak of,” say Annie, gently.



“well say! why do you leave we, Annie? you be the good genius of

the Grange, and I be almost ready to think that, if you have never leave

it, we should all be there still.”



“that’s right. put all the blame upon a defenseless woman.”



“I be glad you be not there at the end; it be a miserable time.”



he be so deeply serious that Annie grow serious too.



“do you think I would have go if I have know what be come? oh,

George, you cannot think so ill of I!”



“it be well for you that you do go then; you could not have

prevent the crash. I have know it must come from the time my father

die. it have be nothing but wave after wave of difficulty, and

get through or over they somehow ever since. I suppose it would

have be well to give up long ago; but we be so hedge in on every

side that the ruin be bind to be complete when it do come, and you

be just the sort of woman to understand the feeling which force one,

with or against one’s will, to fight it out to the end, and stave off

the fall into a break-down swell as long as possible.”



“George, George, how can you use such an absurd term? you, with your

pluck, your patience!”



“I’ve use they all up, Annie, in the one tussle.”



“then you must let they grow again, and go in for another tussle. you

be young, and have courage and energy. if I be you, I would never

rest until I have buy back the Grange.”



“I don’t believe you would!” say George, admiringly, as he watch the

proud flashing of her eye and the vary expression of her face. “but

I be not like that. I could fight on doggedly for something which be

be drag away from I; but I haven’t it in I to begin a battle on

my own account.”



“then what do you mean to do?”



“I shall get some appointment where I can grow gray with

respectability; my people can manage that, and they will. it be a

scandal for a baronet to starve, you know. why, you silly child, you

be cry! thank Heaven, Annie! I don’t think you be so fond of I.”



“I’m not fond of you--I’m disgusted with you!” say Annie, fiercely,

stiffen herself rigidly as he lean toward she. “why, do you know

that even Harry show more spirit than that?”



“what make you say ‘even Harry?’” ask Sir George, quietly. “I could

have tell you long ago that Harry have pluck and spirit enough for six,

in spite of his impossible manner and boorish conversation. if anybody

buy back the Grange, it will be he.”



Annie listen with her cheek tingle.



“when do you first begin to think all that of he?” she ask, in a

low voice.



“I know, when we be lad together, that there be something in he;

but I own I lose sight of the fact while he lead his loose, lazy life at

the Grange after you have leave he. but, when you leave the Grange this

last time--more than two month ago--he let I see his good side again

one night when we be talk about you.”



“about I?” whisper Annie, breathlessly.



“yes; he tell I he love you with all his soul, and he mean to win

you back to he if he have to wait ten year. and I believe he.”



“George,” say she, in a low, uncertain voice, raise her eye to his,

after a pause, “he have do it already. but--but he willn’t give I a

chance of tell he so. he willn’t let I know where he be, and--and

indeed he don’t care for I as much as you think; for, if he do, he

couldn’t make appointment with--with other woman,” sob she, with

her head in her hand.



“be you sure that he do, Annie?” ask her brother-in-law, earnestly.



“quite sure. I--I overheard it,” quaver she.



“don’t be so certain about it yet, my poor child! if ever a man be

in solemn earnest, Harry be when he speak to I about you, and he be

far too pig-head to change like that in a few week. he swear to I

that you be the only woman in the world for he, and he should never

look at another again. trust I, don’t make up your mind that he be

faithless to you yet. his keep away from you mean something more

than that, or I’m much mistaken in he.”



Annie allow herself to be somewhat comfort by these word, and she

promise George, who of course manage to allow himself as many--if not

more--of the small comfort of life as he have do before his ruin, to

accompany he to Ascot in ten day’ time, to play good angel to he and

raise his spirit.



but in the meantime she have another visit from Stephen, who look more

haggard than ever; and, as he hint to she that Harry be again in

want of money, and as some dressmaking expense have use up all she have

in hand until she receive her next weekly salary, she fasten up a

bracelet, her good pair of ear-ring, and a diamond brooch which George

have give she into a little packet, which she put into Stephen’s hand,

say:



“I have be spend a lot of money upon myself this week, so I cann’t

spare any just now. there be a few trinket here which I never wear,

and I can spare they well than money. would you mind sell they

for I and give the money to Harry?”



“your jewelry! no, I cann’t take that!” say Stephen, thrust the

packet hastily back and open the door.



“Nonsense! you must--I insist! there be not a thing I care for among

they,” say Annie; and with gentle force she make he take they,

pity the poor fellow as she do so for his reluctance to let she

part with her trinket.



a few day after that be Cup-day at Ascot; and George, true to his

promise, come in a hansom to take she to the station; for they be

go down by train. it be a most beautiful day, Annie enjoy herself

with an unclouded delight which infect her companion, and it take

all his loyalty and a little of her tact to prevent his make love

to she again. she be too wise to suggest economy to he when he take

she, as a matter of course, on to the grand stand and spend his money

with rather more recklessness than in the old day, when he have a large

establishment to keep up, and clamorous young brother’ allowance

to pay. man in difficulty always have plenty of ready money, she

know, and be much lighter-hearted companion than man who go on

ploddingly pay their debt as they arise.



George leave she for a few minute, sit, her face all smile and

sunshine, with his race-glass in her hand, examine the carriage

which line the course. he have go into the ring, and have promise

to be back in time for the next race. he return to find she lean

back, white and shivering, with the luster go from her eye, and her

arm hang limply at her side. a lady--a stranger--be support

her head.



“good heaven, Annie, be you ill?” he cry, in great agitation.



“she be go to faint, I be afraid,” say the lady with she.



“no, no, I shall not faint; I be well already!” say Annie, rouse

herself by a great effort. “thank you very much for your kindness. I

be afraid I frighten you. George, take I to have a glass of wine,

please.”



he lead she, support by his arm, to the refreshment-room, and in a few

minute she have control herself sufficiently to be able to tell he

the reason of her sudden illness.



“I see the woman I tell you about, to whom Harry send message, on a

drag on the course; and I see Harry ride up and speak to she.”



George mutter a savage imprecation between his tooth. Annie continue:



“I want you to take I down there among the carriage, to be quite

sure it be she. do take I, George! if you willn’t, I must go alone.”



“I will take you, if you wish it; but, my child, you have well not go.

if you be to see they together again, it would break your heart.”



“oh, no; my heart be not so tender as that, George!” say she, wearily.

“let we make haste.”



she be afraid of her strength give way again if there be any more

delay. so he take she down, across the course, and in and out among

the carriage until they come in sight of the one she be in search

of. Harry be no long beside the drag; but there sit Muriel, her

complexion carefully make up, and dress with more extravagance than

good taste; and in her ear be the ear-ring and at her throat

be the brooch which Annie have send to Harry to help he out of his

difficulty a week before.



she turn away quickly, and whisper to George, cling to he like

a child, and with a little tremor in her voice:



“now let we go away--let we go away--as fast--as we can--straight back

home!”



she bear up bravely all the way to the station and during the journey

in the train; but when they be drive along together in a hansom,

she say suddenly:



“talk about the race, George, please.”



but he could not, for there be a lump in his throat, and all he could

say, as a lift to the conversation, be:



“curse he!”









CHAPTER XXV.





“don’t go to the theater to-night, Annie! send a note to say you be

not well,” suggest George, when they reach the house where his

sister-in-law be live. “you be not fit to act to-night; they must

get somebody else,” he add, with the charming simplicity of the

“outsider” in theatrical matter, who do not know how loath the

rise actress be to give she “understudy” a chance of prove that she

herself be not indispensable to the success of the piece.



“I must go, George; and it will be the good thing for I,” say she,

with a grateful look at his anxious face. “come and see I to-morrow; I

want to talk to you.”



he leave she unwillingly, and that night he take a stall at the theater

where she be act that he might be at hand in case she break down.

but there be no need of such a fear for the train actress; her

performance that night be, to a close observer, somewhat fitful and

unequal; but she give no other sign of the shock she have sustain

that day--in fact, the excitement cause by it prevent her physical

weariness from be so apparent.



the next morning, however, when George call, he find she sad and

subdue, in spite of the effort she make to seem as cheerful as usual.

when she refer to the previous day, she do so quite calmly; but his

self-command about the matter be not so great as her, and he break

out in a few minute and swear that he would find Harry out and upbraid

he for his infamous conduct to the most perfect woman in the world.



“I be not that, George; and Harry know it--that be the bad of it! if

you be to tell he you and I have both recognize my jewel on another

woman, he would tell you that it be only to be even with I for have

prefer to his the society of another man.”



George look at she in astonishment, for she speak with bitter

self-reproach and keep her eye away from his.



“my dear Annie, you be reproach yourself very unnecessarily. when

Harry himself behave to you like a coal-heaver, even he could scarcely

be surprised that you prefer any society to his.”



“not any society--I do not mean that.”



“no, but that of man of his own rank, but not quite of his manner,”

say George, draw his chair a little nearer to her.



“I do not mean that either. as long as I prefer any society to his,

it don’t matter. so I think myself safe; it seem quite natural to

dislike and fear Harry when he neglect I and snub I, and bully

and at last strike I. I feel that, if I stay with he any long,

his very presence would poison I,” say she with rise excitement.



“no wonder! you be quite right to leave he, and, if you have be

wise, you would never have come back to the brute.”



“do you think so? now I think I be quite wrong. even if I could not

have love he, it would have be safe to stay with he, safe for

he and for I.”



“safer for you!”



“yes, yes. I think I be so strong, so hard, that I could do without

affection altogether--especially as affection could, since my foolish

marriage, only mean Harry’s. and I be foolish and care for he too

little to ask myself whether he could do without it as well.”



“he have show that he don’t deserve your, at all event. if you have

stay at the Grange, I think you might have be happy, Annie; but it

would have be thank to your husband’s family, and not to he. you

see, Lilian be just go to be marry, and my mother would soon have

warm to you when her other daughter be go; and, if Harry have not

change his tone, I would have pack he off somewhere, and then you

would have be surround by nothing but worshiper. and, if you have

like the Grange well in those circumstance, my dear child, I don’t

think any one could have blame you.”



“I think they would, though. you see, my fault all through my married

life have be that I look upon my husband as a contemptible tyrant to

be give way to or avoid as the case might be, never as a reasonable

be whose opinion and feeling be to be consider for their own

sake.”



“but you see he have prove that they be not worth consider. I own

to you that, when he be get well, and he seem never happy when

you be out of his sight, and you go on laugh and talk with

any of we rather than with he, and treat he like a cross, spoil

child, to be give way to and coax, while he seem always long

and try to be something more to you--it do seem to I sometimes

that it be rather rough on Harry; but now I see you be quite right,

and it be a good thing you do not get fond of such a weathercock. and

then, when he rush up to town red-hot to see you, and find you all

dull and solitary----”



“but he don’t find I dull and solitary--that be what make he angry,”

say Annie, blushing. “he find an actor here whom--whom I have grow

fond of when I be on tour. it be partly that I might forget he that

I go to nurse Harry when he be ill,” she say, hurriedly. “he use

to come and see I here after I leave the Grange this last time. I tell

he I could never marry he; but--but I do not tell he I be marry

already; and somehow Harry guess that, and make I half-confess it;

and then, instead of bully I and reproach I now that he really

have something to complain of, he take I to the Bingham Hotel, and

be so sweet and kind to I that I--I really think, if he have stay

with I, I should have grow very fond of he. so you see, George, I

be not a martyr, and, if he have treat I badly, it be no more than I

deserve.”



she speak in a very sad, quiet voice, with all the bright ring go out

of it; and George think, as he watch her eye fix steadily before

she, and her lip quiver a little in spite of herself, that, if her

truant husband could see she now, he would realize how foolish he have

be to expect that he could neglect such a pretty little wife without

some more discriminating person’s try to console she.



“well, now you must forget all about a brute who could take away your

jewelry to give to another woman. and of course, it would not be right

to see any more of the other man--the actor. but I will come and see

you as often as you like, and take you out, and have tea with you and

luncheon with you whenever you feel dull; I will come and live nearer

this way--that will be the good plan--and then you can send for I

whenever you want I,” say George, benevolently.



“thank you, George; I be very glad you be in town,” say she, smile;

“but I willn’t trespass upon your kindness so much as that. I be afraid

Harry ben’t worth the determination; but I be not go to give he a

loophole for complaint of I again.”



“but he couldn’t be jealous of I,” say George, with eager surprise.

“you cann’t bury yourself alive for the sake of a man who be deceive

you, who write to say he be get on badly”--Annie have tell he

that, but without say anything about the money she have sent--“and

whom you see a week after on horseback on a race-course enjoy

himself as if he be rich. it ben’t as if I be one of your handsome

actors----”



“you be too modest, George. you be handsomer than any actor I know.”



“Handsomer than----”



“oh, yes; he be quite ugly! that be the hard blow of all to Harry,”

say she, laugh. “but, handsome or ugly, Harry shall never have the

least reason to be jealous again.”



“be you so sure of yourself?” ask George softly. “you know,” he

continue diffidently, “you think you be quite cold and safe

before.”



“I have a safeguard now,” say she in a low voice. “in spite of all

that he have do--and you have not hear the bad--I love Harry; his

forbearance to I when I be in the wrong seem to have subdue I; and

nothing in this world now, not even brilliant success on the stage, have

so much charm for I as the hope of some day win he back to I.”



“I hope you will, Annie--I hope you will. you deserve the great

happiness the world can give, and Harry would be a fool not to snatch

at what many a man will envy he for.”



Annie do not want he to grow sentimental, and she soon turn the

conversation to other matter.



she have a firm friend now in her eld brother-in-law, whom she know

how to manage, and to whom, in this time of his ruin and consequent

trouble, she do infinite service by her sympathy and encouragement.

she could not, even if she have wish to do so, prevent his come to

see she constantly; for, though a man accustomed to depend upon himself

in a struggle, he could find no consolation, now that the struggle be

over, so great as his sister-in-law’s sweet voice and kind eye.



she have drop much out of her circle of acquaintance since the blow

she have receive at Ascot; life have lose some of its zest for she, and

she have grow restlessly anxious for news of her husband. she receive

letter from he now and then, short, affectionate, ill-spell, but

vague, request she to send her answer under cover to Stephen at a

club he mention. she write answer in which, as he never mention

his prospect or her, or the money she have send he, she never

refer to they either. she also write to Stephen himself at the

address give, beg he to come and see she; but to this she get no

answer, until one afternoon she meet he in the Strand and insist on

his return home with she. he be look as haggard as ever, and

seem more uneasy in her presence than he have be before.



“why haven’t you be here for so long, Stephen, when you know how

anxious I should be? and what have you to say to I from Harry about

what I send he? I should have think I deserve a message of

acknowledgment; but he do not even mention, in his very short note,

the help I have so often give he.”



“he be ashamed to do so, Annie. but he be grateful to you all the same.

he often talk to I about the sacrifice you must have make, and he

think of they a great deal, I be sure.”



“but that be not enough. he ought to speak to I about they, and, if he

be too shy to do so by letter, I must hear he express his gratitude

in person. where be he live, Stephen? I must have his address,” say

Annie, with determination.



“I cann’t give it you--I cann’t indeed. I be afraid you would want to

know it, and he have forbid I to give it you; that be why I have

keep away from you.”



“and what reason have you both to give for this very singular refusal?

what be Harry do that he be ashamed to be see by his own wife?”



“he be not ashamed exactly; but he know how proud you be, and he

think, if you know how he earn his living, you would look down upon

he.”



“be it something so very disgraceful then?”



“perhaps you might call it so; at least he think so.”



“tell I what it be. Stephen, do tell I.”



“I cann’t. I swear to he I wouldn’t.”



“then be I never to know? don’t he want ever to see I again?”



“some day, but not yet.”



“but what difference can wait make? if it be disgraceful now, it

will always be disgraceful. but, if it be only that he have take

to earn his living by some employment not generally fill by

gentleman, why, I shall only respect he the more for sacrifice his

pride! that be true indeed.”



but all her argument and entreaty do not move Stephen, who seem

very much agitated by her supplication, but doggedly refuse to yield

to they.



that night she write a letter to her husband, send it as usual to

Stephen to be forward.



 “MY dear HARRY,--After wait impatiently for more tiding of you

 than your scanty note convey, I catch Stephen to-day, much against

 his will, and hope to get he to give I your address, that I might

 come and see you. but nothing would induce he to tell I where you

 be or what you be do, and he say you have strictly forbid he

 to do so. I now appeal to you to put an end to the anxiety I be in

 about you, and to let I come and see you, if you will not come and

 see I. Stephen seem to think that you be afraid that the way you

 be earn your living will shock I; but indeed I think, if I be

 to see you with a black face after sweep a chimney, or drive a

 donkey-cart full of vegetable, you would not complain of the coldness

 of the welcome I would give you. please, please write to I, not one

 of those little hurried scrawl say nothing, but a letter just to

 tell I when I be to see you again. I don’t think you would be jealous

 of anybody I see now, except perhaps of dear old George, whom I see

 nearly every day, and whom I should like much well if only he would

 do something, like you. I know you hate write; but you would find

 time for this if only you know how anxious I be to be sure you be

 well.



  “your love wife,



                                                                “ANNIE.”



she post this letter under cover to Stephen; but she wait three

week without get any answer.



at the end of that time she be surprise by a visit from Lilian, who

have just return with her husband from abroad, having be travel

some month for her health, which have break down. she be much touch

by her sister-in-law’s kindness to George, who have dine with Mr.

Falconer and herself the night before, and have represent Annie as the

guardian angel of the family.



“Wilfred have come up to town, and he be with we too,” say Lilian.

“and he talk of you just as well, and want to come and see you, but

he don’t dare. you be a good little thing, Annie, to keep so staid

now when every one be talk about you, and when Harry have treat you

so badly.”



“who tell you that?” say Annie, sharply.



“George. but never mind; you mustn’t be angry with he or with I. what

have become of Mr. Cooke?” she ask, in a low voice.



“Mr. Cooke! oh, he be marry, I believe; at least I be sure he be!”

she answer, in an indifferent tone, but blush.



“Married? oh, well, I be glad of that!”



“there be no need on my account,” say Annie, haughtily.



“no, no--of course not, child. still I be glad.”



“People say they get on very badly. and now he be ill, I hear.”



the tear be start to Annie’s eye; and Lilian, whom ill health

have soften, begin to cry too for sympathy. Annie fight down her

“have you hear from any of the other--William or Stephen?” she ask,

to turn the conversation.



“Stephen come to see I this morning. he be in wretched health, and

he to come and see I this afternoon, and I expect he be wait for

child. I don’t know what to wish for you.”



and Lilian, whose movement be slow and languid, and whose

beautiful face have grow thin with illness, kiss her sister-in-law

CHAPTER XXVI.





Annie do not sleep that night. thought of poor Aubrey and the wreck

it be Friday, and she could not leave town until Sunday, her only

 “DEAR HARRY,--I will come down and see you on Sunday by the 2.30 train

                                                         “your darling.”

“oh, heaven, what will he say to I?” think she.









CHAPTER XXVII.





Annie hear her husband open the door, but she do not turn round;

then she hear his footstep advance to the middle of the room and

excited in her nothing but contempt and disgust.



he clear his throat again, and again she take no notice. at last he
