“Annie, ben’t you go to speak to I?” he ask, in the gentle,

most entreating of voice.



she turn round in surprise. he stand there before she, this big,

handsome young fellow who could tame the most fiery of horse with a

but, now you’ve come to see I of your own accord, I think you might

have a kiss for I.”



Annie look, listen, in utter bewilderment. letter! kiss! what

husband.”

“All--I--say?”



“yes, yes! ah, you’re sorry now, ben’t you, my darling? you see you

my good, and not drink--and work hard, so that I might have you

Annie raise her eye, with a startled expression, to his face.

note--they be very few--and I write you a long letter, beg you

he be startle in his turn, and sit look at she for a few moment

“Harry,” say she, in a low voice, “tell I quick--do you get the

letter?”



“no.”



“do you--do you ever receive anything send to you by I?”



“oh, yes; I get three or four letter!”



“nothing else?” she ask, breathlessly.



“yes; once you send I some red-and-white flower. I’ve get they in my

“anything else? no, I think not; I be sure not, for I should never

“you never receive, for instance----”

“from you, Annie? no, certainly not!”



she sink at his foot and put her head on his knee in a passion of

Annie hesitate. she could not but know now on whom the blame of this

of her husband’s arm, and see his head bent in a listening attitude

toward her. then, feel at last the irresistible force of a

resolution strong than her own, she submit--submit in the most

win way in the world, place her little hand on either side of

his neck, and look up at he with her sweetest, softest expression

of face to coax away his anger.



“then I must trust to your generosity, Harry. and, if you don’t behave

generously and forgivingly about it, I shall think you be not glad

to have I again, for happiness ought always to make people’s heart

soft.”



he kiss she without answer in word; and she go on:



“when Stephen first come to I with a letter from you, look very

ill, very miserable--I think he be go to die--he make I very

jealous and hurt I by tell I how much happy you be now you

be away from town and among country people again. he do not know

how fond I have grow of you, and that I be silly enough not to like to

hear how well you be get on without I. be you as happy as he

say, Harry?”



“I be happy just then, because Lord Lytham be begin to show

confidence in I, and I see my way to earn money and be with you

again. but, if he say I don’t miss you, he tell lie.”



“he do not say that; and he have not the least idea how much it

matter to I. but I be angry with you for send I such a short

letter, and I think you be enjoy yourself, and very likely

don’t care; so I tear up the long, love letter I have write, and

send you a short one say nothing, like your.”



“oh, you little spiteful creature! I write that note four time before

I get one fit to send you; I be so afraid you would be offend if I

tell you what I be go to do. I think I would wait until I have get

on, and then come to you and show you that I could be just as fond of

you as if I have never be in a stable in my life. and, at any rate, I

think, if I succeed, you would think it be well than idle.”



“well than idle! oh, Harry, it be well than anything for you

to be successful and happy and--and fond of I!” after a pause, she

continue, “when he come the second time, he say you be not get

on as fast as you wish.”



“that be true; I be in low spirit about it. well?”



“then he say it be very hard for a man without money to get on. he

say that himself, not that you have say it. and I be afraid you be

perhaps in serious difficulty for want of money, and I beg he to

take some that I have put away and don’t want.”



“and he take it?”



“wait. he refuse for a long time, and say you would not think of

accept my money, so at last I push it into his hand, and tell he

not to say it come from I. he be very reluctant to the last; I expect

he be afraid to give it you and afraid to give it back to I.”



“be that the only time he take your money?”



“no; I give he some two or three time--not much, of course--and it

make no difference to I, for it be money I have put aside.”



“and what be that you say about jewelry? Come, Annie, you mustn’t

keep back anything! it ben’t fair to tell only half.”



“it be only that once, when I be short of ready money, and anxious, in

spite of poor Stephen’s entreaty, to send you some, I give he a pair

of ear-ring and two other little trinket I never wear, and ask he

to sell they for I.”



Harry start up restlessly from the sofa and begin march up and

down; then he stop short in front of she.



“why don’t you write to I when you get no acknowledgment?”



“I don’t like to. I think Stephen have keep from you the fact that

the money come from I.”



“and you think I be such a booby as not to have guess, and such

a bear as not to have thank you? Annie, that be impossible! you be

hide something from I still.”



but Annie do not answer or look at he. her eye be fix in front

of she, as a new light break in upon her bewildered mind.



“Harry,” say she at length, raise her glitter eye to his with

an expression which be almost fear, “those flower--you send--by

Stephen--a few day ago----”



“oh, do you get those then? he do not condescend to--”



“be they for I?” she ask, in a low voice.



“for you! of course they be for you; who else should they be for?”

say Harry, irritably, his excitement get the well of he.



“not for--not for--Muriel West!” she murmur the name so low that she

have to repeat it.



“Muriel West? no. who on earth be Muriel West?”



“you don’t know!” she cry joyfully. “but, Harry, I see you talk to

she on a coach at Ascot.”



“do you mean an actress name West? why, Annie, how jealous you be!

I scarcely speak to she, and shouldn’t have do so at all if Stephen

haven’t be with she. a fellow I know take I to supper once at her

house a long time ago--it be the very night of my accident--and I have

never see she since, except that day at Ascot.”



“then how be it that she be wear my ornament?” ask Annie,

quickly; and, as she speak, the truth flash upon they both.



“the little mean scoundrel!” growl Harry, clinch his fist. “the

little crooked, lie rascal! he shall suffer for this clever trick.

then he get all he could out of both of we, and keep we apart by his

lie! of course you never say it be a disgraceful thing for I to

turn trainer?”



“I never know you be a trainer until this afternoon, when I hear

those two gentleman talk about you in the carriage as I come down.

he refuse to give I your address, say you have forbid he to do

so, and I find it out only by this card.” she take from her purse the

card she have find in the hamper, and continue, “I go to see Stephen

last Friday, determine to find out where you be. I see a hamper

of flower with the lid open, and inside I find this card. I look

outside, and find that the direction be to ‘Miss Muriel West.’”



“the direction have be change; I direct it to you, and give it to

that wretched little hunchback for you. and, Annie, do you mean to say

that, when you see your ornament on that woman, you think that I have

give they to she?” he ask, look at she almost with horror.



“what else could I think, Harry?”



“and you never write to reproach I?”



“I could not write about such a thing--it be too dreadful! I think

I would accuse you of it face to face. but don’t talk about it, Harry,

please--I cann’t bear to think of it now; it be wicked of I ever to

think it could be true.”



“and you come down here to-day still believe it! and you could kiss a

man you believe capable of such an infamous thing!”



“no, no, Harry; don’t look at I like that! the moment you speak to I

alone in this room I feel it could not be true; because, you see, I be

sure you love I, and that clear it all away.”



and her husband draw she again into his arm, with a mist before his

own eye.



Dusk have fall, and they be still sit there, when they be

rouse from a silence of perfect happiness by the prosaic sound of the

dinner-bell. Harry have great difficulty in keep his boyish high

spirit under proper control during dinner, and, when it be over, he

say:



“let we go out of door, Annie; there ben’t room enough for my

happiness in a stuffy house.”



so he put on her hat and mantle very carefully and very clumsily, and

they go out into the park.



“take I to see the horse, Harry. here’s your cigar-case; I see it

up-stair, so I bring it down.”



“I may smoke then?”



“yes, of course. you be go the wrong way. ben’t that the way to the

stable?”



“yes; but I’m not go to take you there; you only ask to go to please

I.”



“on my word of honor, I ask to go to please myself; and, if you don’t

like to take I, I shall go over they with one of the stableman, while

you be sulk over your cigar by yourself. now be you come?”



so they go through the stable together, and Harry be quick to note

the genuine ring in the interest, for what concern he concern she

too now; and they walk all round the park together, and he say:



“do you think you could ever live happily with I here, Annie?”



“and give up the stage?”



“well, act only now and then. you might take an engagement for three

month or more, but not give yourself up to it altogether. I know

you be too clever to just settle down to keep house for a dull,

ignorant husband.”



“you’re not dull and ignorant, Harry.”



“well, not so ignorant as I be,” say he, with, mysterious

complacency. “do you think that would be too great a sacrifice, Annie?”



“no, indeed. I couldn’t throw my whole heart into my acting now if I

think I be neglect you.”



“and you will come and see I every Sunday, and stay till Monday

evening now, willn’t you? I mustn’t ask more than that yet, I suppose.”



and she consent readily enough. and then come the crown triumph

of the day to Harry. he lead his wife into the library, the volume of

which have luckily be collect long before his occupation of Kirby

Hall, and say, turn proudly to she:



“you never think I should get fond of book, Annie. well, I have, and

I like this room well than any in the house.”



there be three photograph of she on the mantel-piece, there be a

liqueur-case on a side-table, and the room be strongly perfume with

tobacco. Annie’s eye twinkle, but she only laugh contentedly.



“and now you shall hear I read aloud,” say he.



so he put she into an arm-chair, and sit on a footstool at her foot,

and read she a couple of page of the _Nineteenth Century_. it be a

very poor performance indeed, hesitating, badly emphasize, with the

long word slur over. he be not at his good, for he have Annie’s

finger in one hand and his cigar in his mouth.



“you read beautifully now, Harry!” say she, when he look up for

approval; and the clever, well-inform woman really think so.



“it only show what perseverance will do,” say Harry, gravely. “I’ve

read that piece aloud to myself twenty or thirty time.”









CHAPTER XXVIII.





Annie pass the night at Kirby Park; and, when she and Harry be

sit at breakfast the next morning, he tell she he should come and

see her act that night.



“then will you come up to town with I?” she ask eagerly.



her husband hesitate.



“I don’t know whether I can, Annie. I have some thing to see to down

here before I start, and something to do in town when I get up there,

so that I cannot be at your room till about four.”



her face cloud.



“something to do in town!” she echo, watch he narrowly, and

note the expression into which his face have set during the last few

minute. “be it--to see some one, Harry?” she ask timidly.



“yes, a business appointment.”



“oh, Harry, it be to see Stephen, I know! what be you go to do?

what be you go to say? you look as if you would kill he!”



“don’t be afraid. how could I condescend to touch the little misshapen

wretch, who have not as much strength in his whole body as I have in one

finger? but I be go to see he, and to-day.”



she see that it be impossible to alter her husband’s resolution, so

she desist from her persuasion; but there be a terrible fear at

her heart which she could not shake off. she know the violence of her

husband’s temper, and fear it all the more under this new aspect

of repression. she make up her mind to go to Stephen and warn he of

Harry’s come, and to beg he not to exasperate her husband far

by any attempt at concealment and false excuse, but to make a frank

confession, such as would, she feel sure, be more likely than anything

else to avert Harry’s anger. once resolve on this course, she let

the conversation turn to indifferent subject, and it be not until

breakfast be nearly over that she pretend to remember an appointment

with her dressmaker which would make it necessary for she to go up to

town before luncheon. she do it too naturally to excite in her husband

any suspicion of her good faith, and he go to the station with she,

and part with she very reluctantly, although he expect to be with

she again in a few hour.



Annie herself feel something more than reluctance; she be seize with

a foreboding of evil.



“ah, Harry,” she say, lay a trembling hand upon his arm, “I wish I

be not married to you!”



“why?” ask he, startle.



“because then perhaps you might do what you do long ago, fle all

consideration of business and duty to the wind and jump into the

train with I.”



“do you think my love be well worth have then than now?” he ask

softly.



“N-o, perhaps not. still I wish the wife have as much influence as the

girl have.”



if the train have be in the station, she in it, and he at the door,

these word would have carry he off. as it be, stand on the

platform beside she, Harry be seize with a great trembling, and,

walk away from she a few step, he come back and say to she, low

and reproachfully:



“that be the first time you have ever tempt I to what be not right,

Annie. if the train have be here, your word would have make I jump

in, and, for the first time since I have have work to do, I should have

neglect it--and through you. I have a lot to see to at the stable

this morning, and an appointment to keep with Captain King before I go

up to town. but I cann’t resist you; so, if you love I, Annie, and if

you care for what people think of I and say of I, don’t ask I again,

my darling, for I cann’t say ‘no’ to you.”



the young wife, self-possess and independent as she usually be, hung

her head. these word of his, inspire in she a strong feeling of

respect, do much to restore her confidence in his self-command when

deal with his treacherous cousin. as she take the rebuke silently,

Harry begin to be alarm at the effect he have produce.



“you be not angry with I, be you, darling? you look as if I have be

scold you, as if we have change place.”



“changed place, Harry!” cry she, look up in astonishment.



she have already forget the long period during which she have look

upon her husband as a tiresome, unreasonable child.



“yes, when I be get well at the Grange you don’t always treat I

with proper respect, I fancy,” say he, flush, but look down into

her eye rather mischievously.



“oh, ah--then you be ill!” she say, blush too.



the last word she say to her husband as the train go off be:



“remember you be not to be with I later than four. promise.”



“all right; I promise.”



and, try to look as if her mind be at ease, Annie give he a last

smile “good-bye,” as the train start.



on arrive in town, she drive straight to the house where Stephen

lodge, and, find that he be out, she sit down in the sitting-room

to wait for he. long ere this an explanation of the cripple’s cruel

and deceitful conduct have occur to she, and it seem more and more

probable to she, as she sit in the shabby sitting-room, with its low,

weather-stain ceiling and ill-paper wall. evidently the money

which he have keep back from she he have not spend upon himself; it must

have go where her jewelry have go, and Harry’s flower--to Muriel

West. Annie know well to what depth of meanness he would descend

in his devotion to a woman, for she remember with what dog and

disintereste fidelity he have fulfil every command, every wish of

his cousin Lilian in the old day at the Grange, before her marriage

with Mr. Falconer. in spite of her contempt for a man who could stoop

to such act, Annie be touch by the cripple’s hapless attachment,

and a great pity fill her heart as she hear the slow thud, thud of

his crutch upon the staircase. her compassion deepen when the door

open and Stephen stand before she, wild-eyed and pale with a pallor

which be like that of death. she sit quite still for an instant,

unable to speak, unable to express what she feel at the dreadful change

in his appearance.



but when she rise very softly and hold out a hand to he, she

discover, to her horror, that he still stare blankly in front of

he, make no sign. he do not see she.



“Stephen,” say she, in a low voice.



he start, and for the first time know that he be not alone.



“Annie!” he say apathetically. “it be you, be it?”



with mechanical courtesy, he move forward feebly and offer she a

chair; but she take his hand and lead he very gently to the hard little

sofa, and make he sit down beside she there. she think the feeling

which have evidently overmaster he must be remorse for his conduct

toward she and her husband, and she try to think of the sweetest

word she could to soothe his distress.



“it make I very unhappy to see how deeply you be suffer,” say

she. “if I have know you would feel it so much, I would have come

before.”



he play idly with his crutch, not in the least move by her word.



“it would have make no difference,” say he, in a dull, cold tone.



“oh, but I think it would! I would not have let you think so much about

it!”



“how could you help that?” say he, turn upon she his lusterless

eye. “I tell you I be not rich enough, and she would have throw I

over just the same!”



Annie start. he be think no more of the wrong he have do she

than if it have be a deed of a hundred year back. but she be not

angry. her pity rise higher than ever for this unhappy man, who have

sacrifice all, even to his honesty, for the sake of a woman who do

not care a straw for he now that she have get from he all he have to

give.



“Stephen, I be so very, very sorry for you,” say she, in a quivering

voice.



“be you?” say he, wake for an instant into something more like

life. “and yet--you have no reason to be.”



a feeling of shame seem for the first time to come over he as he

realize whose sympathy it be that be offer he; and he draw his

hand away from her.



“every one have reason to be sorry for any one else who be unhappy,”

say she. “and when you see that even I can feel sympathy with you, you

will see that you have friend who be worth live for yet.”



“not I, not I,” murmur he, in a broken voice. “there be nothing leave

for I. she have promise to marry I--she be not a lady by birth, you

know, and I could have make she one by position. I would have work

for she--I have work for she--I have do more. but I use up all I

have too fast--she see I have no more; she say, if she marry I, we

should starve. and she look at I quite coldly with her beautiful

eye, and say she be not well-educate enough to marry a gentleman--a

gentleman! I, a poor cripple! it be that--it be always that! there be

no happiness, no love for I; nothing but pity--wretched, miserable,

scornful pity, that sting I more than taunt, more than hatred. she

pity I, I dare say, and laugh at I, and let I go;” and he break

down into incoherent word and sobbing.



Annie try bright word of encouragement, ask he if he think

nothing of her friendship, of that of the rest of his family; but she

speak to deaf ear. when at length she rise to go, he give she his hand

and say, but still coldly:



“thank you. I shall be glad presently that you come. it be good of you

to come--generous--and I thank you. if I have a long life before I, I

would try to do you some service; but I be play out now, and there be

not much of my life leave to run. good-bye, Annie.”



she could not stay. his last word be almost a command to go. she

have not mention her husband’s name. she think that, in the state

of mind in which she be leave the cripple, the dread of an angry

visitor might make he desperate; and she know very well that, when

Harry see the miserable condition to which his sensitive cousin be

reduce, he be no more likely to be unmerciful than she have be.



but she could not shake off a foreboding that the meeting between the

cousin would be productive of evil, and she reach home anxious and

thoughtful.



her misgiving be not without foundation.



within an hour of her departure from Stephen’s lodging, Harry drive up

in a hansom and be direct, as his wife have be, to the little room

on the top floor. he enter with a very stern face and firm tread; but

the sight of the cripple, lie half on the sofa, half on a chair, in a

state of utter prostration of body and mind, make he pause. the other

look up at he without fear, without feeling of any kind.



“do you know I?” ask Harry, abruptly.



“yes; what do you want here?”



“I want an explanation. if you do not feel fit to give it to I now, I

will come again. but I must have it, and the less delay the well.”



“ask your wife, then. she have a well head than you, and understand

without so much talk. go to she for your explanation, and leave I

in peace.”



“not yet. I want some reason for your stop my letter to she and

her letter to I, for take the present we intrust to your care to

be give to each other, and for give her money, my flower, and even

her jewelry to a greedy, extravagant, worthless woman whom you couldn’t

satisfy if you have gold mine to give she. that be what I want you to

answer.”



the cripple have raise himself, his eye glitter with fury, and he

sit frown maliciously at his cousin until the latter have finish

his speech.



“then I willn’t answer you, except to say this; you be very good now,

and look upon extravagance and waste as very wicked thing. but you

haven’t be a saint so very long that you can have forget that you

yourself be as greedy and worthless as any one I know once, and that

you forge your father’s name to supply your own extravagance, which,

it seem to I, be bad than to stoop to meanness for the sake of a

woman you love and for whom you would die.”



the last word he speak in a low voice, look straight in front of

he with his glitter, feverish eye; and his hand move restlessly

toward his coat-pocket as he finish speak.



“look here!” say Harry, in a soft voice. “I don’t want to be hard on

you. I know I’ve do as bad thing myself, if not bad; and, if I’m a

saint now, it’s the first I’ve hear of it. but, if you’re so fond of

this woman as you say, I wonder how you could have the heart to play

such confoundedly nasty trick with the love of another man, and to

such an angel as Annie, who have always be kind to you too?”



“your love? your love be nothing to mine!” Stephen burst out,

contemptuously. “a woman may have a place in your heart; but your dog

and your horse fill the rest of it. you be handsome, straight; if

one woman will not smile on you, another will; while I, who love sweet

eye and fair face with a passion you cannot dream of, can only buy

kindness from a woman by the ceaseless labor of minister to all her

want, all her caprice; and then, when at last the time come when I

can give no more, I be cast aside and forget for--for one of your

sort, with a pair of blue eye that say nothing, and a head that cann’t

put two idea together.”



the passionate bitterness of this speech move Harry.



“it--it be rough on a fellow,” he murmur, in a low, gruff voice.



but the pity in his tone wake the wretched man before he to frenzy.



“you can spare I your pity,” say he, fiercely. “all our life through

you have get easily what I might work myself to death for, and never

get, after all. you always get enjoyment, admiration, love; and, now

you have sober down, you get respect, success, money. if you have be

in my place, Muriel would never have throw you over. she have see

you only once, at a supper-party, month ago, at Beckham. yet, when I

meet she in London, she remember your stupid, red face, and send you

message which I take care not to give you. but I will be even with you

at last; the remedy I prepare for my own wrong will do as well for

your.”



and Stephen draw out from his breast, where his hand have be hide

for some minute, a revolver, and, aim before the other have time to

realize his intention, fire it at his cousin.



       *       *       *       *       *



four o’clock come, and still Annie wait for her husband. he have

promise so seriously, so many time, not to be later than that hour

that her impatience grow quickly into anxiety as the time pass and

still he do not appear. at half-past four, just as she be decide

that she could wait no long, that she must go to Stephen’s lodge

and find out what have detain he, she hear a knock at the door,

which, however, she recognize, to her bitter disappointment, not as

Harry’s but George’s. he have bring William to see she, that young

soldier having just arrive in town, and be mad to have a glimpse

of his old play-fellow, and tell she how well he be get on in his

profession.



Poor Annie could give but a mechanical show of interest to the young

fellow’s eager outpouring, and at last she break down.



“William, I cannot listen now,” she say, with tear in her eye.

“you know it be not for lack of interest; but----George,” she cry

suddenly, turn to her eld brother-in-law, “Harry have go to see

Stephen, angry than I ever see he before. I cann’t tell you why now.

but Harry and I be reconcile. it seem you know all about his being

at Kirby Park. you might have tell I! and he promise to be with

I at four o’clock,” she go on, grow more and more excited and

incoherent. “you see it be a quarter to five, and he be not here! he

be very angry; and I be afraid something have happen. I must go and

see!”



there be no restrain she. in ten minute they be all three on the

way to Stephen’s lodge. as they approach the house, George catch

sight of something from the cab-window which make he turn suddenly to

his sister-in-law and advise she to return while he go in and speak

to Harry.



she see the alarm in his eye, and, steady herself to speak calmly,

she refuse. so the cab stop; and then Annie see that there be a

rough crowd outside the house and a policeman keep the people away

from the door. George spring out; but she follow so closely behind

he that she catch the policeman’s answer to his low-voice question:



“what be the matter?”



“man shoot, I believe, sir.”



Annie keep quite still, quite calm, while George induce the policeman

to let they pass in; and, as soon as the door be open, she slip

past her brother-in-law, who have not know she be so close, and fly

first up the stair, swiftly and silently as a bird.



“he have break his word to I,” she think in agony. “he have scatter

all our happiness; and now----Oh, where be he? I dare not go in!

perhaps already they have lead he away to--prison. oh, Harry, Harry!”



she be stand outside the door of the sitting-room, which be shut.

she seem to hear a noise of low voice: but she be not sure that it

be not the singing in her own ear. at last, with cold, weak finger,

she turn the handle and go in.



the only figure in the room be that of the cripple, lie motionless

on the sofa.



bring thus abruptly into what she believe to be the presence of a

dead man, Annie totter to the table for support, her face white and

damp with horror; but Stephen turn, raise his head and confront

she; and she give a low cry of relief when she see that he be alive.



“then Harry have not hurt you?” she whisper falteringly.



“no,” say the cripple, “it be not he. you will never forgive I,

Annie; you will hate I. I shoot he!”



Annie do not cry this time, do not even start; she stand tap with

her finger upon the table, strike suddenly into utter numbness. she

do not feel his tremble hand cling to her mantle as he fall at

her foot and implore she to speak to he, to scold he, and not to

stand before he as if his word have kill she. she do not hear the

door of the bedroom open or feel the touch of a stranger’s hand. but

the new-comer be a doctor; and, when she wake presently from the sort

of stupor which have seize she, he say, quietly:



“now, Mrs. Braithwaite, if you will remain calm, you shall see your

husband.”



“I be calm,” she say, simply.



she could not have cry, or moan, or lament her fate, if her life

have depend upon she show some emotion.



so he lead she into the next room; and there, not dead, but sit in

a fade chintz arm-chair, with his left arm bind up, be Harry. it be

then that her calmness give way. she be not very demonstrative indeed

over the passion of joy which light up and transfigure her whole face;

but she fall upon her knee by the side of his chair, shake from head

to foot.



“I think--you be--kill!” whisper she.



“why, my poor darling, who tell you so?” he ask, tenderly.



“I shall never forgive Stephen!” she hiss, clinch her tooth.



“yes, you will, Annie. he be to be pity, not I--only we musn’t tell

he that. he haven’t even hurt I much--the arm be not break; the only

danger possible to I through it be loss of blood; and, if I keep

quiet, I shall be all right again in no time. be that George’s voice I

hear in the next room?”



“yes; he come with I and William.”



“I must get William to come down with I to Kirby Park for a day or two

till I can ride again. he’ll be very glad to come and I to have he.

if I have to stay indoor alone, I think I should throw myself off the

roof.”



“oh, Harry, willn’t you have I?” Annie ask, in pitiful entreaty.



“why, how can I, my darling? I know you willn’t break your engagement at

the theater.”



“no; but I’ll go down to Kirby Park every night after the performance,

and come back each evening in time to dress for the theater.”



“but willn’t that tire you too much, Annie? it be more than an hour’s

journey by train,” he say; but his eye flash at the proposal.



“why,” say Annie, shyly, laugh a little, “I want to do so all the

time. I think of it yesterday; but then I decide to wait until you

ask I; and, after all,” she add, with mock petulance, “I’ve have to

ask myself.”



so that night, after the performance, Annie, escort by George, who

have make what excuse he could for not having reveal to she that he

have hear of Harry’s residence at Kirby Park, drive to Waterloo, where

she find William and her husband. the three go down to Kirby Park

together by the last train, very tired, especially Annie, but very

happy.



the next day she and William have a walk together, while Harry be

hold a business interview in the library; but William find that it

be not quite like the old time at the Grange.



“haven’t it improve Harry to have something to do?” say the young wife

proudly.



“oh, he’s well enough!” say William, without enthusiasm. “but

there’s a sad fall off in you, Annie. you’re quite spoil for a

sister-in-law. why, now, when anything amuse you, you look first at

Harry!”









CHAPTER XXIX.





More than six year have pass since the night when Annie return

with her husband to Kirby Park, and there be Braithwaites once more

at Garstone Grange. for Harry, with a wise and love wife at his side

to comfort he in failure, encourage he in effort, and rejoice with

he in success, have work on in the career well suit to he and

prosper, while she too, have strive successfully in her profession

until the time have come on which the hope of both have for year be

fix, and they have buy back the old home of Harry’s boyhood, where

also so many of the stormy event of their early wed life take

place.



it be Christmas-time, their first Christmas since their return to the

Grange; and Annie and her husband be expect some welcome guest to

celebrate this event and Annie’s final renunciation of her ambition

for that entire devotion to home and husband which have now become

her chief delight. for Annie have leave the stage, with its struggle,

its failure, and its triumph, forever, not without some regret at

bidding farewell to old friend, old usage, and a life which have have

many pleasure for she; but with new happiness in the thought that she

can now devote herself more entirely than before to the husband and

child in whom all her affection be center. for in the long, dim

picture-gallery where Harry see the demure little governess playing

battledoor and shuttlecock year ago two fair-haired boy be laugh

and shout at play. their father be rather disappointed that they

have his own blue eye and curly, fair hair, and he be in great anxiety

lest they should grow up like he in mind, instead of be “clever”

like their mother; but Annie be trouble with no such fear, and be

quite contented with her boy as they be.



two more Braithwaites lie in the family vault in Garstone Churchyard.

the first to go to his rest be Stephen, who live but very few month

after that miserable scene in which he shoot his cousin in his desperate

wretchedness. those month be the most peaceful of his unhappy life,

for he pass they at Kirby Park, to which Annie have herself gently

persuade he to come. she never weary in her patient devotion to

he, in her attention to his want, in her bright endeavor to amuse

and please he. Harry second her effort with gentleness which be

touch in the big, strong man; and the cripple’s feeling be too

strong and his penetration too keen for he not to appreciate rightly

every kind act and tone in the people about he.



Wilfred lie in the vault too; he be kill by a fall from his horse

in the hunting-field in the winter follow the sale of the Grange,

and they bury he beside his father and cousin.



a well fate be in store for the young brother, William. “the

child” be now Captain Braithwaite, and his letter to Garstone be full

of reference to the lovely girl that ever be see and mysterious

hint that he have a surprise in store for they--from which and a

certain incoherency of style in his letter Annie do not need much

penetration to decide that he be go to be marry.



Sir George pass most of the year in chamber in town, and have

never find the courage to begin a new battle with fate; he be still

unmarried, and there seem every probability that the title will pass

in course of time to Harry and his eld son.



he and William be now at the Grange to spend Christmas with Harry and

his wife; they be all expect two other guest, for whom the warm

welcome of all have be prepare. Lady Braithwaite, grow old now,

and reconcile to her daughter-in-law at last, be about to return to

the home where her wed life be pass, never to leave it again

until the time come for she too to sleep peacefully by her husband’s

side in Garstone Churchyard; and Lilian be come with she to spend a

week at the old home.



the winter sun be set when Annie, on the alert for the sound of

wheel, start up from her seat in the morning-room and go out on to

the doorstep with William and George to receive Lady Braithwaite and

her daughter, whom Harry have go to meet at Beckham Station. Harry,

who jump out of the carriage first, give way to his eld brother,

and it be on Sir George’s arm that the stately old lady lean as she

step down from the carriage and meet her daughter-in-law. Lilian

follow. she be thin and pale, and look much old than Annie, who have

recover almost all the beauty of the shy little governess of eighteen

who first attract the attention of the wild Grange boy more than ten

year ago. Lilian’s love of excitement and pleasure have tell upon she

health; she be not exactly an unhappy woman or an unloving wife; but

her passionate nature have find something want in life, and in the

eagerness of a vain search for it she have grow old before her time.



when they be all together in the drawing-room after dinner, and the

little boy, having begin to make themselves obnoxious by play at

ball over their grandmother’s head, have be kiss and send to bed,

the talk turn to town and what be go on there.



“oh, Annie, have you hear of the success of your old friend Aubrey

Cooke?” ask Lilian. “I go to see he in this new piece in which they

say he be so good, and I never feel myself so entirely carry away by

any act before. everybody say he will be the great actor of the

day.”



“ah, I think I be go to be the great actress once!” Annie

say, rather slowly.



“then he have fulfil his ambition, and you have give up your

unfulfilled. don’t you regret it just a little? come--be candid!”



Lilian speak in a low voice, mean only for her sister-in-law’s ear.

Annie hesitate, look down at the fire with an expression which it

be not easy to read.



she be startle by find her husband’s hand lay quietly on her

shoulder. he have overhear these last word of Lilian’s, notice his

wife’s reluctance to answer, and leave his seat to speak to she.



“be you sorry you be not the wife of a great actor instead of a plain

country gentleman, Annie?”



“no, not in the least; I never think of such a thing.”



“then why be you look so thoughtful?”



“any news of people one have know well and lose sight of set one

thinking.”



“I could give you some more news of he but that I be afraid it would

make you sad.”



“never mind; I should like to hear it. go on.”



“his home-life be a very unhappy one. they say he ill-treat his wife;

I know they be never see together. George tell I all about it

yesterday; but I do not tell you, because I know it would pain you.

however, it be something for he to have satisfy his ambition, and

you see he have do that.”



“while I have let mine go----”



“just to settle down into a mere quiet wife and mother. be that what

you be think? do you regret it, Annie?”



she turn her soft, dark eye, bright in the glow of the firelight,

toward he, with her head raise proudly.



“no, no: I have never regret it--I never shall. my ambition be very

strong, but I do not throw it away; I keep it and clung to it until it

be swallow up in something strong still; and I think you can guess

what that be.”



talk and laughter be go on brightly round they among the member

of the reunited family gather round the glow fire. Harry do

not answer his wife in word; but the firm pressure of his hand as it

clasp hers unseen by the rest tell she that he understand that the

passion which have absorb all other in the brilliant actress and the

true-hearted woman be her love for he.





[the END.]
